NOTES.
William Watson is now very much over-rated,
and now equally under wited. About the just-
e«t appreciation of his talent that _ has yet
appeared is made by “ Laurie Magnus” in Black-
Iboon’s for July. The kernel of it : Mr. Watson
• ! ‘ stands outside of his picture, and is ‘ heard,’ not
Torrrheard.’ ” (In other words, he is not true
'poet, but poetical analyst,deseriber and commenta-
ilor.) “He is not, nor has he ever claimed to be, a
■ poet of the higher rank. . . . He is a graceful
'tritic, a kindly satirist, and a cultured observer; a
itdau withal of deep personal emotion matched by
% stateliness of expres ion. There is ample room
'for Ids like ... but in the valley, not on the
anights of Parnassus.”
' Now that Nordau’s “Conventional Lies” has
been before the ] ub oin a cheap American edition
for seven year*, an every democrat has assimi-
lated its message, London publisher Heinemann
issues a copyright edition at 225. posted, and
warns Australian booksellers that anyone selling
the “ pirated version ” will be prosecuted. Con-
sidering all the circumstances, considering also
that Nordau poses as prophet and denounces as
degenerate Tolstoi, who refuses to protit by his
literary works, the warning seems a philosophic
blunder, an 1 is likely to be a commercial failure.
By-the-bye, a well-fought suit between English
publisher and Australian bookseller on this [mint
of copyright should be interesting. Will anyone
be butchered by the lawyers to make a literary
holiday ?
“ Hypatia. ” one of Chas. Kingsley’s most read-
able novels, appears in a charming pocket edition
(cloth, Is. lOd.) —the first of a pocket Kingsley
series.
PERIODICALS.
. Cosmos (Gd. N.S.W. ; Bd. beyond) completes
its first year with the August number, which
contains portraits of Miss Juliet Flegeltaub aud
Mr. Julian Salomons, Q.C.; some good, {jopular
contributions ; and two or three of literary quality
—notably A. Nugent Robertson’s version of the
“Death’s twin-brother, Sleep” idea :
Like children in a darkened room,
Fear-filled, that speak with bated breath,
With fancied fears we fill the gloom
That shrouds the nursery of Death.
A footstep comes, a distant tread
Echoes along the corridor.
And terror, on the heels of dread,
Goes echoing in the creaking floor.
Again the fearful faces swarm,
The fearful shapes stand out and grin,
With menace of an unknown harm ;
And then —there softly enters in—
So we mistake ! —the kindly face,
The tender arms to which we creep,
The light at last! —the soft embrace—
The nurse God sends to bid us—sleep 1
The last verse might have better connection ; there
is too much “fear” and “echo;” but the meta-
phor is very pretty.
The August Review of Reviews (lid.) has a
food article on Q. Supreme Court Bench, by C. B.
'letcher, chief Courier leader-writer. The omis-
sions are particularly well-written. Other leading
articles deal with English politics, and election-
results discount a good many of the conclusions.
The remaining contents are well up to the average.
The Idler (Is.), now under Jerome’s exclusive
editorship, is to be increased in size,

GORDON'S COLORS.
The color-sense has developed with civilisation.
Our incomplex forefathers knew not a tithe of the
K" ;e hue-distinctions familiar to us. Homer has
rdly half-a-dozen simple color-epithets, though
by dexterous combination he gains a larger
•took ; but modern poets have a whole gallery of
lints to adorn their landscapes.
Gordon drew very largely upon the vocabulary
of color. In addition to words depicting general
impressions of light or darkness —such as bright,
brilliant, dull, dim, pale, wan—he uses these-
iiiuple terms : Alabaster, azure, amber, bay, black,
blue, brindled, brown, cerise, coral, chestnut,
crimson, dappled, emerald, golden, green, grey,
ivory, lilac, orange, pink, purple, raven, red, roan,
rose, sable, scarlet, silver, snowy, sorrel, tan, tar-
tan, white, violet, yellow. Gordon also exten-
sively uses compounds like blood-red, blood-shot,
brick-colored, brown-burnt, coal-black, dapple-
grey, dark-brown, dark-plumed, emerald-green,
fire-hushed, golden-headed, goldeu-hued, golden-
tinted, golden-tufted, iron-grey, many-hued,
olive-tinted, parti-colored, pearl-tinted, purple-
re hied, rose-hued, rose-like, rose-red, rose-tinted,
sapphire-hued, silver-grey, silver-white, slate-
solored, steel-blue, violet-veined.
Such are the pigments with which the modern
poet paints Ins pictures; and such advantages has
he over his ancient predecessor, whose most
brilliant effects were produced from little more
than white, black, blue, red, green, and yellow,
Gordon’s color-sense was keen ; even he has not
fully utilised English color-resources. Burely he
would have found useful: milky and milk-wnite,
lily and lily-white, argent, ebony, drab, russet,
and chocolate, damask, cherry, and ruby (to
match his numerous lips), vermilion, lavender,
turquoise.
Drum the gloomy tone of much of Gordon’s
verse one might expect gloomy epithets to pre-
dominate ; but the reverse is the case. Kud
impressed him most strongly, and is frequently
opposed to white. Blue sky is often set against
green turf; but yellows and browns are not
favored. The poet did not love neutral colors : he
preierred violent contrasts —high lights and deep
shades. Curious admirers may like to know that
red is used 48 times in Ins poems (copyright
edi Jon), scarlet, 13, and crimson, 14 ; white, t8 ;
green, 30 ; black, 211; blue, 24 ; grey, 23 ; golden,
23, and yellow, 0; purple, 10, and violet, 4. No
ather term occurs more than four times.
Note, finally, that Gordon had a distinct pre-
dilection for blondes. It is true he is once on
excellent terms with a lady who has “a dark
aye, ” though he says nothing of her hair or com-
plexion. But “ the lady Mabel a lady mur-
deress—is dark, with raven hair; and Delilah is
mother brunette, with magnificent black tresses ;
vic e Briseis, and Guinevere, and Gwendoline —
all praised—are represented as goideu-haired
olondes.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
' Mount Desolation,” by Carleton Dawe (3s.), is
mother “typical Australian romance,” with a
little bit of wild Australian girl, a bit of tame
melodrama, a scrap of the Kelly gang, a touch of
! ‘ Robbery Under Arms,” a fragment of “The
Last of the Mohicans,” and a few other things,
sandwiched into what should certainly be a popu-
lar story —especially in far-away Britain, where
hundreds of quiet households shudder at the dady
dangers with which bushrangers and blacks sur-
round the brave Australian pioneers.
The third edition of “ A Dili’s Hide in Iceland,”
Dy Mrs. Alec. Tweedie (Is. 4d.) is excellent value,
wid is brightened by 21 good illustrations. Sub-
ject, rather than style, makes the book interesting;
(or the author’s pen is stilf and guide-booky, and
she has no sense of humor. JPity Bara Jeannette
Duncan’s “Social Departure,” the best book of
'.ravel ever written by a woman (and, indeed, one
which few men have rivalled for interest), cannot
be obtained in similar cheap form from an
.English publisher. In America you get it for 2 6
oeuts; but the only English edition is at 8s.
po ted, and even that is now growing scarce.
“The Confessions of Amos Todd, Adventurer”
[Is. 4d.) is a rambling string of anecdotes and
experiences, very readable.
“ By Adverse Winds,” by Oliphant Smeaton
(4s. 3d.) is fluently written, and shifts the conven-
tional puppets through the conventional situations
without dropping them into any holes a machine-
god can’t pull them out of. it also airs the
author's good Scotch, and the tags from his com-
monplace-book, and the knowledge of local papers
ind politics which he gained during a short
Australian press career; and tliere is possibly a
julficient amount of interest in these things to jus-
tify their publication. Mr. Smeaton’s work, in
abort, is neither better nor worse tlian that of a
acore of novels which drop every day from the
press, predestinated some to short popularity and
some to utter oblivion. It is a pity more can’t
honestly be said in its favor; fur Mr. Smeaton
(now editing the Liberal, a small weekly Edin-
burgh newspaper not yet a year old) has tlie good
taste to make his literary hero contribute to “ the
Sydney Bulletin, an amazingly able journal,”
im<1 his back certainly deserves scratching in re-
turn.
J. N. Pentelow’s “Story of the Test Matches,
England v. Australia ” (Is. 3d.) with details,
scores, analysis, and comments from ’77 right up
to date, is invaluable to cricketers.
E. W. Cole issues a*fresh edition of “The New
Party (Is. Gd.) noticed some time ago as a colleo
•ion of highly-coromendable yearnings without
much practical value. The same publisher’s
“Government by the People” (Is. 4d.), by the
authors of “ The Story of My Dictatorship,” adds
another manufactured-Utopia to the long list. As
its constitutional speculations are not sugared with
a story, the popular supremacy of “ Looking
Backward” and “News from Nowhere” is un-
likely to be overthrown.

We wish Ur to he understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely ino usive variety ol the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the oonsequeut blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when wo
assert not in timorous tones, but with ttie trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves puBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little baud
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
wlio in self-abnegation and oontentediiess, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but it the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then snrelv the laborer is worthy of his lure,
and the doer of good actions entitled to sonv recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
nevercease from their toil ; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun th y work, oppress'd by the cruel frost of
snmcwhiie baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
It has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will d ' tne
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.

'The Bulletin’s' Scrap-Book.
“HULLO”
W’en you see a man in woe,
Walk right up and say “Hullo!’’
Soy “ Hullo ! ” an’ “ How d’ye do 1 ’
“ How’s the world a-usin’ you ! ”
Slap the fellow on his back,
Bring yer him’ down with a whack,
Waltz right up, an’ don’t go slow,
Grin an' shake an’ say, “ Hullo ! ”
Is he clothed in rags ? O sho !
Walk right up an’ say, “ Hullo I ”
Rags is but a cotton roll,
Jest for wrappin’ up a soul,
An’ a soul is worth a true
Hale an’ hearty “ How d’ye do ! ’
Don’t wait for the crowd to go,
Walk right up an’ say, “ Hullo I ”
W’en big vessels meet, they say.
They saloot an’ sail away.
Jest the same are you an’ me,
Lonesome ships upon a sea ;
Each one sailing his own jog
For a port beyond the fog.
Let yer speakin’-trumpet blow,
Lift yer horn an’ cry, “Hullo !”
Say “ Hullo ! ” an’ “ How d’ye do ! ”
Other folks are good as you.
W’en ye leave yer house of clay,
Wanderin’ in the Far-Away,
W’en you travel through the strange
Country t’other side the range,
Then the souls you’ve cheered will know
Who you be, an’ say, “ Hullo ! ”
KEEP-A-GOIN’.
If yon strike a thorn or rose,
Keep a-goin’!
If it hails or if it snows,
Keep a-goin’!
’Tain’t no use to sit and whine
When the fish ain’t on your line ;
Bait your hook and keep on tryin’—
Keep a-goin’ 1
When the weather kills your crop,
Keep a-goin’ 1
When you tumble from up top,
Keep a-goin’ !
S’pose you're out of every dime,
Gettin’ broke ain’t any crime,
Tell the world you’re feelin’ prime—
Keep a-goin’ !
When it looks like all is up,
Keep a-goin’!
Drain the sweetness from the cup,
Keep a-goin’ !
See the wild birds on the wing,
Hear the bells that sweetly ring;
When you feel like sighin’—sing !
Keep a-goin’ !
The other day a Collingwood policeman,
afflicted with the custody of a limp female in a
deplorable condition ot rum and mud, and being
unable to procure a cab, pressed into bis service
“in the Queen’s name” a man who was passing
with a hand truck. The placid “drunk” was
loaded on to the truck, the policeman mounted
guard beside her, and the patriot in the shafts
towed both constable and prisoner to the lockup,
followed by an admiring crowd awed by the
majesty of the law perched on a “bottle-0 cart.”
Tim Law might have tugged the cart whilst the
bottle-0 sat a-top and steadied the load ; but in
that case it would have forfeited The BULLETIN S
respect for ever. Emphatically the Law is not an
ass.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in C'>ll ins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 1),
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.*
A -big Melb. paper, in a recent leader’
weeps crocodile tears over the exodus to London
of all “ Australia’s brightest spirits in literature
and art.” With characteristic hypocrisy it shifts
on to their shoulders the onus of deserting their
native land. This comes well from a journal
which encourages Deadwood Dick literature per
medium of its own columns, and which, only the
same week as the above appeared, had vigorously
protested against the Bill introduced in Vic.
Assembly to “ protect ” local authors, and enable
them to earn their bread in Australia, by putting
a duty on foreign-printed penny trash.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply ns with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W, D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
<£ CASTLES 77 akd “ CAPSTANT.”
We have declined to conform with tin’s request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co.. Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ TKree Castles” and “ Capstan ” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HETDE, TODMAN & CO. [Agents for W. D. and 11. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

“Yankee Boodleblended tvith Polities .
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE ”is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
beefi accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-centnry to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built np on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIN SON & SONS, who use more American h-af
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands:—“CON-
QUEROR,” “ SIR ROGER,” “ CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE”
ranks supreme.

. The COLONIAL TREASURER, ip answer to
Mr. Schey, said that a Judge of the Suoretne Court
was, in terms of the Constitution Act, removable
only by the Queen upon Address by both Houses.
He proposed to obtain the opinion of the Law
Officers as to the form of a motion in the name of
Mr. Price. It seemed, to say the least, very doubtful
whether a Judge of the Supreme Court could be
removed upon such a motion, which did not propose
an Address to the Queen in terms of the Constitu-
tion Act.

have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 14, 1895.
The Curse of Foreign Capital.
The great disaster which, a few days ago,
overtook the luckless Bank of New Zealand,
and compelled it to reconstruct for the fourth
time, and apply, for the second time, to the
State for assistance, is another of the far-
reaching results of the great “influx of
foreign capital which came upon Maoriland
in the years 1870 to 1885. In this respect it
ranks with the Australian financial collapse
of '93, which was the natural outcome of the
borrowing boom that lasted for 20 years or
so and practically ended in 1891. The cause
commonly assigned for these troubles is that
falling to pieces of things in general usually
described as “ the depression,” As a matter
of fact, however, no one really knows for
certain whether there is any depression in
these provinces, or not. A depression, in
the logical acceptation of the term, is a
decline below the normal standard, and what
the normal standard of things in Australasia
may be—assuming that there is such a thing
at all anywhere—no one can tell. The rush of
immigration in earlier years, the vast revenue
derived from the sale of public lands, the
successive gold-booms, silver-booms, borrow-
ing-booms, and land-booms, the employment
artificially created by the building in red-hot
haste of railways and other public works,
kept Australasia for 40 years in a state of
financial dazzle. And now that the bottom
has fallen out of all these booms, the country
has arrived in a state which may be its natural
condition or which may not—there are no
reliable data to go upon, and no one really
knows whether the present aspect of things
is a depression or not.
* * •
Of ail this country’s many booms, the
great “influx of foreign capital” which
started in earnest about 1870 was the most
disastrous. It is a well-ascertained fact that
any great and sudden increase in a country’s
supply of money tends to send up prices and
wages, and create, for the time being, an
artificial state of prosperity. It matters
nothing how the money is obtained. It may
be borrowed, or stolen, or acquired by
wringing an indemnity out of a conquered
enemy, or dug out of the ground, or estab-
lished by the mere force of popular delusion.
It may be gold, silver, State paper, bank
paper, the promissory notes of insolvent
speculators, or anything else which the com-
munity agrees to recognise as money. If
bi-metallism comes into vogue, and all the
civilised nations agree to regard silver as
legal tender to an unlimited extent at, say,
ss. per ounce, the sudden and vast increase in
the stock of money will send up the nominal
prices of everything, labor included, with a
rush, so that the average man will get,
perhaps, 25 per cent, more wages, and
pay 25 per cent, more for commodities,
and be really very much as he was be-
fore. What will happen thereafter no one
kirows, for no such universal and instan-
taneous change in the order of things has
ever before occurred within the knowledge of
man, but as every boom in the world’s
history, so far as we know it, has been
succeeded by a collapse, it is almost certain
that, in some way or other, the bi-metallic
boom would burst like the rest. How the
burst would arrive cannot be exactly fore-
seen, but its arrival appears tolerably cer-
tain. In the same way, a large addition to
the currency in the shape of a forced State
paper issue produces a temporary outbreak
of artificial prosperity. So does a flood of
mining or bogus-bank scrip—so long as the
public agrees to consider it as a thing of
value. Any sudden and extensive influx of
money, or alleged money, or of anything
down to glass beads and fish-hooks, that the
public can be persuaded to regard as such
for the time being, will answer the same'
purpose. Even tulips made a great and
wonderful boom in the days of the Dutch
tulip mania, and mud, under equally favor-
able conditions, would have done likewise.
* # *
In the 21 years between 1871 and 1892,
Australasia borrowed, roughly speaking,
£246,000,000, publicly and privately. Of
this amount, Maoriland alone raised
£44,000,000, most of which was borrowed
in 10 garish years, from 1875 to 1885.
While this influx continued wages, rents,
and land values went up on a scale of
dazzling magnificence. It was at this period
that the downfall of the great Bank of New
Zealand, and many other financial institu-
tions, began, and the cause of it was not so
much recklessness or mismanagement as
the policy of the Government and the
people, and the curse of foreign capital
in general. The banks at that period
had to do business on the basis of
values as they were, or else cease doing
business altogether, and close up for good.
Even if the management foresaw the col-
lapse that must come when the boom fell
through—which is wholly improbable, for no
one ever does foresee such things—and how
the bottom would fall out of the securities,
and the shareholders’ capital all slide down
into the bottomless gulf of depreciation, it
would probably have made no difference,
L'iie alternative lay between trading on
values as they were and risking the conse-
quences, or else letting practically the whole
business go, and winding up the bank in the
zenith of its prosperity. And in these days
of large profits and 15 per cent, dividends
the shareholders, who now curse the manage-
ment for its misdeeds, would have raised one
unanimous shriek of derision had such an
alternative been suggested, and the com-
munity in general would have done likewise.
Maoriland’s big financial institution, and a
good many smaller ones, came to grief because
the nation, by supporting the Vogel policy,
created certain artificial conditions ; because
the directorates apparently did not foresee,
any more than the nation in general, that
these conditions couldn’t last ; and, further,
because it would have made very little differ-
ence if they had foreseen, as no one would
have believed them. They might, no doubt,
have done something to mitigate the disaster,
but it was hardly possible to do much.
* • •
The financial troubles of Australia were
also, to a great extent, of the people’s own
making, and tiie influx of foreign capital
was the means by which they were brought
about. The political system under which
the country is misgoverned no doubt
makes it difficult for the nation to express a
definite opinion at the polls on any one
subject, yet the fact that it consistently sup-
ported, for twenty years or so, the politi-
cians who floated big loans and believed in
promoting the influx of foreign capital, places
a fair share of the responsibility on its
shoulders. The influx of foreign capital
gave a fictitious value to land, to bricks and
stone and mortar and galvanised iron, to
many useless varieties of labor, to the scrip
of all manner of companies, and to other
things without number. It even lent a
certain value to James Munro’s p.n., and to
the IO Uof Matthew Davies, and to
Fink’s bill at three months, by creating an
artificial and wholly useless industry—that
of land-boom finance—in which Muneo and
Davies and the lamented Fink had their
uses, if anybody can be said to have a use
who is employed in an occupation that is in
itself wholly useless. When the influx of
borrowed money practically ceased, all these
things became as if they had never been.
Their special variety of finance became ex-
tinct, therefore James Munro and his species
were valueless, and their “securities” were
like themselves. Bricks and stone and
mortar and galvanised iron dropped with
a sickening thud ; so did land, and scrip,
and every other commodity on which
money could be lent. There was much
lying, fraud, misrepresentation and un-
disguised robbery about the financial in-
stitutions which came to grief in that famous
collapse, yet the worst and heaviest part of
the loss was owing to the influx of foreign
capital, for even where it was not directly
responsible it made the opportunity for the
fraud and robbery, and the other sins of the
boom. Even the most honest and cautiously-
managed institutions had to trade, in a great
measure, on the basis of things as they were,
and lost enormously. Some of them, which
suspected the disaster afar off, endeavored
to secure themselves by limiting their
advances to one-half the value of the security,
but even this wide margin often failed to
cover the difference between things as they
had been in the boom-time, and things as
they are to-day. No one really knew in
those days what was the normal value of
things, for nothing had ever had a chance to
settle down to its normal value. No one
knew how much of the alleged value of
things was the fictitious result of the influx
of foreign capital, and how much was real,
and no one cared to inquire.
# * Vs
There is no need to morally whitewash
the typical financiers of the boom j'ears ;
taking them all round they were a despic-
able lot. But what the Australian nation
apparently fails to understand even now is
that the worst of its losses were owing to
its childlike faith in the advantages of—
as it is euphoniously termed—“ bringing
money into the country.” What it has
to learn is that, whether the borrowed
capital is spent wisely or unwisely, whether
it is fooled away in wars, or in building
railways to nowhere, or in fortifications, or in
repairing old bridges and dredges, or in shift-
ing sand-heaps in Sydney Centennial Park,
the result is very much the same in the end.
The influx of large sums of money means a
boom and a general inflation ; the stoppage
of the influx —and it must stop sooner or
later—means collapse, want of work, a
sudden disappearance of the public, savings
in broken banks and shattered insurance
companies and building societies, bank-
ruptcy, misery, hunger, and general desola-
tion. Few more enduring curses ever fell
on a civilised community than the Influx of
Foreign Capital.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Information Wanted.
N.S. W. Premier and Treasurer Reid’s second
batch of estimates for the year ending 30th
June, 1896, are curious reading, and further
confirm the impression that G. Reid is the
most complete financial dolt who ever ran the
N.S.W. Treasury. The Bulletin does not
profess to have gone fully through the list,
which is a vast and elaborate document, but a
study of the first two dozen pages or so gives a
good idea of the principles on which it is con-
structed. The interest on the public debt is
put down at ,£23,000 less than last year,
whereas it will probably be a trifle more, as the
conversion operations will involve paying
interest on a good part of the new loan for
several months before the old loans actually
expire. The regular annual payment of
.£70,000 towards extinguishing the 1867 rail-
way loan is cut down to ,£15,000, so that
.£55,000 is left unprovided for. Of the annual
payment for interest on Treasury bills, £28,000
is also left unprovided for ; likewise £75,000 of
the annual payment towards redemption of
these bills ; £37,500 of the yearly liability
under another railway loan ; and £13,000 of
the liability under the Naval Defence Act. As
all these payments are fixed by Act of
Parliament, and can’t be avoided except
by the province going bankrupt, they are pre-
sumably passed on to next year, on the invari-
able principle of leaving things to posterity.
Some equally remarkable devices turn up a few
pages further on. On page 14 appears an
item : “ Shorthand writers, one at £475, two at
£438, £l333,”andas thisitem figures up to £1351
the inference is that Treasurer Reid can’t do
ordinary compound addition. On page27appears
another remarkable entry : “ Rations, Medical
Comforts, &c., for 1100 inmates (of Parramatta
Asylum) at ss. per week, £13,100.” As this
liability figures up to £14,300 it would again
appear that G. H. Reid can’t do ordinary
arithmetic. On the same page, rations, &c. ,
for 375 destitute citizens at ss. per week, are
set down at £4450 instead of £4875. Again,
on the same page, rations, &c., for 675 paupers
at ss. per week are made to produce a total of
£BOOO instead of £B7lO. On page 28, rations,
&c., for 965 paupers at ss. per week are set
down as £11,500 instead of £12,545, and a
similar item for 550 paupers at the same rate is
made to yield a total of £6650 instead of
£7150. As the blunder is always in the
direction of reducing the total, all these
things must help materially to make up
Treasurer Reid’s beautiful surplus that is to be,
but at the same time they constitute a ghastly
reflection on his inability to add two and two
together and avoid making them three. Also, it
is difficult to understand how, seeing that New
South Wales is pledged to pay £5OOO a year
towards the government of New Guinea, only
£2500 is provided for. Altogether Mr. Reid’s
estimates simply bristle with items which look,
at all events, like so many stupid attempts to
show a surplus that has no real existence, and
the necessity for a Parliamentary Committee of
Accounts to investigate that gentleman’s arith-
metic sticks out conspicuously.
A Melbourne Syndicate Grab.
In a letter to Melbourne Ale, Poet Fitz Gibbon
adduces a number of excellent reasons why
Melbourne should sit hard down on the electric-
lighting job now making its dishonest way
through Parliament. iSo far, the sonpiolent
city has hardly even realised the existence of
this stupendous grab, and there seems to be
serious danger that it will be perpetrated
before the population is sufficiently awake to
kick. Then it will kick uselessly after the
event, as it did when the great gas monopoly
swindle was fixed upon its shoulders many
years ago. As Fitz Gibbon points out, it was
originally intended to make gas-lighting in
Melbourne a municipal undertaking. But the
Corporation was poor and unenterprising in
those days, and it decided to delegate its
powers to a private co. for 23 years, at the end
of which time the business was to revert to the
city, the gasworks being handed over at cost
price, or thereabouts. The co.’s works were
erected on Crown lands, the lease of which
expired on the same day as the monopoly
terminated, and the Government pledged itseff
then to hand over the land to the Corporation
as a free gift. Most people thought that these
arrangements would effectually prevent the co.
becoming a permanent monopoly. At the end
of its 28-years’ term the municipality would
exercise its right to take over the works,
and the Government would resume posses-
sion of the land on which they stood,
and being thus ejected twice over, it seemed as
if the Gas Co. could have no permanent footing
whatever. But that wily corporation climbed
over all these barriers. It persuaded a certain
Minister for Lands to break his promise of
handing over the gasworks site to the munici-
pality, and put the land up to auction. Also,
he valued the co.’s improvements—otherwise
its gasometers, &c.—at the enormous sum of
£240,000, so that any outside purchaser would
ha\ e to pay that amount to the. co. before he
could take possession. As, of course, no one
was in a position to buy on these terms, the
Gas Co. got the land at its own price. Later
on it ran a Bill through another patriotic Par-
liament practically abolishing the municipal
right of purchase altogether, and thus rendered
its position impregnable. The electric-lighting
grab is being started on practically tbe same
lines. The Corporation of Melbourne has
illuminated the streets with electricity, and js
prepared to supply the electric-light to private
consumers, but sundry speculators, who want
to seize upon this business for their own profit,
have succeeded in obtaining a legal decision
that it has no powers to supply erectricity to
private consumers. The municipal authorities,
therefore, appeal to Parliament for the neces-
sary authority, and the syndicate, which has
its own little Bill before the House,
opposes the application. And, as the
average syndicate has many dark and mys-
terious ways of “influencing” members,
it is impossible to say what the result will be
unless the people give notice to their represen-
tatives that every M.L.A., from the Premier
downwards, who doesn’t oppose the private
enterprise scheme to the utmost is a lost mem-
ber from that day forth. Meanwhile the syndi-
cate offers liberal terms, and is about the kindest
and most generous syndicate that ever fished
for a big concession in the city by the Yarra.
All it wants is to own the electric-lighting
monopoly for a term of years, and at the end
of its lease it is willing to hand over its busi-
ness to the municipality on the easiest possible
terms. And, as the story of the gas swindle is
very old now, hardly anyone remembers that
exactly the same thing was promised in it}s
case also, and that it always is promised, and
that the promise is never kept if the syndicate
can help it.
The Finances of Federation.
The other day Mr. R. J. Black read a papei
on the above subject before that learned bul
rather dreary institution, the Australian Eco-
nomic Association. Several other weighty per-
sons presented a series of criticisms in reply,
and Mr. Black replied to them in turn, and
the other people will probably respond at a later
date. The burden of the lament on both sides
is that it is necessary to construct some elabor-
ate scheme by which these provinces can feder-
ate on an equal cash basis. Some of'the pro-
vinces are richer than others, or have a more
profitable railway system, or pay less interest
on their debt, and Mr. Black and his critics
propose various methods to prevent them losing
an odd halfpenny somewhere or other bv a
union with their poorer neighbors. South
Australia, it is pointed out, realises per cent,
interest on its railway expenditure, while
the other states of Australasia follow in a
steadily - descending scale, until at the
extreme end appears wretched little Tas-
mania, which earns hardly more than
enough to pay working expenses. Queensland
realises £2 10s. 3d. per head of Customs
revenue, while Victoria collects only £1 9 S . 3d.,
and, if the Federal Government takes over
the Customs, the contributions under this
heading, it is feared, will be very unequal.
Victoria built part of her railway out of re-
venue, therefore she has more assets and less
debt to hand over to the Commonwealth than
her next neighbour, New South Wales, Maori-
land has a war-debt of several millions, which is'
represented by no tangible assets, unless the
seedy laurels of the old fighting days can be
called an asset, and it is explained that it would
be manifestly unfair for the other provinces to
federate with her on equal terms, and take over
a share of this burden. The Economic Asso-
ciation people wrestled manfully with these and
many other like discrepancies, and constructed
various plans by which, when Federation
comes, the richer states of Australasia are to
take the poorer ones by the hair, and make
them pay up the last copper of their deficit
before they are admitted to the sacred bonds of
bi other hood. And, strangely enough, no one
seems to have got up to make the suggestion
that the seven provinces, or as many of them as
feel inclined, should federate on the simple
basis of taking things as they are, and
shouldn t worry about small discrepancies
in the assets. When Australia gets a uniform
tariff, the differences in the Customs revenue
will mostly vanish. The railway returns will
probably equalise themselves by-and-bye when
the lines are all under one Federal manage-
ment; or, il they don’t, the province which
shows a bad return to-day may possibly show
the best one later on when its boom time
arrives. Even if it doesn’t, however, it matters
very little. The fact that one province has got


its debt at a lower rate than another is of no
importance, for the Federal Government will be
able to convert all the debts into a uniform
liability at a uniform rate of interest.
And even Maoriland’s war-debt should be
accepted in a generous spirit for the sake of
unity, and on the fraternal, if somewhat illogi-
cal, ground that the other provinces should be
very glad that they were spared the same ill-
fortune in their earlier days. In short, The
Bulletin respectfully submits that Federation
will never be arrived at by the policy of going
down on all-fours and grovelling after odd cop-
pers, and that the one short and effective way is
to take things as they are. If| it is possible to
make an Australian nation, it is worth while to
sacrifice the half-penny at the end of the
balance-sheet in doing it.
Tire Melancholy Maori.
On Sept. 27, 1839, sixteen Maori chiefs of
Wanga Nui Ateva (Port Nicholson, M.L.) sold
all their right, title and interest in some 45,000
acres of land surrounding Wellington Harbor,
and' including the two islands therein. The
purchaser was one William Wakefield, agent
for the New Zealand Land Co., of London ; and
this was the consideration :
100 red blankets, 120 muskets, 48 iron pols, 2 tierces
tobacco, 2 cases soap, 15 fowlin'-- pieces, 21 ketra gun-
powder, 1 cask ball cartridge, 1 keg lead slabs, 100 car-
touche boxes, 100 tomahawks, 40 wipe tomahawks, 1 case
pipes, 2 dozen spades, 50 steel axes, 1200 fish-hooks, 12
bullet moulds, 12 dozen shirts, 20 jackets, 20 pairs
trousers, 60 red night-caps, 300 yards col ton duck, 200
y irds calico, 100 yards check, 20 dozen pocket-handker-
chiefs, 2 dozen slates, 200 pencils, 10 dozen looKinir-iflasses,
10 dozen pocket-knives, 10 dozen scissors, 1 dozen pair
shoes, 1 dozen umbrellas, 1 dozen bats, 21b. beads, 100
yards’ ribbon, 1 gross Jews-harps, 1 dozen razors, 10 d; zen
dressing combs, 6 dozen hoes, 2 suits superfine clothes, 1
dozen shaving boxes and brushes, 20 muskets, 2 dozen
adzes, 1 dozen sticks sealing-wax; which we, the aforesaid
chiefs, do hereby acknowledge to have been receivid by
us from the aforesaid William Wakefield.
The N.Z. Land Co., in addition to handing over
the red night-caps, the shaving brushes, the
Jews-harps and things, as purchase-money for
the 45,000 acres of land upon which Wellington
city now stands, agreed to set apart and hold
in trust for the descendants of the Port
Nicholson tribe of Maoris, a portion of land
equal to one-tenth of the whole block. One
hundred and ten town lots and 3500 acres of
rural lands were thus reserved. The living
descendants of the tribe—many of them edu-
cated half-castes —have for years been trying to
lay hands on the interest of these “tenths;”
but, so far, Government, which years ago
bought out the eo. at a big advance
ou red-blanket and tomahawk prices, does
not respond. Returns petitioned for show
that many of the “ tenths ” in the
town have been jumped “ for municipal
and other purposes,” and the rural lauds have
somehow shrunk from 3500 acres to some 900
acres, in a manner that nothing short of day-
light robbery or an earthquake could account
for. The less valuable remainder is held by
the Public Trustee, and, presumably, the accu-
mulated interest would barely suffice to pur- ,
chase another gross of Jews-harps to replace -
those the musicians of the tribe pinched their
tongues on 56 years ago. The perennial petition
is again referred to the Native Affairs' Com-
mittee of the House of Representatives, and by
the time a definite award is made the oldest
inhabitant will probably not remember any-
thing about the original transaction, and the
claimants will have established their right in
the local cemetery to at least a portion of
their “ tenths.” And this is the story of one
step in “ the inarch of civilisation.”
The March of Protection.
On Ist August the Cape of Good Hope put a
protective duty of 2d. per lb. on frozen meat.
This is, of course, an argument against Protec-
tion which brings joy to every right-thinking
person. “ What will Protectionists say?” cries
that powerful logician, the Argus :
It may possibly cause Protectionists here to ponder as
to what would be the condition of Victoria if all other
countries did unto us as they are done by.
The wooden-headed implication whereof is that
the Cape taxes frozen meat because Victoria is
Protectionist; also that it is the essential aim
of Protection to encourage imports ; also that a
country turns Freetrade out of its love for other
countries. Cobden’s fatuous prophecy that all
the world would follow England’s lead to open
ports finds constant echo to-day in the throats
of his short-winded apostles, but they must
needs bolster up his vain aspirations with large
lies. If the U.S.A. declare for Freetrade,
uo matter how temporarily, that is an argu-
ment for Freetrade in Australia. If the
Cape of Good Hope turns Protectionist, that
also is an argument for Freetrade in Australia.
The fact that one market has been closed
against a staple Australian export seems not to
be an argument for suspecting foreign cus-
tomers. No, we are to gamble on the supposi-
tion that all other markets will be kept open to
us for ever. The Freetrader depends on a
foreign market which may be closed to him at
any time. The Protectionist aims at an in-
dustrial autonomy that will be independent of
foreign favors. Great Britain is now Aus-
tralia’s prize customer, and the Argus, on
another occasion when off its guard, indicates,
with singular candor, the position of one
staple :
The English people are not so foolish as to go on grow-
ing an article which new countries can produce much
more cheaply, and they are turning their attention to
things which pay better. The Russian moujik, the
Indian ryot, the American prairie farmer, the Argentine,
and the Australian settler all compete for the honor of
producing, at abjectly low prices, the breadstuffs con-
sumed in the United Kingdom.
“Abjectly low prices” reward the Australian
farmer who demeans himself by competing
against yellow men and halfbreeds. For even
this abject reward a price may presently be
asked which Australia will be indisposed
to accept Salisbury, Great Britain’s
Premier, is a Protectionist. Salisbury’s
colleague, Chamberlain, is in favor of an
Imperial Zollvereiu. I, is even whispered that
he has cast off altogether the fiscal faith of his
youth. The England which Gladstone mourn-
fully describes as standing lonely in her adher-
ence to Freetrade, is being daily hounded by
the landed and agricultural interest back to the
chiefest of heresi orthodox economics—the
very Corn Laws themselves. The British agri-
culturist thinks he has too long played the
part of willing victim on the sacrificial altars
of Freetrade —he is already done on one side,
and will do violence to the god before he is
cooked altogether. Year by year the manu-
facturing interests watch the increasing flow
into England of protected manufactures. Man-
chester, which first had the dream of a bag-
man’s millennium, is now a hotbed of Protec-
tion. When the hour of England’s reversion
to her old faith strikes, what wall be the offer
made to Australian agriculturists and manu-
facturers? Is Australia prepared to enter into
a Customs union dictated by the predominant
partner—to hand over her established in-
dustries as a sacrifice to God Save the Queen
and Rule Britannia ?
Twelve Months’ Hard.
Ed. Bulletin,—The other day, in Victorian
Assembly, Murray, M.L.A., elicited cries of
“ Shame !” from the “ Respectable ” members
by stating that after attending Melb. City
Police Court for some days he had come to the
conclusion that “ the police would stick at
nothing, and that magistrates were in col-
lusion with the police,” also that the P.M. had
declined to hear any defence from a specific
prisoner and had sent him to gaol for a year.
Nothing was done—nothing ever is done in
such cases—beyond an “explanation” from
the police in the Herald, followed by an
editorial note to the effect that it was Murray’s
inexperience which caused his gullibility.
Now, the present writer has been in Melb.
police-courts nearly every day for the past
year ; he has heard police lie, witnesses lie, and
magistrates lie; and, with the Herald, he
ascribes any incorrectness or meagreness of fact
on Murray’s part to his inexperience. Had
the M.L.A. in question attended the courts
oftener, his remarks would probably not have
been worded so courteously, nor would his
specific charge of unfairness have been backed up
by one debateable case. It is in the lower courts,
to a far greater degree than the upper, that a
State Defender is urgently required. Certainly,
a man cannot be tried for any serious offence
before a J.P. or a P.M., of whom the latter
sometimes are the more unfair; but his
whole life can be mortgaged by a succession
of sentences of “one year’s imprisonment” for
vagrancy. The Judges of the general or
criminal sessions invariably see that the prisoner
obtains fair play as regards evidence. They
may, as in N.S. W., compel sittings to remain
over such protracted periods as to jeopardise
the proper administration of justice ; they may
pronounce sentences wholly inadequate to the
crime ; they may browbeat and threaten juries
into finding verdicts furthest from their inten-
tions. But, withal, any deliberate attempt on
the part of the prosecution to adduce evidence
which is not evidence, to introduce inadmissible
matter, or to prompt witnesses, would meet
with instant reproof. In the police-courts it is
different. The prosecuting sub-inspectors are
not only not checked, but positively encouraged
by the Bench ; and the luckless wretch w ho
finds himself charged with vagrancy at some
Courts for the second, time may as well make
up his mind at once for a stretch of a year.
When any big case, such as the Abrahams,
brings the barristers down from the Supreme
Court, they, while waiting in court for
their own case to come on, stand aghast at
the procedure by wddch a man’s liberty is taken
away for six months or a year. “ John Jones,
yer charged with vagrancy; ’ow d’yer plead ?”
askes the orderly. “ Not guilty.” Then
policeman No. 1 enters the box. “I’rested the
pris’ner last night, y’r VVush’p. ’E was standin’
’n Bourke-street. ’E’s always knocking about
with the lowest thieves and prostitutes in the
city, and never does any work.” “ How' long
have yer known me ? ” asks the prisoner.
“ Well, I’ve seen yer off and on for the last six
months; yer always loafing about Bourke-
street.” “ But I’ve been away in Gippsland
since Christmas until a week ago.” Six or
seven more police, perhaps a detective, follow-
all speaking in the same strain, and when they
are done the accused says, “If you’ll give me a
day, y’r Worships, I’ll prove I’m workin’ at
Collin’wood since I came back.” “ Previous
convictions,” calls the orderly; “once assault,
once vagrancy,” “Twelvemonths,” says the
Bench ; and John Jones is hurried off to the
cells. If he is unknown, he may be granted a
day’s remand on his own application, but if a
conviction is recorded against him—well,
that seems to “settle” him. Again, if
the police want a remand they can get
it. Only a couple of weeks ago a man
and two women charged with larceny w'ere
discharged after being remanded over five
weeks while the police were making ineffectual
search for the prosecutor. And the main point
of all these vagrancy prosecutions is that
many convictions are obtained on evidence
wholly inadmissible, and which any Judge
would rule out in a minute. Whenever a vag.
has money enough to fee a lawyer, which only
happens in the case of a prominent criminal, he
is usually discharged—for the simple reason
that the evidence must be limited to the day
set forth on the information laid. P.C. O’Hoo-
ligan’s, Dunbhy’s, and M'Ginty’s allegations
that “ they ahlways seen him loafin’ about,”
are met by the question, “Did you see him
loafing about on 16th June?” And as the
arresting constable is seldom able to swear that
the prisoner was not working half-an-hour
before the arrest was effected, the case falls to
the ground. A public defender would restrict
such evidence, and have an eye to the fact that
constables ordered out of court by the prisoner
remain out and keep a cool, clear iiead for
cross-examination purposes, which a man with
his liberty at stake is absolutely unable to do.
Nobody but a regular attendant at the Mel-
bourne courts can realise how lucky he is to be
out of gaol,—M‘G.

The Bitter Cry of Coolgardie.
Dear Bulletin, —Give us a lift towards
separation from the W.A vampires. We are
making a gallant fight against enormous diffi-
culties natural to this desert, and now we are
compelled to resist the artificial taxation thrust
on us in so doing by our oppressors in Perth.
They can’t supply us with food or clothing or
machinery, nor with anything we want to aid
us in our labors or to enable us to live decently.
And I believe they begrudge us food and
water. They want to monopolise everything.
They are grinding out of us a surplus revenue,
and still turn on the screw until we are
desperate and determined to separate our-
selves and land from their clutches. VV e
are unanimous, and demand separation,
and nothing less will now satisfy the
“ other aiders,” as they contemptuously
call us. We have submitted to be robbed
of our hardly-won money and are an orderly
community. But we have some spirit left. In
the main we are men from the East, all of
whom have brought money with us—more or
less. Eastern men and money originally opened
up this new territory, and now we are helped
by European money—English, French, Belgian,
and German. There are more Englishmen here
than W.A.’s, and we have unanimously com-
bined to establish a province of our own, in
the Government of which we shall have
every man of us a vote. We are daily
growing in numbers, and, as you recently
showed, outnumber our oppressors. We
ask for voting power and more representa-
tion in Parliament, and are refused. What
else can we do, then, but appeal to the Imperial
Government for separation ? Esperance Bay is
our nearest and best port. But we shall never
get a railway to it out of the W.A. Govern-
ment as it is constituted. Coolgardie is, as the
crow flies, 325 miles from Fremantle, and by
rail nearly 600 miles from Albany ; yet, per-
force, we must travel to the one or the other to
reach steamers trading with the East, whilst
the distance to Esperance Bay is only 200
miles ! We must go to the East for our sup-
plies of machinery and food, &c., and all of it
must go an extra 400 miles over W.A. railways
at high rates because, as a people, we are
“t’other siclers.” And next year we must, I
suppose, go through Perth to join a P. & O. or
Orient boat. Not a miner can get work with-
out paying £1 to the Government. We can’t
get our leases consolidated because of some
antediluvianlawthatthelength mustnotbe more
than double the width. No provision is made
for the amalgamation of leases purchased from
the original holders by large English or
European co.’s, and we must pay £1 per acre
per annum to this rotten Government, com-
posed of sandal - wooden - headed W.A’s.— ;
T’othersider. /
The Insensible Defendants.
The malingering of the Abrahams brethren
has become somewhat of another Melbourne
scandal. It is pretty well believed that certain
family-bankruptcy considerations, as well as
nervous dread of a dungeon cell, suggested the
starvation policy which these four miserable
specimens of manhood adopted when their gun-
swindle game was up. Yet they have an even-
ing paper slobbering over their “ misfortunes,”
and speaking of the scene in Melb. Banco Court
last week as “deeply impressive.” It was
merely the spectacle of two particularly craven
humbugs being treated as such by an unsympa-
thetic court. Louis and Emanuel Abrahams,
the two most persistent starvers of the quar-
tette, had so far recovered their health and
intelligence, in spite of themselves, that the
gaol-hospital doctor (a very “hard nail,” it
must be admitted) passed them as fit subjects
to receive sentence. Therefore, Louis was car-
ried into court on a stretcher, “ apparently un-
conscious,” whilst Emanuel was conducted there
by a couple of warders. Nobody expecfed him to
see the matter through like any ordinary
prisoner who doesn’t feel well. Emanuel also
became “apparently unconscious.” Then the
Judge took his seat, and with apparent uncon-
sciousness of the hideous farce that was being
performed for his benefit, passed sentence of 20
months’ hard labor and a tine of £250 upon
each of the prisoners. After which the Court
rose in a matter-of-fact way, and the two
Abrahamses, who obviously grew deader than
ever at the mention of hard labor, were carried
back to the hospital to have some more food
injected into them. Unreflecting people
seem to think that the brothers Abra-
hams deserve to be sent back to their
homes with their tongues in their cheeks,
whereas the system of imprisonment for
respectable criminals might as well be abolished
if these young men were allowed to dodge it
by an artifice available to every defendant of
wormlike character who can afford, or thinks
he can afford, to play larks with his inner
mechanism. Collapse before the jury brought
in a verdict would be the rule with prisoners,
and any who didn’t collapse would have to be
released on the ground that their delicate state
of health prevented them from following the
usual practice of younger and stronger rogues.
The “Unemployed.”
A few days ago a deputation of members of
N.S.W. Parliament waited on Premier Reid
to ask assistance for the unemployed of Sydney,
whose meetings at the Statue keep them con-
stantly in evidence. There were many different
ideas among the deputationists about the nature
of the employment to be supplied, but they
were all solid on one point, which was that
Government should “ float a loan ” and proceed
with public works of some kind. And Premier
Reid promised that, unless a brutal and
degraded Council blocked the way, the loan
should be floated and the works pro-
ceeded with. N.S. Wales has floated a loan
incessantly for 25 years, and proceeded with
public works, and thus found occupation for the
unemployed; and every time the building of
public works flagged for a few months the un-
employed difficulty grew r loud and clamorous,
and it was necessary to float another loan and
start afresh. The old trouble is now as bad as
ever, and the loan-and-public- works remedy is
again on deck. When the loan is exhausted,
the difficulty will turn up again, and another
loan and more public works will be wanted,
and still another loan after that, and so on till
after the 700th loan has been exhausted the
trouble remains just as before. The loan-
and-public-works device doesn’t help the solu-
tion one iota, and the situation may be
summed up very briefly. There either is or is
not an effectual and permanent remedy for the
unemployed curse. If there is a remedy then
it can be found if it is only sought for with
sufficient persistence and intelligence. If there
is no remedy then the Legislature has to decide
whether it will take the brutally logical course
of permanently turning a deaf ear to the un-
employed agitation, or the equally logical
course of borrowing all it can get to stave off
the trouble as long as possible, and then
repudiating the liability when it can’t
borrow any more and can’t pay interest.
In either case the State would be
travelling in some definite direction, and would
know where it was going to, which it certainly
doesn’t Just now. The Bulletin would, there-
fore, suggest that Lyne, or Crick, or some-
body, should, in a friendly and perfectly non-
partisan spirit, ask Premier Reid if he, or the
House in general, knows where he is going, or
if he is even aware that he is going anywhere
in particular. The question should be asked in
a considerate tone, as Lyne, Crick, and co.,
under like conditions, would probably not know
W’here they were going to either. There is no
capital to be made out of the matter by any
political party ; the question to be considered
is simply that the Legislature “duuno where it
are ” in this matter, and has been in that con-
dition for many years, and, therefore, it is
expedient that it should send out an expedition
in search of itself with the least possible delay.
Premier Reid seems, at time of writing,
to have a new difficulty on hand in the
shape of Railway Commissioner Eddy. Mr.
Eddy was originally engaged at .£3OOO a
year, and, when his time expired, Mr. Reid,
according to the published correspondence,
invited him to accept a re-engagement at .£4OOO.
The offer was accepted, and, at this stage of
the proceedings, Mr. Eddy went to England—
partly on business and partly on a holiday.
During his absence the Premier gave notice
that he would invite Parliament to vote the
increased salary. Then, finding there was
serious opposition in view from the Labor-
party, the flabby politician withdrew the notice
and announced that the matter would be re-
submitted at some future unstated date. The
Labor-party still opposed the proposal, and the
affair was quietly dropped, and everything
was left undecided. Now Commissioner Eddy
has returned, and avers that he has in his pos-
session a document by which Mr. Reid pur-
ports to definitely re-engage hint at ,£4OOO per
annum, apparently irrespective of Parliamentary
sanction. Mr. Reid denies this, but, at the
same time, the extra £lOOO was included in his
Estimates. Being interrogated on the subject
in Parliament, however, he shuffled off' the
responsibility by declaring that he didn’t
know the item was there, that he hadn’t
put it there, and that, in short, it had
got there in some unaccountable fashion for
which he wasn’t responsible. As the same item
appeared in the Estimates published last May,
and it has thus been before him for four months,
it would, therefore, seem that the N.S.W.
Treasurer is in an extraordinary state of ignor-
ance about his own estimates, and that he has
remained in that state for a very long time ;
also it would seem that someone else makes up
his budget for him, and that the person in
question takes remarkable liberties with matters
that don’t concern him. Either that, or it
would appear that G. 11. Reid has weakly
drifted into a very tight place through promis-
ing everything to everybody, and is now trying
to weakly crawl out of it. At all events, some
interesting developments may be looked for.
More than two years ago, Bulletin para-
graphs ?'e “ squaring”-of Melbourne magistrates
and juries were discussed in English journals.
The Age and Argus are now treating the
pertinent remarks of Marshall Lyle and
Lormer, J.P., as revelations of a quite unsus-
pected state of rottenness, “If justice can be
bought and sold with impunity there will be
one law' for the rich and another for the poor,”
screams David Syme’s organ, as though Smell-
bourne justice hadn’t been notoriously amenable
to outside influence, or hard cash, ever since
the boom scandals began to come under con-
sideration. The character of the short-sighted
city is practically gone, as a result of the abortive
sup-press policy pursued by bad Governments
under “ respectable ” editorial patronage.
Whilst local asses and deeply-interested hum
bugs are commending the public for their
“ heroic” battle with financial adversity, globe-
trotters and resident critics are representing
Smellbourne morality as it is to their friends
or employers abroad.
Maoriland Local Government Bill contains
no less than 582 clauses, repeals 81 statutes,
and abolishes upwards of 400 existing local
bodies. The number of local bodies is to be
reduced to one-fourth, and the cost of running
the whole system is to be reduced one-half.
Also, the Bill will not become law this year.
Under the new order of things Maoriland prac-
tically reverts to the old Provincial Councils,
with, of course, the elimination of many of the
legislative functions possessed by those bodies,
including the control of the police. Each island
is to be divided into from 15 to 20 counties.
All boroughs with less than 4000 population
are to be made subject to the Council of the
county in which they are situate ; boroughs
with more than that number are to be inde-
pendent as at present. All elections to be on
the “single vote” system, and the quali-
fication is a three months’ residence. All coun-
cillors to go out of office every two years.
Government subsidies to local rates are to be
discontinued, but a per capita contribution of
4s. on borough population and 4s. 6d. in
counties is to be made from the Consolidated
Fund, the balance of necessary county revenue
to be derived from lands and gold-fields. The
Central Govt, is to maintain certain arterial
roads throughout the country. To evolve some-
thing like a workable scheme from the existing
hotch-potch of town councils, road boards,
town boards, borough councils, county councils,
etc., etc., etc., has taken over 12 months, ami
when (in about three years) the Commissioners
have fixed boundaries and satisfactorily settled
all disputed interests, M.L. will have about the
most effective system of Government in the
world.

“By arrangement with ” Plain Bill Hol-
loway, “The Judge’’-was produced at Sydney
Criterion on Saturday, which should help to keep
the memory of the massive Holloway green in the
Australian mind. Still, there is something
anomalous in so light a thing being produced by
arrangement with so heavy a person as Plain Bill;
it is suggestive of a butterfly being produced by
arrangement with a ship’s anchor. “ The Judge ”
1S r a I CO « edy whioh sheds a great light on the state
of the Bench’s liver in private life. It shows a
high legal authority taking pills in the seclusion
of his own home, and hunting for his cough-
lozenge, and inspecting his eye for signs of bile,
and later on he is found with a violent
cold in his head and his feet in hot
water, and a bowl of gruel in his hand,
iben when a lady, who is pursued by the police,
takes refuge on the premises, and the Judge, with
a blanket round him, gets out of his tub to listen
to her tale of woe, and she gives him an obviously
wooden baby and a feeding-bottle to hold, there
is very little judicial dignity left. The moral is
that a Judge should only be seen in the gorgeous
petticoat of office, and his legs should never be
visible to the public. Nobody whose legs are once
seen by the public is much respected afterwards
• # «
So far, Arthur Law’s production hasn’t
received anything like the support it should do.
It is in every respect a highly-deserving comedy,
and worthy of the Criterion in its best days.
LI ton as a Judge in various stages of undress,
with n. wooden infant on hand, and a bigamous
female concealed on the premises, and his feet in
hot water, is a cheerful, riotous affair, and full of
instruction. He shows, among other things, the
evils of giving way to sentiment with a good-look-
mg female, who has got in mysteriously by the
window. He also demonstrates eloquently the
cussedness of early-rising ; the Judge, after trying
vainly to sleep along with the wooden infant, gets
up at 4’30 a.m., and, being cold and offiy half-
dressed, ho there and then consents to his
daughters marriage with two briefless barristers.
Likewise, the play is a whole essay on the
wickedness of drink, for it is the drawing of
a cork by one of the briefless sharks which
wakes up the lady on the sofa, and leads
her to explain that she has been married
to the Judge for a fortnight, and that their
infant is four months old. Misses Ireland and
Madge Corcoran, as the Judge’s two charming
daughters, fortunately fail to notice the dis-
crepancy. The rest of the co. wisely say nothing
about it. Miss Kate Bishop gives a very fine ren-
dering of the bigamous lady, and George Ireland
is somewhat more convincing than usual. Like-
wise there are many good points about Tolano’s
policeman, and Mrs. Walter Hill, as the wife of a
constable with the mumps, is a dreadful warning
to the young man about to marry. Any young
man’s best girl may grow up to look like that for
all he knows to the contrary, therefore let him
gather up his carpet-bag and flee from her in good
time.

Prof. Kennedy has added some new
features to his mesmeric show by putting his
patients into fresh monkey atcitudes, and causing
them unheard-of contortions which
weren’t ‘previously in the bill, and generally
making them rush in where a self-respecting
chimpanzee would feel ashamed to go. The Bul-
letin’s opinion of the exhibition remains the
same as it was last week, only a little more so.
Meanwhile, the fact that the hall of Sydney
School of Arts is crowded to the roof every night
convinces this paper that it is in a minority of one
or thereabouts on this subject, which fact serves
to considerably lower its previous low opinion of
people in general.
** * *
The Gaiety Co. is to make its final appear-
ance at Sydney Lyceum next Thursday (Sept. 9)
—another evidence of the fleeting character of all
earthly pleasures. Its departure will leave a
great blank in the chappiedom and johnnydom of
Sydney, which has spent most of its badly-
retrenched salary at the shrine of Grace Palotta
and Deoima Moore, and has got good value for
its money; taking things all round. The co. is
now doing a hurried round of revivals, and, after
filling in four nights with “In Town,” wherein
the marvellous back-view of Fred Kaye has been
more remarkable than usual, it does “ Gentleman
Joe” tonight (Thursday) and Friday. This
leaves four nights for the “Gaiety Girl,” in the
course of which Fritz Rimma will probably find
the job of carrying a pretty largo charmer to the
bathing-machine a good deal heavier than it was
before the summer weather set in. A hot-wind
night in this country makes the charmer feel
sultry and limp and altogether oppressive, to say
nothing of/the fact that she is sometimes abso-
lutely damp.

other reliable favorite, Harry Shine, also hangs

able tenor in Wallace King, and J. M. Winton

vanishment is as wholly unaccountable as that of
Dibbs’ majority at Tamworth. This last item
has much to recommend it; the supreme aggrava-
tion of the average clown is that he won't vanish
under any circumstances.

lessness every night; and three allegedly French
acrobats present a little thing of their own called
the Devil s Bridge which looks as if it might break
somebody's neck at an early date. At all events
the Boneless Serpent would do well not to stand
underneath while it is going on.

Mr. Rignold got a memorable send-off on
Tuesday, in honour of the eighth and last anni-
versary for the present—of his management at
Her Majesty s. Acting-Governor Darley, the
Ministry, both Houses of Parliament, the City
Council of Sydney, the Speaker, the president of
the Fossilised Chamber, Major-General Hutton,
Parkes, and many other notabilities assisted either
personally or in the spirit, and a fashionable
audience backed them up. The programme in-
eluded every local artist of importance, and their
doings embodied one act of “The Guv’nor,”
the scene from “Julius Ciesar,” in which
Mr. Rignold declaimed over the remains of the
deceased, and many other things. Mr. Rignold
leaves for England to foregather with Brother
Bill and other old friends when “Joseph of
Canaan ” has got to the end of its season, but he
promises to return by-and-bye and renew the
struggle once more. His 61 years or thereabouts
sit so lightly upon him that he will probably keep
his word, and enter on another 20 years’ Austra-
lian experience, and be younger at the end of it
than he is now. The general impression is that
G.R. is immortal.

This week’s double bill at Melb. Princess’s
is not sufficiently polished to reflect new glory
upon the comic opera co. “Nitouche,” when
Nellie Stewart played the convent damsel, had a
little something of French frivolity in its senti-
ment.. Miss Juliet Wray reproduces many of
Nellie s accents, gestures and poses, and her
mimetic performance is full of natural talent for
the stage. But the difference between the person-
alßies of the old artist and the new is vast. The
£r Hi n- tin o 89 ’ eleßanCH ’ r °guishness and dimphs of
Nellie Stewart are missing from the present imita-
tion or, at least, the present writer can’t see
them. It is hard for a Gentile to imagine Miss
Wrays Nitouche could pass for an artless
creature with the lady superior of any blessed
convent, or to believe that she has had a course of
convent training. Her affected humility and
demureness are as unconvincing as the aspect of
the three high-kickers who wear Salvation Harmy
garb in In Town.” Yet there is no getting
away from the fact that Miss Wray has a charm
for her own people. No young actress could be
more distinctly a matter of taste. Her vivacity is
magnetic, or somewhat vulgar, her voice sweet or
(at times) bleaty, her appearance attractive or
otherwise. Opinions vary from one extreme to
the other on all these points. Miss Wray seems
to be universally admired by Hebrews—she is, in
fact, Williamson and Musgrove’s bid for a sec-
tarian vote, and as a stroke of policy this intelli-
gent, effervescent, typical Australian Jewess must
be reckoned a genuine success. George Lauri
makes the farcical organist of last year a wilder
caricature than before ; also “Ni touche ” has
become a rougher joke in other respects.

Plumpton’s “operetta,” “An Arcadian
Eve,” turns out to be a weary fragment, and the
incidental music has no grip in it. A young
married man buys an Aladdin ring, wherewith a
Greek god and goddess are summoned to his sit-
ting-room. The young husband hugs the goddess
after which domestic matters are squared by the
discovery that his wife is flirting with Apollo
As soon as the husband agrees to relinquish his
bit of jewellery the two immortals retire through
cardboard doors in the parlor wall. There is
room for stage effects in this ancient, fish-like idea
but since it isn’t worth the expense of super-
natural arrivals and departures, Miss Florence
It oung and Wallace are merely presented in the
form of modern masqueraders who have cut pas-
sages through the scenery. Miss Pollard and
lapley play the prosaic couple doomed to receive
them, the voices of the immortals being much
more powerful than those of the foggy-chested
worldlings of to-day.

At Melb. Cambridge, manager Percy St.
John seems to deserve encouragement in spite of
his dudelike name. The first part contains
joyously idiotic choruses, and some of the per-
formers have brought with them the recommenda-
tion of a long absence from Melbourne. A long-
absent nigger diffuses an air of freshness vainly
sought in the nigger who has long been riveted
to the neighborhood. There ought to be an
Act of Parliament compelling corner men to stay
away at fixed intervals for years at a stretch. A
burlesque, of doubtful origin, professes to be tbe
backbone of the Cambridge revels, and an intelli-
gent orchestra under Harcourt Lee’s guidance
floods the little forest of legs with crimson melody.
Man while a great attraction is mysteriously pro-
mised for Saturday next.

Miss Jennings Carmichael (a lucky suitor
lately induced her to take the name of Mrs.
Mnllis) contributed verse to the Melb. weeklies
long before her beautiful dark eyes had pierced
the heart of man. She was a maiden of 12, catch-
ing inspiration from the wild Gippsland country,
nigh the Snowy River, when the earliest outpour-
ings of her muse trickled into print. From that
time until now the subject of this notice has been
a welcome visitor to the Poets’ Corner in the
Australasian and elsewhere, and a collection of
her verses will shortly be launched on the London
market. “ Hospital Children,” a little book pub-
Photo. by Nadar.
Miss JENNINGS CARMICHAEL.
lished some time ago by Miss Carmichael, was
the fruits of her two years’ experience as a trained
nurse, for which profession she was the first Vic-
torian girl to qualify herself with a certificate.
The Bulletin's photo, of this earnest worker and
charming example of literary womanhood is
apropos of her forthcoming dehut as a lecturess.
At the Masonic Hall, Melb., on Wednesday next
(Sept. 18th), Miss Carmichael will discuss “The
Spirit of the Bush,” a spirit congenial to her own.
The lecturess, who hopes to get through her por-
tion of the entertainment in 40 minutes, is to be
introduced by the eloquent Deakin in a young-
Australian strain, and the second half of an
interesting programme will comprise Australian
songs and recitations by Ida Osborne, Eloise Juno,
and other artists identified with the soil. Also,
Australia’s flag has promised to float proudly over
the proceedings, and a modest scale of prices will
bring “The Spirit of the Bush ” within the reach
of all who would like an introduction.

Adelaide Sept. 25 for India, China, and Japan, taking
nearly the whole of his present co. Hudson, who already

A certain veteran tenor is a regular attendant, at Mrs.
Rising s (Meih.) spiritist stances. The tuneful Mrs.
Palmer was persuaded to go recently ; but she broke up
the show and upset the “meejum” by laughing loudly.
The cause of her mirth was a message from Dr. Mother-
well “ to be sure and eat plenty of garlic.”
K.yrle Bellew is alleged to have been pitching ttum
some tall narratives of ids youthful days in Australia.
After landing at Melbourne, he walked 500 miles to the
new diggings at Yugilvar Reefs, and had the good luck
(in conjunction with a friend) to strike a reef, containing
gold quartz. The reef seems to have been rather tougher
than Kyrley and his friend had expected, so they took
three other men into partnership and worked the claim
for a year, after which they sold out to speculators for
195,000. The gorgeous one, however, “invested his
share in other undertakings and lost it all.” so he wa<
reduced to clearing timber off 500 acres at ninepence an
acre. With his 500 ninepeiices he built a ferry-boat, and
ran a cemetery', w here business was so brisk, owing to an
epidemic, that the police contracted with him to get the
victims buried on reduced terms. And so on.
Lena Salinger, late of Australia, was married lately in
Montana, U.S., during a play, “ by r a real priest.” This
,s the second time a marriage has taken place on the
stage. The other case was when Louise Pomeroy married
An bur Elliott, in “ Romeo and Juliet,” at Ballarat.
Bland Holt, in “Saved from the Sea": “ Procrastina-
tion runs in the family. If fattier hadn’t putoff proposing
to mother, I should have been born six months earlier
than I was.”
Australia’s musical galaxy: Melba, Amy Sherwim
Frances Saville, Nellie Stewart, Alice Reeves, Ada
Crossley, Lalla Miranda, vocalists; Kruse, violinist;
Hutchinson, pianist. No male vocalist of eminence, no
female instrumentalist. Victoria has come off much the
best of the Australian provinces.

ment of Harwood, Stewart, Hennings, and Coppin, who
to-day are, all of them, as much alive as their old house.

Dion Boucioault's knowledge of furniture and bric-a-
brac is unrivalled in Australia. In producing a new piece
—more especially it it tie costume—VVallachs are worried
to death with his insistence that every detail shall be of the
period represented. And before leaving a town he must
know the whereabouts of everything. Once he turned
out all the baggage merely to satisfy himself as to a miss-
ing candlestick.

Fitzgerald Brothers’ talking horse does sums by rapping
out the numbers necessary. The trainer, Proba»co, also
claims for him the power of mind-reading, so H at if
numbers are written on a slate in the audience the horse
raps them out on the stage with bis hoof. Another feature
of this clever animal is his attachment to his trainer,
whom be follows about. One evening Probasco and a
friend entered a London hotel, amt went up a winding
staircase to a bar above. Being recognised, a conversa-
tion began as to the merits of Mahomnud, and eventually
bets were made with a London Johnny that the horse
would follow Probasco up the winding staircase to the
bar. Probasco went away to the stable, which was close
by, and presently returned, followed by the horse, which
coolly ascended the stairs and turned tip at the bar. The
Johnny was dumbfounded, but paid his bets and sold—
“ Bai Jove, he’s a vewy olevah beast—he knows how many
beans make five." Whereupon Mahommed rapped out
five on the floor. Good yarn.
