To read this ! - A handsome illustrated catalogue
has just been issued by that old-established drapery
firm of Riley Brothers, 568-570 George-st., Syd-
ney. It contains a wonderful amount of informa-
tion, and quotes the lowest prices of good articles
in Household Drapery, Ladies’ and Children’s
Wear, Men’s and Boy’s Clothing, Boots and
Slices. Riley Brothers still continue the system
originated by them 20 years ago of sending
Drapery Bales and £2 parcels, carriage paid, and
a large business is now done daily in
postal orders from Gd. upwards. One of Riley
Brothers’ latest investments is the purchase of the
entire stock in N. S. W, of Dr. Scott’s celebrated
electric corsets and hair-brushes. Eminent doctors
say, and thousands of testimonials aver, that the
corsets are a splendid remedy for ladies’ ailments,
especially backache, and the electric brushes give
instant relief in cases of sick headache, neuralgia,
sleeplessness. The corsets are just like ordinary
stays, and perfect-fitting. Sizes only 24 to 21)
inches ; price, Gs., post free. The brushes are
pure bristles, not wire ; price, 3s. Gd., post free.
Please send to Riley Brothers, 568 570 George-st,
Sydney, for new catalogue.

ficial to the human constitution as thorough
cleanliness, a theory verified by modern revela-
tions of the deadly microbes which infest the
Hesh and blood—especially if we do not keep
ourselves in a clean condition, which ought to be
the first aim of every individual with any esteem
for himself. The use of Amykos has proved to be
very important to this end, by its great sanitary
power over the skin, mouth, teeth, &c. If you
value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration.*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
September 10, 1895.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
The ball given in Randwick Town Hall last
Thursday, in aid of the St. Vincent de Paul
charity, was redolent of Randwick —the suburb
where turfites most do congregate. The room
looked more like a saddiing-paddock than a town
hall. Sweet charity always leads to the most en-
joyable mixtures, and the most interesting partners
in the crowd were several smart-looking jockeys—
heroes of the hour—whom the society gang present
openly worshipped, with a hungry yearning for a
“moral for the Metrop.” in their eyes, regardless
of the well-known saying anent jockeys’ tips.
But, so far as I heard, the light-weight sphinxes
acted as sich. The room was cheerfully dressed,
a la “Jolly Japan,” with lanterns and scarlet
umbrellas, and, as the dance was financially a
success, the hon. secs., Mrs. Tom Barlow (in well-
cut white satin brocade and chiffon) and 'Mrs.
M‘Manus (in white silk), together with Mrs.
Leach and Mrs. T. Payten, as hon. treasuresses,
deserve all sorts of congratulations,
“ Take away the tea ; we don’t want tea,” is not
exactly a feminine sentiment ; and the num-
ber of women retailing tea and scones for a
livelihood in more-or-less artistic bun-shops
is not likely to be lessened by the fact that
an attractive society damsel —a daughter of an old
N.S. W. family of deservedly high social position
—who was a ministering angel in the pioneer
society-managed tea-shop has already captured a
swain at the spout, as you might say, in the
person of a young professional man of good pros-
pects. But it is only a fair quid pro quo for a
youth to sternly insist upon marrying the maiden
who destroys him with soda scones and tannin at
high noon. The least she can do is to nuise him
for the rest of his days after having made ducks
and drakes of his digestive organs.
I am sorry to have to say that a good many of
the so-called “society” tea-shops are haunts of
plain living and high winking. It is the bad cook-
ing and the very superior spooning so often and
unavoidably kept on the premises when “lydies ”
are at the helm that sends busy men to grilled
chops, served by a waiter in shirt-sleeves
travelling over a saw-dust floor, with no accessory
coquetries of frilly muslin, curtains and pots of
verdure. And the busy man is right.
The popular manager of a lively theatrical co.
tells a Trilby sort of yarn about an experience he
once had. He was putting a would-be star
through her paces, and, after having tested looks,
voice and all that, naturally expressed a respect-
ful wish to know if the lady was—er —well-enough
supported to go into the ballet, which now and
then presents a forked appearance. Imagine his
consternation (that’s the name he gives it) when
the aspirant in a twinkle revealed a lace petticoat
and a pair of cotton tights and levelled a spirited
if clumsy kick at the managerial nose. She was
engaged, but, strangely enough, never got an inch
beyond the second row. A lady of her capacity
ought to be playing Mrs. Fotiphar by now.
Apropos of Trilby. The fashion of having your
left foot photographed will not, I predict, hook
itself violently on to Australian society, for the
simple reason that boots are narrow and feet are
wide, and bunions and corns grow side by side.
How many women are there who could unveil
their silver pedestal to the profane gaze of a
camera-fiend without the result being reminiscent
of a section of gnarled timber? Echo answers, a
mere handful. Just think what a betrayal of
Bufferings nobly borne—what a tragedy in five
toes it would be if society took off its near-side
boot and got its bare foot photo’d ! How the
arithmetical impossibility of putting five into two
would be refuted as the imprisoned foot came out
with a pop from its narrow Parisian case and
commenced to expand into a palm-leaf fan. No—
Trilby feet-studies won’t be general. So far,
society gets the hoof of its most uncompressed
offspring taken instead—a wealthy Sydney man
having set the fashion.
The planet of the star concert co. (managed by
Mr. Stevens and Mr. Howells) is certainly Mark
Hambourg, the pianist—a lad of 16, with a
Byronic look about his collar and tie, a shock of
darK-brown hair, and a high-strung expression of
countenance. As an artist he is superb— his
exuberant youth favors his fervid, furious style, of
course, and though he has a slap dash way of
mauling the piano, somewhat reminiscent of the
high-action trotter, and, though he makes melody
something like some animals make a hole—that is
to say he burrows into the music with extravagant
gestures—still, all these perfectly natural atten-
dant phenomena suit him very well. I heard him
expounding Beethoven and Ghopin. His touch
suggests the iron hand in the velvet glove, and he
carries his hands like a horseman, well down, in
defiance of the old-fashioned “wrists arched”
theory. He is a great artist, undoubtedly—a
boy’s-size Paderewski ; and the women are begin-
ning to worship already—his applause is always
the loudest and strongest. You see he is too
young to have married anybody yet, and women
always encore a bachelor on the off-chance.
Miss Lily Moody, the contralto, is a loss to the
operatic stage, and shows she grieves for it by
the “ business ” she puts into her platform work.
I like the sonorous contralto quality of herpower-
ful voice, and hers is a free, generous sort of sing-
fulness suggesting reservoirs of lung-power and
melody—a large, good-tempered voice in cordial
agreement with her comic-opera nose. Her articu-
lation is excellent, which is not a vocal virtue of
Miss Evangeline Florence (she studied music at
the same time, and from the same professor, as
Grace Palotta, who was greatly interested in her
Australian debut), whose sweet, high, true soprano
voice is indeed “ bird-like,” for 1 never met any-
body yet who could tell me what Dickey was
piping about—have you? Nevertheless, Miss
Florence is a great success, ranging to F in alt.,
which she sings with a pure, silvery intona-
tion. She has graceful movements and a re-
fined stage-presence; and on Saturday morning she
looked very dainty in a muslin gown, with a
romantic pink and black hat. Miss Lily Moody
wore muslin too, with a big black cavalier bat on
one ear and no gloves, so far as I could see. Miss
Moody’s style is as debonair as her voice—a slap-
you-on-the-back sort of a maiden, whom you
instinctively take a liking to. By the way, when
the two lasses took to singing duets, the effect
was reminiscent of locusts and wild honey—Miss
F. as the insect and Miss M. as the other thing.
Mr. Weir assisted the co. with his refined tenor
voice, and Herr Yollmar accompanied at what
was in every way a bright, first-class concert.
Apropos of concerts, at a recent rather good
sample of the genus, the real audience was enter-
tained by the suggestive behaviour of a couple of
men, who stamped in at an inopportune moment,
took a seat in the front row. and began to wildly
entertain one another with loud conversation,
under the very eyes of the performers. Then,
when their conversational powers ran out (which
happened very soon, of course) one man settled
himself with studied carelessness, and, drawing
out a newspaper, folded it exactly to his liking
and remained buried in its columns for the rest of
the evening. He suggested a man who hasn’t
got any light—not even a candle in a bottle—like
Weary Willie, who absorbs his mental pabulum
by lamp-post light. Still, people who only come
in for warmth and shelter ought to get a little
further back.
The Gaiety Co. put up “In Town” again at
Sydney Lyceum last Saturday, in front of the
usual fluttering, chatty, restless, humming, first-
night Lyceum dress-circle, which was just a
little less full than usual. There are lots of
smart sayings “In Town,” I believe —I nearly
caught one, only just as I thought I had it
a fat lady behind me, who kept beating her beaded
breast with a palm-leaf fan—thump, rattle, like a
penitent doing peccavi—called out to a friend four
seats away that “a nasty tomcat ate four of the
chicks, only out last Wednesday,” and the friend
(who had bell bracelets on and sounded like a
dust-cart) replied, in good carrying tones, that
“children were safer than chickens; cats never
ate them.” Whereupon the whole company
laughed outright and waited for a retort from
Beads. She was silent (barring the rattle of her
trimmings), and 1 had almost got to the hang of
“ some do it this way,” when an avalanche fell
over me from the starboard in the shape of a large
fat man in a boiled shirt, who caught his patent-
leather feet in my flounce, and apologised most
loudly and handsomely, and then found he was
jammed unless I rose and folded my seat, which I
did at once, of course ; but, by the time he had
fallen into the aisle and disturbed the whole
colony of people sitting on the steps (who all had
to have their pardons begged), the song was over,
of course.
Decima Moore makes a pretty Kitty Hether-
ington. I wanted to hear the “Romeo and
Juliet ” duet—it looks as if it was really good—
but, just as Bradfield and Decima turned up,
Beads was seized with a mad longing to tell the
dust-cart and the rest (the rest were only aggres-
sive by reason of incessant giggling and strong
mixed perfumes—l didn’t mind ’em all fanning
together and blowing my hair every way) all
about her new hat for the races, which was quite
a poem, trimmed with green bows—sash-ribbon
width—with clusters of cornflowers and diamond
pins. The recital of these glories had the effect
of quieting the rest for a second or so, and I
nearly heard some of the stage dialogue when my
knees were knocked sideways, and the fat man
appeared again with a pair of hired opera-glasses.
He burst past me all right, but found a hitherto
unseen friend in the dust-cart, whose bells rang a
regular peal as she leant all over a youth sitting
in front of her, and began airy badinage with the
fat man, who of course dropped the sweets he was
carrying, which scattered every way, and made
everybody choke with laughter. Then—but I
mean to continue this at the earliest opportunity.
You get a lot of it at the Lyceum, and lam in
hope that Mr. Williamson will engage Fritz
Rimma to stay behind in Australia and throw
out the magpie brigade—especially on Saturday
nights—with great violence.
The most impressive thing about “The Judge '
at Sydney Criterion was the sure and certain fear
everybody felt that Kate Bishop would be caught
and thrown back into her dungeon in the last act,
directly the female policeman took the chase in
hand. The awful fact of woman’s inhumanity to
woman was never more glaringly brought home
to a guilty dress-circle. Apart from these
gloomy suggestions, “The Judge” is a brightly-
dressed, well-staged nightmare. Harrie Ireland’s
principal frock was a blue satin, cut below high-
modesty mark, and terminating in a pair of blue
shoes, while Madge Corcoran sports a cream satin
Empire gown, which every young matron should
rush to get a pattern of. Miss Kate Bishop (who
got a good reception) wore black all through the
piece, but William Elton’s attire principally con-
sisted of a blanket and a pair of bare legs.
But masculine legs are a farthing a dozen on
the stage —so far as thrill goes. There is nothing
exciting about the revelation of a pair of male
pins, which are always with us, as you may say.
Man is a bifurcated animal every day of his life—
even the most unsophisticated child of nature
doesn’t think that he terminates in a merman’s
tail—and it doesn’t cause a very howling sensa-
tion if he goes a step further and takes off his
boots and socks. Now, if the foot-bath incident
occurred to “ Matilda ” instead of “John” what
a thrill it would create, to be sure ! There was an
appreciative house for the first night, including a
box full of ladies who powdered their noses
during one of the waits with a puff the size of a
snowball.
Lately, within a few days of one another, two
greatly - respected citizens of Sydney passed
off the scene for ever—Mr. W. H. Paling,
whose magnificent gift of the Carington Hospital
property at Camden, together with a cheque for
ten thousand pounds, provide a lasting monument
to his memory better than all the “ storied urns ”
in the world ; and Mr. Henry Wise (inevitably
nicknamed “Penny ” Wise), a trustee of N.S.W.
Savings Bank. Mr. Wise was an authority on
matters of Art, though he refrained from bearing
office in any of the societies.
Next week will be a festive whirl in Sydney,
what with races, visitors, new Spring fashions,
and evening parties. The A. J.O. Spring meeting
commences to-day (Saturday), and is to be con-
tinued Tuesday, Thursday and Saturday, and, in
between, comes the ever-lively polo Gymkhana at
Kensington racecourse (P. O’Mara, secretary);
a reception, given by Major-General and Mrs.
Hutton, to meet the Victorian Commandant and
Lady Holled-Smith ; and balls and hops galore,
given by Government House, the Australian
Club, and other tried hosts. Taking it altogether,
any society person who doesn’t know how to
whiz had better flee into the mountains, or go to
Coolgardie until this annual burst of frivolity
peters out.
With the racing season almost quite ripe, it was
no small-sized treat to me to have the privilege,
the other morning, of going over Newmarket —
the well-appointed stables at Randwick, where the
lengthy and amiable “ Tom ” Payten looks after
the high-priced gee-gees run by “J. B. Clark.”
The surprising feature about all these beautiful
thoroughbreds, without exception, is their doci-
lity. They all behave perfectly, and take as much
wholesale petting as so many babies. I saw the
imported Fitz Donovan, a son of the English
Derby winner, Donovan. He is a most artistic-
looking nag, of an uncommon, coppery-sorrel
sort of color, with a mane like a lion’s. When he
arrived from England he was a most pronounced
rogue, but now he is as amiable as the rest. I
saw Mrs. James White’s darling Coil, a son of
Abercorn, with a market value high in the
thousands, and I saw old Autonomy having a
rest after his many triumphs—l saw everything, in
fact—all the equine heroes past, present, or to
come, which are domiciled at Newmarket. The
stables look like a ball-room —polished wood for
the stall-doors; all the brasswork as bright as a
ship’s; beautifully-swept asphalt floor ; a balcony
all round, and above that a lofty dome. You
could pass the “Newmarket” nags in review
in a light silk dress and satin shoes.
A batch of new music lately received includes
the“Epacris” waltz, by Darrell Bensusan, It
is a most tuneful and excellent dancing waltz—
every moment is pretty and the tempo is smoothly
sustained throughout. From Gordon and Gotch
comes a rollicking sort of paraphrase of “ His
Heart was true to Poll,’’called “ True to Nell,” by
Harvey Barnett, words by W.G.S. to the effect
that the frantic wiles of strange ladies are thrown
away on a gentleman whose heart is allegedly
true to Nell; but the awful ignorance of feminine
foibles displayed in the lines, “ Your hair is
charming I must own, but Nell's you ought to see,"
spoils the whole thing. Any man so ignorant as
to ask a girl to acknowledge the superiority of a
rival’s back hair would probably never have a
Nell to be true to. The music is rattling. From
W. D. Johnstone and Co. comes a polka, “The
B. and 8.,” by Amy Armstrong. The polka is
an obsolete dance just now, so it seems rather a
waste of musical energy to write music for
it; still, if there is anybody anywhere who
dances the polka “The B. and B.” may be re-
commended for coming down with the necessary
thump.
Talking about music, Maud FitzStubbs (who
will probably go on a South African concert-tour
with her old master, Giorza, ere returning to Aus-
tralia) seems to be going forward in London. The
“Heather Waltz ” was played at a State ball at
Buckingham Palace ; and at a ladies’ night at the
Gallery Club, Maud and Madeline FitzStubbs
played the “Heather” as a duo ; appearing again
in another duo, “Nocturne in F,” by Maud afore-
said. Both ladies had a good reception.
My Melb. correspondent writes ;
“ The only detail of the part of Rosette that did
not fit May Pollard like a glove was the raiment
in the second act. An economical management
had evidently designed that Nellie Stewart’s
mantle should literally descend on May’s broad
shoulders, with the result that the observant eye
detected at once that it cramped Rosette 11.
across the chest, was inches too long in the waist,
and altogether had a hand-me-down look about it.
The robe de nuit, as worn by May, is a degree
more actual than was her predecessor’s, which is
saying a good deal.
“ Melb. women have, with one consent, shrouded
themselves in black crepon. One would imagine,
from a walk down the Block, that everyone was in
mourning for some national calamity. But that’s
characteristic ; no sooner does a style have a vogue,
here than it becomes a uniform. At a fashionable
garden-party last week nearly every female, young
or old, married or single, wore black crepon, with
most depressing effect. Certainly, they endea-
vored to lighten the gloom by hats of bewilder-
ing hues, but with small success. The blade gown
per se is becoming and natty, but, in crowds, it is
sombre and inartistic.
“At the first Melb. concert of the Star Co.,
Miss Evangeline Florence was taken ill, and a
doctor summoned to the performer’s room. Medi-
cus made himself agreeable by condoling with the
musicians on the poor attendance and the empty
balcony. ‘ Don’t trouble yourself,'replied Mark
Hambourg, cheerfully, ‘l’ll bring the people!’
And he did.
“ At the Wednesday afternoon concert in Melb.
TownHall.theyouthfulMaxsuccessfullyperformed
a f< at never before attempted here and seldom in
the world. In a composition of Chopin’s occurs
a of prestissimo passages to be played in
octave chords by both hands. The are so
difficult and the passages so rapid that Frederic
himself could only manage single notes in the
bass. This piece of dexterity fairly caught musi-
ca' Melb. by the hair of the head, and from henoe-
forth sixteen-year-old Max will be spoken of as
greatest pianist who ever shook his long hair
' n the Melb. public’s face.
“Vic. vice-regal balls follow one another so
hurriedly that very select people are privately
glad Gov. Brassey will soon cry ‘Over ! ’ ‘Seats
free—everybody invited,’ might have been the
motto of the last function, where more debutantes
hot-housed into celebrity sat all night in a flat,
forlorn row against the wall, and kept up a heart-
breaking pretence of ‘ enjoying myself so much,
thank you.’ His Waxcellency again wore his
court suit; and Lady Holled Smith—Smell-
bourne’s only ‘garrison belle’—shimmered sump*
tuously in scarlet poppies.
“ Mrs. Frank Stephen gave a new notion of in-
expensive dressing by a close-fitting, black-velvet
skirt and mauve-silk bodice, with a breast-plate of
silver lace. Mrs. Albert Miller, as an exponent
of light and leading, appeared in an aesthetic com-
bination of greys; and Mrs. F. T. Sargood, in plain
black silk and spectacles, lent severity to a parti-
cularly emphatic set of lancers. Miss Berry gave
the rightful setting to damask cheeks -and dark
e y es with an Empire robe of pale-yellow silk,
But the quaintest spectacle was Baron Von
Mueller, with his chest court-plastered with orders
and a long and dingy comforter trailing from his
coat-pocket to the ground.
“No one accuses the Maddens of saving out of
the vice-regal allowance. During the regime,
everyone autographing the visitors’ book has been
promptly rewarded by an invitation, a state of
things most conducive to loyalty.

Ormond College (Melb.) staff, has a sweet tenor

and graces endear him equally to local maidens,
who prefer to discredit the rumor of his engage-
ment to Miss Faber, of the B. and B. Co. Mr.
Naylor’s alleged fiancee shares his knack of popu-
larity and has hosts of girlfriends—notably the
Acting-Governor’s daughters,
“ A suburban lady, daughter of a late Melb.
Chamber of Commerce magnate, is to be married
in a day or two to Hon. John Wallace, the
leviathan mining speculator and reputed million-
aire. There is a difference of 55 years between

has a family by a former marriage, and celebrated
his 70th birthday a few years ago. The late Mrs.
Wallace had been a Miss Monaghan, and that
splendid Swanston-street block opposite St. Paul’s
Cathedral, known as ‘Monaghan’s buildings,’ is
now held in trust for her children, one of whom,
a pretty girl of 12, is looked upon with interest as
the catch of the future. The bride-elect is a
talkative young lady, much addicted to hymn-
singing and harmonium-playing at seamen’s
mission-meetings and church working-bees.

turquoise-blue satin, with big, upstanding, gold-

and violets in the front. Even the hilarious red
of her silk blouse couldn’t dominate this particulai
hat.

able woman who has taken to bicycling. The
bike subjected to the task of carrying her has no
sinecure. The lady of the wondrous hats has
golden hair and pink-all-over complexion, and
she wears white gloves on nearly all occasions.

The Redfeni railway disaster has (apart
from costs) cost New South Wales .£34,361
in damages awarded to 33 claimants or
their relatives. The total claims amounted to
£167,469, so that Supreme Court juries have
discounted them by £133,137, or about four-fifths.
The largest sum paid was £6,100 to Dr. Maher,
who claimed £30,000; but other claimants for
£30,000 got nil, and in not one case was there
awarded anything like the amount asked-for.
The policy of the Railway Commissioners in
settling only the most moderate claims out of
court, and expediting the suits by admitting
liability and leaving only the question of damages
for decision, has been thoroughly justified, for it
is hardly likely the claimants would have volun-
tarily reduced their demands by four-fifths, how-
ever persuasively approached. And there seems
no reason to question the absolute fairness of the
awards on the whole; especially when it is con-
sidered that it is only by aet of grace and Act of
Parliament that the country is liable at all, and
that there is no real reason why any individual
should not be expected to insure himself against
railway risks (not due to culpable negligence) be-
fore he takes a railway journey,

The B. L. Corto Fabrica de tabacos (which
means a Havanna cigar-factory) thus advertises
its Rothschild cigars I The tobacco from which
our cigars are made is grown entirely in conserva-
tories, and the cigars are made on mahogany and
marble tables by thoroughbred artists in swallow-
tail coats and white kid gloves. When a man
smokes one of these cigars he simply walks on air,
and dreams that he has a ten-carat diamond scarf-
pin, and a ten-guinea suit of clothes on, and just
married “ rich.” They make the teeth sweet and
the breath white, and will force a moustache on
the smoothest lip in a few years. They improve
and beautify the complexion, eradicate tan,
freckles, dandruff on all baldheads. They per-
meate the house, window curtains, and clothes
with the exquisite odour and delicate fragrance of
heliotrope, new-mown hay and jockey-club. No
well-regulated family can properly keep house
without a box of the B. L. Corto Rothschild
cigars, for the man who smokes them will never
swear, or join a club, or want his own way
“always.” After cremation the ashes of these
cigars are the most powerful concentrated tooth-
powder yet discovered. The B. L. Corto Roths-
child are obtainable at all respectable tobacconists
in Australasia, price 6d each.*

# « M
The agitation among the conductors on the
Ocean-street (Sydney) cable trams has an air of
mystery about it. The employes ha ve not struck,
nor pilfered, perhaps, nor killed anyone ; yet the
authorities are sacking, disrating, and trans-
ferring said conductors. The men profess ignor-
ance of the cause. The gripmen are untouched —
only the conductors are “ made examples of.’’ It
is generally believed that petticoats and secret ap-
pointments are at the bottom of the trouble. In
every Australian city the tram-conductor is the
gayest of Lotharios. Perhaps it’s the brass
buttons; But opportunity is everything. The
conductor is privileged to speak to a “Lydy” every
time she cables it in or out of town ; and if he is a
very nice conductor, young and polite, and comely,
the “ Lydy ” soon gets to be on at least as friendly
terms with him as Bridget is with her vegetable-
John.
• # #
S. A. Govt, has settled the Adelaide Hos-
pital dispute by giving nurse Graham leave of
absence on full pay till the present Board’s term
of office expires. Then the Board will be super-
seded and the nurse will return to iier duties.
Premier Kingston will treat as a no-confidence
motion any proposal in Parliament seeking to sup-
port the Board as against th» nurses, to whom he
has vowed that justice shall be done. Medical
trades-unionists threaten to boycott the Hospital,
but this expedient is not likely to succeed There
are too many hungry doctors.

A well-known firm of Adelaide solicitors
lost one of its most respected clients a while
ago. Deceased was such a nice man that the firm
sent a partner to follow the funeral, and be visibly
affected when the actual interment took place.
It was reckoned a gratuitous act of sympathy on
the part of sorrowing legal adviser* until the
widow’s executors got a bill-of-costs from Flint
and Stealey. On the bill was a modest item of
-G1 Is. for “attendance at grave,” although the
firm charged nothing for its silent tear.

M‘G. : A question for the W.C.T.U.,
whieh assumes every woman an icicle shattered,
not thawed, by the berroot man. How is it that
the average girl, in choosing a fancy-ball cos-
tume, so often takes a character possessing no
“character” at all? In a recent Australasian
is presented a page of thirteen sassiety damsels
as they appeared at Acting-Gov. Madden’s fancy
ball, and of this number five arrayed themselves
as Princess de Lamballe, Lady Hamilton, Lady
Teazle, La Fri vole and Ma Mie Rosette. Oneof the
two historical personages mentioned was deci-
dedly “off-color,” while the three others of fiction
carry with them a host of blushful associations
and situations. Where is the man who would
dare to stroll up in the costume of Don Juan?
While they are at it, the W.C.T.U. might also
explain why nine women out of every ten, in
writing up a confession-album, always give some
extremely naughty female as their favorite
character in history; Who has not turned over
the pages of one of these volumes and seen Mary,
Queen of Scots, on every other page ?

Crown-prosecutor Wade obtained the con-
viction of Butler, the Darlington chemist’s
assistant, for conspiracy in the divorce-ease. As
a result of that conviction, the Johnson inquiry
has been instituted, and the Crown —instead of
having someone else to watch its interests—is
again represented by Mr. Wade, who is virtually
defending Butler—the man he, a few days ago,
prosecuted.

Though everybody in Melb. is perfectly
aware of the truth of “ J. P.” Dormer’s strictures
on J.’s.P. generally, and especially in regard to
Melb. District Court, the fact remains that Mr.
Dormer himself, an avowed aggressive teetotaller,
only attends that tribunal on Thursdays—the day
on which licensing cases are heard. Mr. L finer
certainly explains this coincidence in a perfectly
clear manner, and nobody who knows the man
would doubt his word ; still, an ordinary sense of
decency should prompt him to steer clear of
adjudicating on subjects touching which he bolds
such pronounced and, to the lay mind, exaggerated
views. ,

Attorney-General Isaacs had the conscience
to drop heavily upon a couple of Victorian magis-
trates the other day. A clerk of petty sessions
pleaded guilty to a charge of embezzlement,
whereupon two honorary justices, overruling the
P.M. in the chair, inflicted a small fine upon the
clerk, and sent him rejoicing on his way. Isaacs,
horrified at such an extraordinary proceeding,
called for their resignations. All things eon-
sidered, Attorney-General Isaacs displayed a rash
spirit in this incident. He didn’t allow for the
chance of it becoming generally known in Mel-
bourne, where two embezzling clerks of a very big
legal firm lately pleaded guilty, without getting
themselves, or anybody else, into trouble. Ihe
junior clerk threw himself upon the mercy of the
P. \f. and was sentenced to a small fine ; the
senior clerk faced Judge Hood, in the Grim.
Court, and was —released on his own recognisance
OUR REPTILE CONTEMPORARY.
SCHOOL INSPECTOR (at Dandaloo): “The historian seems to have enter-
tained a very mean opinion of his contemporaries. Now, what
is a ‘ contemporary? ”
YOUNG STUBBS (whose father takes the “Star”): “Please, sir, an
'Evennin' Noose,' sir.”
The moral of this story sticks right out. It is
beautifully obvious. The P.M., the Judge, and
Isaac Isaacs ought to send in their resignations,
if the two country justices were righteously dealt
with.

cell, he is empowered to sit in judgment on the
works of Dickens and Scott and Victor Hugo,
and pass a final decision thereon. Consider the
humor of it!

Dear Bulletin, —How is it that one of
Sydney’s large hospitals—largely subscribed to by
the public—is apparently run in the interest of a
certain clique? Nearly the whole of the honorary
medical staff is composed of men with a Scotch
qualification, and though honorary, these appoint-
ments are much sought after, being a very good
ad vt. 1 n Melbourne the subscribers elect the staff;
in Sydney the election lies with a favored few. Only
a few days since an extra specialist in the hos-
pital referred to was requited, and, of course, an
M.D., Edin., was appointed. There were three
Australians, graduates in England, who applied,
but were passed over. By the bye, the successful
applicant holds a similar appointment at another
hospital, thus depriving some brother specialist of
a chance, however small.— Medious.
The wlr between the Scotch and Irish medical
factions threatens to break out again in another
place. Dr. Graham having set afoot a movement
for the establishment of a maternity hospital in
Sydney, Dr. Mac Donnell Kelly writes indignantly
to the press pointing out that such an institution
already exists—at 561 Elizabeth-street, near Cleve-
land-street. “It has a full medical staff, and a
complete course of lectures in midwifery is given
there at stated times. The management is under
a committee of ladies, who are actuated only by
motives of charity, and who receive no remunera-
tion whatever for their services. Once a month a
party, comprising several leading ladies of Sydney,
well-known for their philanthropy, visits the in-
stitution. All classes and creeds are admitted.
There are no questions asked, and everything is
donetoenablepatientsto bear their trouble in peace
and resignation. The home is entirely non-sec-
tarian. and relies for support on the charitable
contributions of the general public, and has never
appealed to the Government for assistance.”

Sydney legal anomalies of one week : A
cattle-salesman is fined for selling diseased cattle,
though he swears he knew nothing of the disease
at date of sale; a milkman is fined for selling
diluted milk, though he, too, swears he knew
nothing of the dilution; but a publican, proved
to have sold adulterated spirits, is i of fined, be-
cause he swears that ho knew nothing of thf
adulteration 1

Some W.C.T.U. shriekers called in on
Vic. Education Minister Peacock, the other day,
requesting that moi;e sp ice should be devoted in
the school reading-books to pointing out the evils
of intemperance, tobacco and opium-smoking, and
the use of chloral and chlorodyne, and that one of
their members should be placed on the committee
appointed to draw up the new series of books
That some girls, after they leave school, take to
opium and chloral and cigarettes is, no doubt,
true; the W.G.T.U. doesn’t seem to understand
that old Eden story about the joys of forbidden
fruit, and the inadvisability of showing youngsters
certain fruits’ and then forbidding them. Man
is born to evil, as the sparks fly upward, and
W.C.T.U. shriekers are just about the biggest
friends that evil has got.

The amateur Melb, J.P., like the amateur
jock., footballer, &c., makes a good living by
“going round ” —from court to court. He charges
“ expenses only ” —said charges being paid by the
solicitor of the man who hires his worship. As a
rule, he is a busted boomer or ex-politician, and
many of them haven’t worked for years.

A Swedish sailor tried, at Melbourne, to
smuggle ashore soap valued at Is. Bd., whereupon
Dogberry fined him .£lOO or a year’s gaol. The
sailorman hadn’t got the money in his pocket, so
reckoned he would take it out. The sentence was,
however, reviewed higher up, and the soap-
smuggler was let off with one month,
« # •
The J.P. again. According to a Bulletin
correspondent, there is, in the N.S.W. back-
blocks, a J.P. who manages two stations, on
each of which is employed an overseer. The
overseer of one of these stations lately proceeded
against two men for trespass. The manager had
the bad taste to sit on the Bench and bear the
cases, wherein defendants were fined .£2 or one
month. If the facts are as alleged, then that J.P.
was really hearing his own case. The Bulletin
advises its informant to write to the Minister of
Justice, at the same time communicating with
some M.P. who can be relied on to ventilate the
subject in the Assembly.

Every “ beak ” has his peculiarities, and it
has long been’a proverb in North Melbourne that
any woman having an affiliation grievance, who
takes up her residence there and issues a sum-
mons, has a ten-to-one chance of winning. No
P.M. has sat at North Melbourne Court for many
years.
# * *
While a certain northern Australian hos-
pital-election was proceeding last year, a clerk,
getting 50s. a week, asked his employer for a fort-
night’s leave, which he intended to spend exclu-
sively in promoting the candidature of a certain
medico. On the boss expressing surprise at the
clerk’s willingness to Jose a fortnight's wages, the
enthusiast explained that “ Doctor Soandso is a
sort of a relation of mine, you see. He had a
child by my wife before I married her.” And,
added the canvasser, “I think it’s every man’s
duty to help a relation.”

Dear Bulletin, —“ And honored well are
charms to sell if priests the selling do.” Jessie
Nichols, seemingly slain by the abortionist, adds
one more victim to civilised sham. If there is one
thing worse than aggressive vice, it is aggressive
virtue. The aggressive virtue of her own sex
drove the unhappy girl to her terrible doom. It
was from women’s scorn she fled —poor, foolish
girl, little reflecting that every day thousands of
Respectable women sell themselves at the altar for
various considerations, while but few give them-
selves away, like her, from excess of love.—Pad-
dington.
« * «
A vigorously-realistic artist raised his
voice in scornful laughter, the other day, because
a Sydney daily declined to print a sketch of his
showing the butchered remains of Jessie Nicholls.
“The finest thing,” he cried, “ever offered to a
penny paper! ”
* * «
Judge Hartley Williams (Vic.) has just
told a Sunday-afternoon audience as a solemn
truth that he knew a Melb. solicitor who used
every morning and evening of his life to pray to
Providence to send him business. The Judge
reasoned with him, but to no purpose. Likewise,
he knew a barrister who regularly prayed the
Almighty to send him lots of briefs. But the
supplicator who annexes the entire bakery is a
squatter who prays regularly that the Almighty
will strike all rabbits sterile. Then came, after
lots of laughter, this knockdown blow—“ And it’s
just the same with you people when you pray for
rain!” True: if rain is coming, it will come.
If it isn’t coming, you might as well pray for a
shower of iced whisky-and-soda.

Menzie, of the “ find ” near 90-Mile
(W.A.), was married at Perth, Aug. 28, to a
beauteous Weatralian maiden named Jerger,
daughter of a well-known Perth jeweller, one
of the promoters of Fraser’s South Mine. The
bridegroom is about 35, and the bride about 17.
Menzie is a thin, undersized man of the Austra-
lian bushman type, and is gilt-edged ; his dis-
coveries enriching him hy fully ,£OO,OOO.
The marriage of Miss Lena D’Arcy,
daughter of the Mount Morganaire, to Mr. S. T.
Hankey, 2nd Life Guards, was celebrated in
London on 20th July with much splendor, and
was attended by, amongst others, a strong detach-
ment of the R.C. nobility. There were nearly 300
wedding presents, including a silver-mounted
claret-jng from Prince Christian to the bride-
groom, and a scent-bottle with a miniature top
from Princess Edward of Saxe-Weimar to the
bride.

Should anything resembling an equitable
tariff-rate result, the adjustment of the M.L. mail-
traffic will he warmly welcomed by travellers, as
undercut fares spelt intolerable discomfort, especi-
ally between coastal ports. Also, the agreement
will minimise ocean-racing. A century of Wai-
rarapa warnings wouldn’t eradicate the average
skipper’s sporting instinct. When rival steamers
leave port in company, bound for the same desti-
nation, the stokers are hound to sweat for it.
* # *
S.A. Anglican Synod, seeking after a title
for benefioed clergy, wrestled long with “ incum-
bent” and “rector.” One pulpit humorist ob-
jected to “incumbent,” because it sounded so like
“income-bent.” In the end “rector” was adopted
by a big majority.

Miss MADGE ROSSELL, premiere danseuse of the Gaiety Co.
Goaded by the gorgeousness of the Brassey
reception proposals, a well-known Melb. entre-
preneur offered to provide suitable music on
H.E.’s arrival for a small matter of <£4oo. The
Govt, declined his offer, and a local musical society
lias now agreed to lay on the required warble for
nothing.
• # #
Divorce is commonest in cases where the
husband is between 25 and 30 at time of marriage,
and the wife either between 17 and 22 or between
25 and 30. The most divorces of all take place
where both parties at marriage were between 25
and 30. though wives of 19 have a fatal liking for
the process. ....
Among the women, divorces are com-
monest in descending order among housewives,
servants, dressmakers, barmaids, tailoresses,
governesses, actresses, milliners. So that bar-
maids and actresses are not the least moral classes;
lump the dressmakers, tailoresses, milliners — i.c.,
the factory class—and you see what happens to
those who “ go to business.”

N.S.W. has had three cases of “quar-
tettes ’’ up to date. In two cases there were three
girls to one boy ; in the third honors were even.
In 38 cases of triplets, however, the boys
won. Also, when twins are about the proba-
bility is one boy and one girl ; but when the sex
is the same it is safe to gamble on their girlhood.

The Wild-Cat Column .
Under the N.S.W. Land and Income-tax Bill,
church lands are wholly exempted from taxation. Also,
ohuruh revenues are exempted from income-tax., Can
anyone tell why, unless it he to secure the votes of the
pious element in the community? The value of property
in N.S.W. (a country in which Stale aid to religion has
ostensibly been abolished) belonging to the Anglican
Church alone-is sat down by Coghlan at £1,243,633, “ ex-
clusive of the value of glebe lands,” whatever they may
amount to. The Presbyterians own £402,000 worth of
property; the Wesleyan Church, £551,000 worth ; Con-
gregational Church, £201,000 worth; Baptist Union,
£65,000 ; Roman Catholic Church, not given, so
far ns the writer can find out, but probably
about £BOO,OOO, judging by the statistics available
when compared with those of the Anglican Church.
Altogether, allowing for the smaller sects, church pro-
perty in N.S.W. probably represents £3,500,000, and of
this it may be roughly estimated that one-half would
come under the operation of the Land-tax. If there is
any reason why all this mass of property should be ex-
empt, while that of life assurance societies, which mostly
represents the scanty provision made for the widow and
the orphan, is not—apart from the reason that the church
kicks 20 times harder about parting with its money than
any mere worldly instititution is capable of doing—it
ought to be explained forthwith. Soul-insurance societies
should pay their fair whack.

At a meeting of the Maoriland Midland Rail-
way Co., held recently in London, a number of people
complained bitterly of “ the methods pursued towards
those who were foolish enough to embark lh°ir capital in
a venture which was started for the benefit of a oreat
English colony." The spectacle of a crowd of British
capitalists professing that they started building a railway
from purely philanthropic motives and for the benefit of
somebody else is good. John Bull is about the only
person on earth who has the gall to say things like that.
* • * •
The Vic. Govt, claims that when a man’s
endowment-policy matures and he collects the money, it
forms part of his income for the year, and must pay
income-tax. Naturally, the taxpayer kicks violently.
When a man’s endowment policy matures it means that
he has paid his money into the insurance office for a
certain number of years, and has finally drawn it nut
with the accumulated interest. If he drew his accumu-
lated savings out of the savings bank it would be part of
his income for the year when he drew it out, in exactly
the same fashion. The Government also wants to charge
income-tax on legacies, and as these also pay legacy-duty
another strong objection arises. V\ hen the old man
dies, and leaves, perhaps, £5500 as his total assets, and
the law makes the widow pay sundry legal charges and
stamp-", and takes £B2 10s. for succession-duty, it seems
a brutal case of “ rubbing it in ” to also charge income-
tax on the whole £5500 on the ground that it is her
revenue for the year.

The usual pathetic story of the last man who
put in his money before the collapse turns up in con-
nection with the City of Melbourne Bank. An absent-
•nb ded individual, so the tale runs, who didn’t read the
papers and had no idea of what was going on, arrived just
as the directors in the back-room were deciding to give up
'he struggle, and paid in his cash. While he was paying
it in the order came to close np. An obliging messenger
postponed the closure for a few' seconds to let him pass
'mt, and then as he went down the steps the big front
d >or shut with a'orash and his deposit went into lianida-
"ion. Tie crash made him look hack, and the notice
pasted on the door explained the rest. Then he howled
through the keyhole for the return of his money, but
only a hollow echo answered him.

Q. Railway Commissioner Matbieson, who
“brooks, like the Turk (or the Scotchman) no brother
near the throne,” retained his £3OOO a year by three votes
only, when Q. Parliament discussed the matter the
other day. Matbieson, though a good man at small

that he defeated his own object.

A post has been found for stranded politician
Dlbbs in the managing trusteeship of N.S.W. Savings
Bank, left open by the recent death of Mr. Wise. The
hoard of trustees, who recommended the ex Premier’s
appointment, consists largely of personal friends, though
for that matter it would lie hard to find any public body
which does not consist largely of friends of the ex-
member for Tamvvorth. The salary is £1350 a year, and
Damchioago will doubtless settle into his snug billet with
alacrity—if allowed. For the Ex. Council has to confirm
the appointment, and it is hinted, at time of writing, that
this body will think twice about the matter in view of the
proposed amalgamation of N.S.W. and Post Office Savings
Banks, which will necessitate a re-casting of the whole
scheme of officials and salaries. The ex-Premier would
no doubt manage the hank well, but his appointment is
cruelly rough on the old officials, a steady-going and level-
headed lot, who had naturally hoped for and expected a
step up all round. The Australian politician is like the
great American Pie-biterwhen he reaches out for a pie
that is his pie.
• * * •
Further information by the mail shows that the
Bank of New Zealand figures were somewhat mixed up in
the original cable intelligence. As far as now appears
the shortage in the B.N.Z. Estates Co. is £911,678, and
there is a deficiency in the bank of £422,281, and a pro-
bable future deficiency of another £200,000; total
£1,533,979. Of this if is expected that £148.000 will be
recovered from tbe Auckland Agricultural Co., and the
balance will be made good by writing off the total paid-up
capital of the hank (£900,000) the reserve-fund (£45,000)
and all the proceeds (estimated at £450,000) of the’ call
made a few months ago. This will leave the B.N.Z. with
no capital whatever, except the £2,000,000 which it re-
cently borrowed on the State’s guarantee, so another
£500,000 is to be called np from the shareholders, and the
State is to take up £500,000 worth of preference shares,
carrying 3J per cent, dividends.
By way of ffivinpr the institution a frpsh start,
the Government undertakes to entrust to it all its London
financial business, estimated to be worth £14,000 a year.
Also, it is claimed that the B.N.Z. will save some £64 000
a year, which has hitherto been drained out of it by ’the
Estates Co. ; £lO,OOO by replacing its 5J per cent.
debentures by 4 per cents., and £15,000 by economies
in the management. The total net profits of the bank
Treasurer Ward estimates at £148,000 a year, which looks
rather too large to be true. Out of ibis £50,000 a year is
to be paid to the Estates Co. so long as may be necessary
to cover anticipated further losses on realisation, and the
property of the co. is to be sold with all possible speed.
This £50,000 is to be a first charge on the profits. Next
the shareholders come in for 5 per cent, dividends oil
their capital of £600,000, provided they pay up that
amount. Third, the Government apparently comes in for
8J per cent, interest on its £500,000 of preference shares,
though why they are called “preference” under such
circumstances is' ditncuit to understand. Any further
profits go to make up the expected losses in the Estates
Co., and if even this doesn’t cover the shortage the
Government guarantees payment of the balance. Under
these conditions the Estates Co. can never again wreck
the 8.N.Z., and when the former is finally wound up in a
year or two the incubus will be gone for good, and the
bank should have an excellent prospect before it. The
Government will probably purchase a lot of the Estates
Co.’s properties for set tlement purposes, and thus hasten
the winding-up. Also, if the present arrangement is
carried out, the B.N.Z. will probably buy up the Colonial,
the shareholders of which have already decided in favor
of amalgamation, and it will then have one of the largest
banking businesses in Australasia.
Meanwhile, according to cabled information, a
small shindy has arisen in London. The British holders
represent 63,000 shares out of 150,000, and some of them
how many doesn’t appear—are demanding liquidation.
Liquidation would certainly cost a great deal more than
fhe proposed reconstruction, expensive as the latter may
be, but the British shareholder—or part of him—appears
to be in an aggressive mood and wants Blood.
The balance-sheet for the year ending 31st
March, shows that the B.N.Z. has made a loss for the
year on its ordinary business of £37.356. One cause,
though a very small one, of this ruinous stale of things
is doubtless that clause in last year’s arrangement,
whereby one-half of the £2,000,000 raised under the State
guarantee had to be kept invested in Government
securities. At present rales this practically meant that
the bank borrowed a million at 4 per cent., and had to
' invest it at about 3£, and lose the difference. Even
allowing for this, however, the results are fully £73,000
worse than those shown for the previous year. The
deposits show a reduction of nearly £1,000,000 ; the note-
circulation declined by £47,000 ; hut the general business
of the bank seems to have been well-maintained, and the
report gives absolutely no information that is calculated
to throw any light on the causes of the loss.
• • • •
Something to think over. Considering that the
two Government ovethauls of the Bank of New Zealand,
which seems to have been as honest as the average, and
its parasite, the Estates Co., revealed shortages of nearly
a million and three-quaiters, and this after the bank had
twice written off large sums to cover losses, what might
happen if a similar overhaul took place of all the Austra-
lian banks, finance companies, and insurance societies?
The possible revelations are too vast for any ordinary
mind to take them in. The orthodox balance-sheet only
professes to show the state of things “ according to the
books,” and the B.N.Z. is a fair example of what, may
happen when the hooks are inspected by a thoroughly
independent authority. When is the State audit system
coming along ?
• * * •
Victoria, though such a huge exporter of pro-
duce, yet imports annually some £150,000 worth of butter,
cheese, and fruits (fresh, dri d, and preserved), Maori-
land imports fruits, fresh and preserved, to the value of
£125,000. Queensland imports cheese, milk, and fruit, to
the yearly tune of £40,000. But N.S.W. offends worst bv
importing no less than £730,000 worth of butter, cheese,
eggs, ham, bacon, lard, milk, honey, jam, jelly, and
vegetables.
» * • •
A clerk in a recently defunct financial concern
built another nest for himself when he smelt the coming
liquidation. This wise young man trotted round to
another (big, solid) bank one fine morning and asked the
manager to find him a place in fhe office on condition
that he (applicant) should bring 12 good accounts
along with him. His proposal was laid before the
hoard and approved, whereupon the 12 good account-
holders were interviewed by the of the crumb,
ling institution and easily persuaded to transfer their
business to the big hank, where the promised billet was
duly found for the introducer. A few days later fhe
other co. closed its doors, then the dozen new customers
shook hands with the new clerk across the counter.
• • * «
A Butr.KTiN correspondent:—Ramornie (N.S,
Wales) meat works are turning out over 100,0001 b. of meat
everyday. Nothing goes to waste at these works, save
the blood. The meat-extract alone, which used to go to
waste, brings in £2200 a we. k, more than enough to pay
all working expenses. Everything is done by contract-
killing, cutting-up, boiling, tinning, tin-mnking, case-
making, &c. The place is fitted with the electric-light,
and works night and day.
* * • •
N.S.W. bullocks in the form of tierces of beef
are now on their way to Siberia by the steamer Menmuir.
It is an experimental shipment, and the first, perhaps,
ever made from Australia to Vladivostok, the Russian
naval and penal station in Siberia. The port is closed
by ice from December to March, but the Sydney ship-
ment of beef will be there early next month.

A gentleman temporarily residing in a gaol
for fraudulent insolvency, says that, prior to his retire-
ment, he handed over some £lOOO of goods to a bogus pur-
chaser on the understanding that when he (vendor) had
done his lime his pal should ante-up half the proceeds of
the goods. Words fail to express his indignation at find-
ing that his alleged co-conspirator now threatens to put
him hack again for endeavoring to obtain his half-share
by false pretences.
* • * *
Frank Harley Davis’s calyx drill is doing big
work in Vic., where the Govt, has the right to use five
plants. It brings up a core, and in the hardest basalt
does its work at less than half ordinary cost. It promises
to revolutionise boring all over the world, and is described
by experts as one of the inventions of the age. According
to Vic. Government experience, it bores for 6s. a foot
where other methods cost 16s.

measures pints and stoppages, and is said to be almost a

* * • *
The power of Drink. During the construction
of the railway from l.ismore to Byron Bay (N.S.W.), one
man, who had taken out a wholesale license, sold 2300
casks of beer in less than 15 months, making a clear profit
of £2ooo—this not including what he made out of spirits,
etc. The beer was all sold by the cask, the vendor think-
ing it too much trouble to sell it two gallons at a time.
When the extension from the Bay to the Tw'eed was
begun the good thing had leaked out, and some sixteen
gentlemen, eager to make a fortune, forked out their
little £2O each. This, of course, cut the business up ; but
it was still good enough.
As the above line neared completion, a man
thought he saw a good thing in a pub. at the Five-mile,
but had no spare cash. He had some property, however,
and on this he borrowed £6O (locally) for license, etc.
From a brewing firm he got £350 cash to build a house,
and £2OO worth of stock. These amounts he agreed to
pay in three months. Wet weather and pay-day com-
bined to assist, and in ten days he paid every cent, he
had borrowed, paid for his stock, and had in hard cash
£257.
Those times, alas, have departed. Last week at
Buringbar, 13 miles from Murwillumbah, a two-storey
wooden hotel, with kitchen, two good tanks, range, a
large assembly hall and half acre of ground, situated at
the railway gates, sold for £2s—about the price of the
iron on the roof.

“Salvage goods from s.s. Orient” is the legend
on the windows of almost every second Sydney shop.
Which affords a startling proof of the enormous carrying
capacity of ocean-going steamers, and the popularity of
the Orient, in particular, as a freight-carrier.

Somewhere about the last but one of the Melb.
boomsters has been soused in bankruptcy. He repre-
sented a quarter of a million in balloony paper.
Broken Hill rejoices over improved prospects
for lead. A year ago the price fell to, say, £9 ss. ; and,
for the second half of ’94, the B.H. Proprietary Co. aver-
aged only £9 12s. To this it had fallen steadily from £l5,
in ’9O. Now, this persistent fall was the very thing that
was not expected ; it was confidently believed that, with
the reduction in the output of lead consequent on the
stoppage of silver-lead mines, especially in America, lead
prices, at any rate, would bound up. Siher, it was recog-
nised, was too heavily shackled wdlh accumulations. The
unexpected downward course was not checked till 10
months ago. Then the accumulation seems to have been
used up, and for the co.’s last half-year the price realised
averaged £9 18s.—an increase of 6s. on the previous half.
By the middle of July the quotation had got up to
£lO 12s. 6d. ; and immediately on the announcement of
the B.H. (ire it moved to £ll 2s. 6d. It steadied to £ll,
and is still quoted at that—" market firm.”
It is impossible to say just how much lead the
Broken Hill mines are producing at present. The bulk of
it is in the sulphides, which are exported either in the
shape of crude ore or as concentrates; and though they
may assay 70 per cent., the quantity that can be ex-
tracted profitably is uncertain. At a Very low estimate,
Broken Hill is now contributing to the lead-market no-
thing short of 2300 tons a week. The advance in lead
value is thus already worth over £4OOO a week to the
B.H. mines. Of this the Proprietary benefits to the
extent of only about £6OO.
It has been decided that Junction North (8.H.)
shares shall rise several shillings yet. The mine is merely
a circumstance. B.H. North promises to earn almost the
same sort of reputation. At the same time, steps are
being taken here to fairly develops the mine, and a small
concentrating mill may shortly be erected for the sul-
phides.
For the week ended Aug. 31, the output of ore
at Broken Hill was 15,000 tons, including over 8000 tons
of sulphides.
British B.H. Co. has had a fair income from the
rent tor its furnaces leased to the B.H. Proprietary. This,
however, may have an end. On past figures it certainly
was more economical for the Proprietary Co. to smelt at
the British, although paying rent, than at its own
furnaces; hence it was thought that, if any furnaces re-
mained idle, it would be the Proprietary’s own southern
nest. A change in the management of furnaces has
proved, though, that the difference in favor of the British
furnaces was more apparent than real; hence their pro-
bable relinquishment in the near future. Of course, to
the British Co. itself they are next to useless.
Another “Howell man” leaves the B.H. Pro-
prietary’s service—the general manager's secretary, who,
during his six years’ service, has had to do much of the
unpleasant work in trying limes.
Very appreciable quantities of mercury are
being found in the kaolin ore at Broken Hill.
During the last week in August Broken Hill
concentrating mills dealt with 5574 tons sulphides. The
plant now' being erected will allow of an increase of over
50 per cent, on this.
British B.H. reports weekly two or three
hundred tons of rich ore sent to Block 14 Co. ; most of
this is, however, ore that has been long stacked.
* • * •
Last week’s returns from the Junction concen-
trators were 588 tons sulphide treated for 140 tons first-
concentrates, the latter containing 42 pier cent, of
the silver and 55 of the lead in the crude ore. Some
more metal—how much unstated, but probably little-is
in second-grade concentrates and slimes. This is fair
work as concentrating goes at present—only about half
the silver and all of the zinc lost.
At some Broken Hill mines reporters are fur-
nished with the minutest particulars of the week's run at
the concentrators. At others they are told that the
machine has “ had a successful run,” that so many tons
ore were treated, and so many tons concentrates “ of fair
average grade” —or something of that sort —produced.
No use asking for fuller particulars. Hence the piebald
character of the published news of Broken Hill weekly
yields. The statistics of the field’s production official
included—are simply more or less accurate guesswork.
The Central smelters are not contributing much
to the mine’s profit. They will inevitably be closed when
the question of the mine’s sale is settled : the syndicate,
if it does buy, has its special sulphide process, while the
co., if the sale falls through, will erect concentrators.
Mt. Drysdale Proprietary is a min© that is
always tfoinff to strike the Drysdale rich formation, but
never gets there, and, from what the writer hears, Is
likely to be as far off in five years. Worst of all, the
manager reports: “The drive is In the kludeit-looking
country I have seen sines taking charge."

The W.A. mining regulations strictly insist on

the tonnage. In no ease has there been a prosecution

The Adelaide "Corner ” is now so crowded that
extra policemen have had to be put on to keep the
thoroughfare clear.
Melb. democrats seem beginning to stir
themselves, and with good results. A meeting to
protest agaihst sweating was convened in the
Town Hall the other evening, and the bebaubled
Mayor Snowden, who appears to have recently
declared that 4s. lid. a day was enough for a
man, horse and cart, had the icy gall to take the
chair. Then one .Fleming, “a welliknown agita-
tor,” arose and moved that, in view of his
published sentiments, Snowden was not a fit
person to preside, and the meeting hoorayed
enthusiastically, and carried the motion by a very
large majority. Then it was suggested that
Lormer, J.P., who has gained some temporary
notoriety by his tirade on the iniquities of the
nominee magistrate, should take the chair, but
both he and the Rev. Chas. Strong declined
on the ground that it would be discourteous to
Snowden—thereby showing that they cared much
less about protesting against sweating than about
wounding the finer feelings of an alleged apostle
of sweating. Neither Trenwith nor Deakin was
able to control the meeting, and eventually Snow-
den retired in disgust, and had the hall cleared by
police, in the true spirit of the bourgeois who can’t
thrust his cut-and-dried programme down people’s
throats. So the meeting walked out and held
itself in the open air, and bracketed Snowden and
sweating for the purpose of simultaneous com
d mu itiun.

See all the worn-out hammers, lying round
the anvil of Democracy.
Sydney is a dropsical pumpkin growing on
a dungheap of shoddy. To continue this condition
is the Foreign-trade policy.
“To find a parallel for the Mount Rennie
trial one must search the darkest and bloodiest
days of Irish history.”—Dalley.
Charles Lilley’s health is good and his
■ confidence is better. The old man will make a
strong bid for power next Q. elections.
The Reid policy is the policy of the cab-
man. “What’s your fare?” “ (Ji’ll lave it to
yerself, yer Anner.” The Cabman Cabinet.
The S.A. Govt, has instructed the Police
Commissioner to at once report to the Chief Secre-
tary the case of any State employe arrested or
summoned for drunkenness or any other offence.
Premier Turner and his team celebrate
their first anniversary of office this month. Their
hold on the Treasury benches is firmer than ever.
No Govt, of late years has given so much satisfac-
tion.
Woman-suffrage seems to stand a very fair
chance of early consideration by Vic. Assembly,
when one remembers that the handsome spouse
of Attorney-General Isaacs is one of its most
ardent advocates.

abolish the S.A. Parliamentary refreshment-bar.
It is pretty sure, however, that Wood, who, as a
speaker, is a far greater calamity than the Accursed
Drink, will be abolished himself next year.

They call the ten lately-appointed N.S.W.
Leg. Councillors “Moses Reid’s Ten Command-
ments.”
Apropos the shoddy revelations. It is pro-
posed in Victoria to introduce a Bill amending
the Seventh Commandment —“Thou shalt not com-
mit adulteration.” The present version has been
out of date since the passing of the Shiels Act.
The N.S.W. Labor-party needn’t be bull-
dozed by Premier Reid’s diplomatic shuffling on
the Windeyer question. If Reid chooses to make
the matter a party affair—that will be his funeral.
The even and dignified administration of justice
cannot be made a party question by any uncorrupt
Government.
Boodlewraith starts to portion out the
spoils of next election. Gentle “ Willie” Stephens
funks Woolloongabba, and will run with Midson
for South Brisbane. T. B. Stephens was a big
man 20 years ago, and his pachydermatous son,
without a trace of his ability, has travelled largely
on his name.
Both the Victorian and N.S.W. Land-tax
Bills contained a special tax on absentees, but
whereas the Vic. measure defined an absentee as
a person living outside Australia, the N.S.W.
one defines him as a person living outside of the
province. There is no beastly federal spirit about
G, H. Reid.
Sydney Telegraph ; “As a matter of
principle we have no more right to saddle our-
selves with posterity’s burdens than to saddle
posterity with our burdens.” The Telegraph is
evidently afraid that the people are going to rush
in and pay off the public debt, and it wants
them to go slow.
Gen. Tulloch (late Vic. commandant) on
Victorian Protection :
Wages are so low that 300 workers specially petitioned
for assistance to emigrate. . . . Some trades give
high wages—at the expense of the rest of the colony. ~
Won’t either of them suit? Can no good come
out of Nazareth ?
Treasurer Holder (S.A.) squares his finan-
cial statement this year by the simple device of
omitting to pay the annual instalment of .£60,000
due for the redemption of certain old loans.
Amount shunted on to posterity, as usual. The
average Treasurer ought to be re-christened the
Minister for Leaving Things till To-morrow.
The defeat of the prominent Assembly
debaters, at last N.S.W. election, has taken much
importance from the Lower House and transferred
it to the Upper, where recent debates have been
exceptionally strong. There are certainly no men
in the Lower House now of equal calibre with a
few in the Upper—such as R. E. O’Connor, Salo-
mons, M'Lanrin and Pilcher. This undue debat-
ing prominence of the Upper House and the
diminution of debating interest in the Assembly
have a tendency to place the statue where the
pedestal should be. Premier Reid has pulled
down the towering pillars of the Lower House
debate, such as Parkes and Wise, and left thereof
supported by nothing stronger than wind.
A SUBSTANTIAL DIFFERENCE.
Smoking carriage between Hay and Junee, N.S. W. Occupants: Tracy Backein
(bookmaker, late commercial-traveller), Harry Bagwell (commercial-
traveller), and elderly gentleman who snores.
HARRY BAGWELL ( suddenly interrupting the elderly gentleman) : “ How is it
you don't put up at Elvy’s now, Tracy?”
TRACEY BACKEIN ( indifferently, without looking up from his paper): “ Charge
too much, Harry.”
HARRY: “ I saw you having a barney with Elvy over your bill. What did
you do, Tracy? ”
TRACY: “ What could I do! Paid it.”
HARRY : “ No, but what took place ?”
TRACY ( impatiently): “What could take place? Elvy brought his bill. I
said, ‘That's wrong, Mr. Elvy—charge too much.' He said, ‘Too
much, Mr. Backein? There's wine, 7s. 6d., stout' - ‘ Don't
want to know what I've had to drink, thank you, Mr. Elvy.’ ‘ Well,
then there's washing, 3s. 6d.’ 'Yes,' I said, 'cheap, well-done. But
that’s not it, it's the total.’ ‘ Well, but so many days at 10s. a day
makes' - ‘THAT’s what's the matter,' I said. 'You always used
to charge me 8s. a day.’ 'Ah,’ he said, ‘but THEN you were a
‘ traveller,' now you're a gentleman, Mr. Backein.’ ‘Oh,’ I said,
‘it's going to cost me 14s. a week to be a gentleman.’ ”
HARRY: “ But I say, Tracy ”
TRACY : “ What, Harry?”
HARRY: “ Can't a 'traveller’ be a gentleman, Tracy?”
TRACY: “ They say not, Harry."
HARRY: “ No, but hang it ! what's the difference?”
TRACY: “ Two shillings, Harry."— (Retires into his newspaper for the rest of
the journey. Elderly gentleman snores.)
Fegan, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), asks the Govern-
ment to subsidise “ friendly and other societies.”
This is about the largest and wildest order on
record.

In the face of the recent disgraceful out-
rages at Maokay, Tozer had the cool cheek to say
the other day in Parliament that the only dis-
turbed district known to him was Flinders, where
the troubles were entirely of white origin. Tozer
has long ago recognised the value of the axiom :
“Truth is our most priceless possession; let us
economise it.”
“Johnny” Isaacs delights to assert hisforce
of character by voting agin brother Isaac Isaacs in
the Vic. Tariff divisions. But when the life of I.
Isaacs’ party hangs upon a vote or two, J. Isaacs
will get a stern command to follow the relative
who practically put him in his seat. No insubor-
dination is permitted in times of battle, murder,
or sudden death.
Yic. pietists are agitating with renewed
vigor for Bible lessons in State schools. “Children
in Victoria know much too little of the Word of
God,” says the spokesman of a recent reverend
deputation. The Minister did not make the
obvious reply that it was possible for them to
know a Lot too much. But sarcasm is absolutely
wasted on the clergy.
A young man in Melbourne answered an
advt. which appeared the other day, and which
was an offer to impart, in return for the modest
sura of 6d., reliable information as to a certain
and easy means of ranking a fortune without
capital to start upon. The information : “ Take
full charge and control of the Unemployed Move-
ment for the coming season.”
Could you possibly have a more thorough
demonstration of the futility of Chamber-
lain’s policy than the movement to exclude
Hindoos from Victoria? For, mind, Ghollah and
Chunder are our glorious and well-beloved fellow-
subjects of Her Cracious Majesty ; not aliens like
Hung Fat and Ski Hi. And here is J. Cham-
berlain stitching up Greater Britain into one
quilt.
Piddington, M.P. (N.S. W ) for Tam worth,
was a lecturer at Sydney University, and jiis
debating efforts, though promising, are given with
a strong Austrylian accent and in the moat
approved pedagogic style. He quotes, he refers,
he adverts, he reads extracts, he gives everybody’s
opinion but Piddington’s; meanwhile, he flour-
ishes a lead-pencil aloft in a most threatening
manner.
Kingsbury, M.L.A. (Q.), defines a vigor-
ous public works policy thus : “ A vigorous public
works policy means—borrow, bribe, boom, and
burst. It means corruption throughout the
length and breadth of Queensland, and political
railways that have not yet earned the cost of
maintenance.” Kingsbury ought to know : the
party of which he is a member pursued that policy
as long as it possibly could.
The little band of Yic. Freetrade members
fought desperately against every protective duty
on the alleged ground that it “raised prices to the
consumer.” But when the tea-duty, which isn’t
protective, came up they gave an almost solid vote
for its retention for the sake of revenue, and des-
pite the fact that it does, or ought to, raise price*
to the consumer. The Vic. Freetrade membe)
only worries about the consumer when he finds i
duty helping along a local industry and furnishing
employment. When it does none of these things,
the consumer can have prices raised upon him til
the Day of Judgment.

The £475 exemption in the N.S.W. Lane
tax Bill was re-afflrmed by 76 votes to 14. Th*
76 were a motley crowd consisting of men win
believed in the exemption, men who said the.'
didn’t believe in it but that they thought th*
country wanted it, and men who said frankly thav
they voted for it because they hoped and believer
it would damn the measure in the Council
Meanwhile the two Houses are in a curious post
tion. The President of the Council has decider
that the measure is a taxation bill, and can’t b*
amended in the Upper House ; the Speaker of tin
Assembly has decided that it is not a taxation
but only an assessment bill, and can be amendec
by the “ fossils.” And as the fossils badly warn
to alter it they will probably take Speake
Abbott’s ruling as decisive, and strike the exem;
tions out bodily. Then—Deadlock and Chaos.

victed, and when it became almost certain that
his sentence was wholly unjust, it cheerfully spem

conviction. But the idea of refunding to Dean oi

“justice.” Dean’s lawyers remain almost wholh
unpaid—as a warning to other lawyers not to inter
fere the next time an innocent man is convicted
and thus become a nuisance to influential pe)
sonages.

MELBOURNE MATTERS.


A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Moiled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture. all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, no tli 1 1 ie w a beautiful complexion of
peach an- 1 v t' e had by anyone who makes
a eontrnnie. T ncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
