Personal Items.
Mark Twain arrives in Sydney on Monday.
N.S.W. Postmaster-General Cook, having
changed the color of his own coat, is going to
change that of the postmen’s.
Ex-Gov. Carington signalised his rise in
the peerage by purchasing the famous Castle of
Gwydyr in the vale of the Conway River, Wales.
Ham, M.L.C. (Vic.), ran the first sausage-
machine in Ballarat. The diggers, full of con-
fidence, ate the sausages and gave him a good
atart.
George Theatre Royal Coppin is the only
clean-shaven Vic. M.L.C., beating Hon. James
Buchanan by a short “ sideboard ” for hirsute
virginity.
Melba’s papa, David Mitchell, is “run-
ning” the mild-cure bacon in Victoria. David
grows the Lilydale pigs that are being treated by
the new process.
Photo. by Freeman.
The late Mr. AUGUSTUS MORRIS, one of
the last surviving members of N.S. Wales
“Mixed Parliament.”
A certain re - elected Victorian Leg.
Councillors can barely write his own name, and
can’t read at all. However, he’s good enough for
the Leg. Council.
Saturday Review, reviewing H. Parkes’ B
senile sonnets: “Let us be thankful that Aus-
tralia boasts no ‘blood-stained Jeffreys.’” The
Saturday evidently doesn’t know everything.
The oldest aboriginal of Narandera
(N.S.W.) district, Jack Mack, died recently, aged
DO years. He was a total abstainer—the only nig.
teetotaller-on-principle The Bulletin ever heard
of.
A poet called the other day at the office of
an eminent Melb. solicitor supposed to be a
patron of Australian literature. To the huge
delight of the clerks, the first thing the poet did
after introducing himself was to put his hand in
his overcoat pooket, pull out a sock and com-
posedly wipe his rubicund proboscis therewith.
A personal illustration of the poetic foot.
Diana Baxter, last of
the Tooram tribe of blacks,
has died, aged 65, at Warr-
nambool. When Gov. Hope-
toun first visited Warrnam-
bool, Diana wandered in
while luncheon was on,
slapped H.E. on the back,
and remarked, “My boy,
here tiokpen for you.” The
Governor took it and gave
i hi- lady a sovereign in ex-
change, whereon she ex-
claimed, “God bless you, my
boy. ”
Salmon Wilson’s will
directed that all the furni-
ture, china, and pictures at his London house
should be sold, “ excepting the family portraits.”
Evidently the lineal descendant of Alfred the
Great had obtained an assortment of ancestral
“old masters ” from the Wardour-street dealers.
Strange to say. he didn’t purchase a family grave
in advance. “The testator bequeathed .£l5OO for
the erection of a vault and monument at such
cemetery or burial-ground as the executors may
think fit.”
Rev. F. Barclay, of Collingwood, Vic., is
a parson who has done some service to Demos
In England, some few years back, he was presi-
dent and active organiser of the successful London
busmen’s strike.
Again the clerical humorist! Rev. Phillips,
who escaped the Ku-Cheng slaughter, speaking of
those who didn’t: —“The great misfortune was
that only two of them were dressed ! ” “ Take
my life—but spare my collah !”
While Gov. Brassey’s son got a severe
drubbing at the British elections his brother,
Albert, managed to squeeze in for the first time.
He is the antithesis of his titled relative, being a
cast-iron Tory and an army man.
Dr. Alfred Austin Brown, just appointed
Vic. Govt, meat-expert, is a M.B. and M.Ch. of
Melb. He did all his medical work while earning
his bread as an official of Melb. G.P.O. ; in order
to attend lectures and hospital work in the day-
time, he always worked on the night-shift. A
man of such grit should be a square peg in a
square hole.
Amongst recent deaths reported from
England is that of Lady Lisgar, widow of Gov.
Young, of N.S. Wales (1861-’67). After the death
of Sir John Young, she married Sir Francis Tur-
ville, that Governor’s private secretary, who also
predeceased her.
Parkes lately told an interviewer that
once when very hard-up he discovered four £lO
notes between the leaves of a book in his library.
“But now,” said he, waving his hand towards the
shelves, “you would search long before you would
find £4O in them.”
The Potiphar case of the Melb. Law Courts
is burnt into the memory of middle-aged lawyers.
A ladies’ doctor was unsuccessfully sued for alleged
assault on the beautiful wife of an Anglican
clergyman. The action would never have been
brought but for his sneer—“ Potiphar’s wife.”
J. L. Purves will shortly repeat before the
Bar the sulphurous and variegated lecture which
he recently gave before the law students. Thus
Alma Mater —“The lecture took three hours to
deliver, yet there was not a single moment at
which the audience was bored.” What an appe-
tite for brimstone!
Police-Inspector Duffy, a veteran N.S.W.
officer, who emigrated to Victoria in the early
fifties, and was on duty at the Eureka Stockade
on the morning the rebellion broke out, has died
at Maitland. He it was who tracked down and
shot the murderer of Senior-Sergeant Wallings,
beyond Dubbo, in 1878.
A Sydney clergyman, high up in the
L.O.L’s., lately addressed a public meeting having
for its object the changing of the name of the suburb
of St, Peter’s. A Imost as amusing as the case of the
determined anti-R.G. who once declined to post
his English correspondence because it would be
carried by the s.s. Rome.
Coming men, like events, cast their
shadows before. The barque Mark Twain, from
Buenos Ayres to Melbourne, put in to Portland,
Vic., last week. She was much disabled through
the weather she had encountered. And that is
what is the matter with Mark Twain himself, who
comes to Australia with a very slim purse.
David Syme narrowly escaped becoming a
great explorer. Immediately after his arrival in
Victoria he advertised that he desired to join
some other person with a little money in making
a trip across the continent from Melbourne to
where Port Darwin now stands. He would long
have ante-dated the futile attempt of Burke and
Wills.
Judge Harding (Q.) died a disappointed
man. Sixteen years on the bench and the author
of erudite text-books, he hoped to be Chief-Jus-
tice, but saw the honor snatched by a needy poli-
tician. His irascible temper improved with age,
and his later life was free from such incidents as
his scenes with one Swanwick, whom he once sen-
tenced to 40 years for contempt. Score this to his
credit —he fought strongly throughout for the
better defence of prisoners.
Augustus Morris, ex-official-assignee, and
one of six remaining members of the N.S.W.
mixed Parliament of 1851-1856, died at Manly on
Aug. 29, aged 76. Few people thought him so
old. He was born in Van Diemen’s Land during
the reign of Col. Sorell (1820), and had all his life
been a well-respected and energetic citizen. But
five members of the Parliament in question now
remain —Henry Parkes, pensioner Merewether (a
nominee), Daniel Cooper, Charles Nicholson and
Saul Samuel, the latter four all resident in Eng-
land. At one time Mr. Morris was a very
wealthy pastoralist and most popular employer,
but he lost his all in the great pastoral crash and
had to seek an official aupointment in his old age.
His wife died a few weeks ago, and her demise
greatly affected the deceased. Mr. Morris was, in
many respects, an able man, and his memory
will be kept green as that of a cultured and
benevolent citizen of unimpeachable honor.

Knipe, an esteemed citizen, with an ineradicable
skein of harmless quackery. Contested numerous

to do it. Built Knipe’s Folly, a wonderful castel-

When Purves, Q.C., pleads before Chief
Justice Acting-Gov. Madden, people will be
always apt to remember that the Ciiief Justice is
more or less related to Surgeon O’Hara, and that
Madden’s brother-in-law, Frank Stephen, had a
row with Purves re the defeat of O’Hara (the
Madden family candidate) at a Melb, Hospital
election.
Melb. Paunch paints Reeves, the Maori-
land Minister, as one of a “narrow-minded and
uneducated set of legislators and as one who
through some extraordinary freak holds the
position of Minister of Education.” This non-
sense owes its birth to the Argus, which
ought to know better. As a fact, Reeves is a
University man, and a journalist of very high
capacity.

Solomon, M.P., blushed violently when
he rose in S. A. Assembly to take charge of “ The
Synagogue Bill.” He is the only avowed Jew in

Mr. C. Thackeray, promoter of the N.S.
Wales Fishermen’s Association, has been ap-
pointed superintendent of the Sydney Boys’
Brigade. It will be Mr. Thackeray’s constant
endeavor to teach the boys to speak ihe truth.
While the Fitchetts, Blanksbys, Garrards
and Curnows declared the other day that the
strongest of Australia’s religious efforts should be
in the direction of uniting the scattered faiths of
Methodism, Chief Justices Way and Darley ad-
vocated a union by all Protestant Churches, with
a notification that the R.C. Church must be com-
pelled to stand alone on the other side. In asso-
ciation with these it is well to recall that another
Chief Justice —Higmbotham—invoked Metho-
ists, Protestants of every description, and Romans
to come out of ecclesiastical Christianity and
unite in Deism.
A pitiful story re the last moments of
“ Salmon ” Wilson is going the rounds. The Earl
of Huntingdon married a daughter of the gilded
squatter, and after the wedding Wilson found that
there was a claimant of the Huntingdon earldom
—who, however, did not wish to contest a money-
less title —a “homeless coronet.” The discovery
that his daughter’s husband might not be the
“ Real Mackay ” chiefly caused the rich man’s
fatal illness. On his death-bed he sent for the
alleged claimant and asked him to promise that
he would do nothing to disturb the “man in
possession.” The promise was readily given, but
the claimant’s heirs may yet be heard from.
Had B. M. Clark pushed his Bradley’s
Head adjournment motion, in N.S.W. Assembly,
to a successful vote, Reid might possibly have re-
signed, as Parkes did in a similar case. Then would
have ensued as acrimonious and fratricidal a fight
for power as Australian politics have witnessed.
On going to the country, Parkes, Barton,
M'Millan, Bruce Smith, Dibbs, Wise, R. E.
O’Connor, Jacob, Crick, John Davies, and B. W.
O’Sullivan, with congenial sutlers, would fly one
flag, and M'Gowan, Brunker, Sleath, Reid, Pid-
dington, Angus Cameron, Want, and Garran,
also with congenial sutlers, the other. It didn’t
come to pass, for Clarke didn’t push a vote, out
Time has the occasion in store.
Melb. hasa Leg. Council election again after
many years. J. M. Davies —lawyer, pietist, land-
boomer, anti-gambler, social puritan, brother of
Matthew, &o. —opposed by lawyer Godfrey, whose
redeeming feature is advocacy of minimum-wage.
The Sargoods, Services, &c., are working hard for
Davies, whose seat is plainly in danger. Godfrey
is a sorb of Ishmaelite in all societies and might
make things lively in the sleepy Chamber.
The policy of the N.S.W. Justice Depart-
ment is to “ review ” in secret the Mount Rennie
case and quietly release the surviving prisoners—
thus getting rid of the agitation. The prisoners
must be released—the Department knows that.
So, to spare the Judge’s feelings, it proposes to
go through the farce of allowing one of its
luminous worms to crawl in the darkness across
the graves of the hanged, and label the journey an
“enquiry.”
According to the Queensland Sugar Works
Guarantee Act, Government may only guar-
antee debentures issued under a Central Mill sys-
tem. Q. Govt, has had the audacity to spend
public money in taking up the debentures of the
Marian Central Mill, an illegal proceeding- to
which the Auditor-General promptly drew atten-
tion. However, Attorney-General Byrnes had a
little retrospective amnesty-bill drawn out, and
things will soon be all right again.

The higher emotions get

It is related of million-
aire Tyson that a station-
manager once tried to take
solid advantage of the long
man’s misogyny. He sold
Mr. Tyson a large number of
sheep, the buyer reserving the
right to cull. The manager,
fearing that the millionaire
would get the best of the bar-
gain, invited several ladies to
be present. This was too much
for Tyson, who jumped on his
horse and left his overseer to
do the culling.
When the Earl of Yar-
mouth was visiting the Hope-
touns in the Vic. western dis-
trict, the giddy young thing
one day astonished the local
coachdriver by springing on to
the back of one of the leaders
while the coach (loaded with
passengers) was travelling
rapidly; the horse also ex-
pressed surprise, and soon
landed the flighty youth on
the roadside, where he fell
heavily on his head, and
emitted a fervent “ Oh, h— ! ”
“Yes, that’s the place you’ll
be goin’ to one of these davs,
my boy,” said the driver.
Higgins, member for Geelong (Vic.) wants
the tea duty abolished. “I ask nothing that
could in any way embarrass Ministers,” said
Higgins. “But they may easily consent to remit
the tea duty. It will not cost more than .£lOO,OOO
a year.” Then Minister Best, to whom £5 of
Customs revenue is a matter of life or death now,
tried to get up and reply, But his legs gave way
under him, and he fell back into his seat.
That Freetrade is synonymous with cheap-
ness is again demonstra'ed in Melbourne. This
time in the case of cream-separators. One machine
which costs less than .£lB abroad and is duty-free
is sold in Melbourne by philanthropic importers
for <£4o. Other importers’ monopolies, like sew-
ing-machines and bicycles, are also about 150 per
cent, above ’Ome prices; but the Arous points
triumphantly to tea as a proof that importers do
not combine to bleed the people.
It is an open secret that W. M‘Millan,
M.P., is not likely to prove an ardent supporter
of the present N.S.W. Govt. He is a close friend
of Bruce Smith and B. R. Wise, and thinks the
latter has been treated very shabbily by Premier
RGd. He is also averse to the proposed Upper
House reform, and is indignant at the scant
courtesy shown to Railway Commissioner Eddy
in regard to his promised increase of salary. By
the way, M’Millan and Eddy went to, and re-
turned from England together.

“ Are you going to vote by your fore-
man?” was the contemptuous question put by a
British peer on a memorable accession to the
newly-appointed members of his order. Queen
Anne had created 12 new peers with the object
of getting a particular Bill through the Lords, and
on the measures coming to a vote the ancient
fogies taunted the devoted 12 to the brink of
desperation. Reid’s 10 plus two are about in the
same fix in N.S.W. Legislative Council, and the
historical parallel is in many respects complete.
In taunts, at all events, the Council fogies will
hold their end up.

“Banjo’s” forthcoming volume will be

In the mail to Captain’s Flat. Three
classes. First class ride all the way. Second,
walk up hills. Third, get out and push up the
pinches. The above is the only method of getting
to the end of the journey, when feed is scarce.
Prices, 10s.; 7s. 6d.; and ss. No ladies or children
taken third class. No cripples carried second or
third class.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Dear Bulletin, —I met in Melb., the
other day, a Coolgardie miner—a grizzled, old
fellow with a face burnt to the hue of a fire-brick
and huge, hairy hands. I wanted him to tell me
about some of his experiences—which I had heard
were uncommonly eurious—in Westralia, but he
preferred to read me some of his poems in MS.
They were all about girls with golden hair, and
eyes so blue, and lips so sweet, and hearts so true,
and similar callow sentimentalities. It was very
funny, but I dared not laugh—the man was so
intensely in earnest. Seeing that he seemed to
be interested in that sort of thing, I asked him
what he thought of certain pieces of verse by
Lawson and others, published in The Bulletin—
which he said he took regularly. His reply
astounded me. Folding up his MSS. and replac-
ing them in his pocket, he remarked slowly, “ I
know nothing about ’em. I never read anything
in The Bulletin but the‘Wild Cat’column.”—
Crekve Roe.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
Dear Bulletin,— .Re your par. as to
Premier Turner’s billycock. The Opposition
might subscribe to buy him a new one, as sug-
gested, but they could not find enough amongst
them to induce him to wear it. That hat and the
ancient brown ulster (both used constantly for
years) not only reconcile him to the democratic
section of St. Kilda electorate, ns “ one of them-
selves,” but create a distinct individuality for
their wearer—and the cute Premier knows it! He
is one of the very few men in Melbourne who
appreciate the value of eccentricity in dress
(especially headgear). Many Melbourne men’s
prominence is largely due to their hats. Look,
for instance, at Broken Hill Knox’s broad-
brimmed semi-conical billycock! No self-
respecting tramp would accept it as a gift.
Yet, every street-boy in the cir.y knows
that magnate as “ ’im with the ’at!” (Mr. Knox’s
shape is patented for his sole use). The form of
Judge Moles worth’s cady is even more daring and
original than his recent utterances. Dr. Strong’s
reverend topper is sired by a bishop’s, the dam
being a quaker’s broad-brim. “ Auster ” Sharp,
the boss Vic. timber-merchant, sports a buff
“ blood-basin ” 3£in. in height with a brim 18in.
wide (absolutely unique). Every Melb. man
knows Hugh Peck’s squat, curly-brimmed half-
belltopper hat, although no one knows that Hugh
has ever done anything to bring himse f into
particular prominence. What Southerner cannot
recall C. B. Fisher’s eccentric grey billy-cock, or
fail to recognise it as sole-surviving brother to
starter Watson’s ? Ex Postmaster-General Der-
ham’s hat is undoubtedly bred from Knox’s,
although it is not nearly so upstanding as its sire;
nevertheless, Mr. Derham’s “roof’’does more to
popularise his biscuits than any number of advts.
Every man, indeed, can recall ancient memories
of others who would be utterly forgotten but for
the quaintness of their head-gear. It’s said that
no businessman except a millionaire can afford to
wear a shabby or out-of-date hat in London ; but,
if a man wants to get known in Melbourne, he
should consistently wear an original shape— say, a
rough beaver or a Welsh sugar-loaf, for instance.
That man is sure to get. on. “By his hat shall ye
know him.”— Arthur Robinson.
When Charles Dickens edited the Daily
News, the proofs were fetched in to him by a
livened footman, on a salver !
The London find N.VV. railway has, at
Crewe, a special factory of wooden legs for its
employes. Jfut never a director patronises it.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Within the past 13 years the Melbourne Cup
has been won eight times by horses handicapped at from
7»t. lllb. to Bst. 51b.—a martin of 81b. only. There were
two winners each at 7st. 1311)., Bst. 31b., and Bst. 41b. ; one
at 7st. llln., and one »t Bst. 51b. Tills year there is
nothing at Bst. Sib. or 7«i. 1111)., but the Bst. 51l>. and 7st.
131 b. horses include Bruin, Ascot Vale, Aureus, Dream-
land, Brilliant, Laundress, Sainfoin, Bradfoid, Nuda, The
Trier, Hortensius, and Royal Master.
• • • •
Not one of the last four Melb. Cup winners has
put a race to bis credit since taking the big pool. Only
°!l!i (Patron) is entered again this year, and the
odds against Patron’s dicky foreleg coining to the post
are fully 5 to 1.
r • * • •
The Tasmanian “knowing ones’’ swear that
Music will make them dance to an unexpected tune in the
Melb. Cup. Music is one of the original eight-stoners,
along with Newman, Nobleman, Taranaki, Burton, and
Alpine. The Tasmanian tips, however, are seldom worth
much at Flemington.
• ♦ ♦ ♦
W. R. Wilson expects that every ticket in the
St. Alban’s “Distribution” will be sold by the date of
drawing-Oct. 24. It seems possible that the eleventh-
hour applicants will find themselves shut out, judging
from George Adams’ experiences with ids money sweeps.
Every holder of one or more shares in the St.
Alhan’s lottery wishes Wilson a splendid run of luck at
the finish. When you have made up j our mind to win
four horses in training, as well as the*estate, with a £5
investment, you naturally' want those animals to come
into your possession covered with glory. If any of Wil-
son’s distinguish themselves at Randwick a whole mob of
sanguine owners-to-be will feel that their properly has
improved in value.
* • • «
A Melb. parson last week remarked that
Gambling in aid of a church is very different from
gambling on the racecourse.” The Bulletin s colonial
oath ! One could never get 30 members at a shilling to
go for a i,T-note on the course, and then offer the winner
a kiss to be allowed to raffle it again.
♦ * * •
A Melb. bookie’s yarn. “Last year, when I
was betting on the two Cups, I left my blue pencil at
home one day, and bought a black one. I only laid one
double that afternoon, and it was Paris and Patron. This
year I wouldn’t write a ticket in black for a fiver."
• • ♦ •
In South Australia betting on racecourses is
illegal, but the man who lays lotalisator odds can’t be in-
terfered with because the courts have declared that he is
only betting on commission, and that betting on commis-
sion isn’t betting. The courts don’t profess to decide
what it is ; they merely decide what it isn’t, so the law is
in a state of temporary paralysis. It would simplify things
a lot if the bench, when it decides that something isn’t
the tiling it seems to be, were required at the same time
to explain what thing it really is, so that the law will have
a chance of knowing where he are, in a manner of speak-
ing. It seems safe to assume that every thing is some-
tiling’, so this request isn’t unreasonable. Moreover, it
would be interesting to know why betting on commission
isn t betting. The judges ought to know.
* * * *
A novel but feasible system of preventing the
nnging-in of Australian trotters in Maoriland is sug-
gested by a Wellington sport, who lias got tired of inves-
tigating piotests T 6 strange-looking prads usually
bought for a couple of quid from a Maori.” His idea is
to brand maiden trotters by' tatooing the gums of the
teeth, a registered code of marts making identification
clear. Tampering with the brands would render the
horse liable to be laid aside for life. A unique proposal
with little pain to the horse. r ’
* • • •
The comely Sheet Anchor horse, Duhallow,
winner of a Vic. suburban race last week, will now likelv
be the Davis stable selection for Caulfield Cup in prefer-
ence to Damper, who has lately failed publicly ’ when the
cash was up.

Last year a Q. man worker! “ the race-game ”
(toy horses going round-tbe winner takes the pool) on
the racecourse, and got “ run in,” machine and all. Next
day be pleaded that the same game was play ed in aid of
the churches, &c.—lint Hie J’s.P. indignantly sentenced
linn and confiscated bis machine. At, the Govt, auction a
chinch member bought that machine, cheap, and now
works it (at least, bis pretty daughter does) all round the
different church bazaars, with great success to the church
and himself.

As the hard-going at Randwick has prevented
many trainers from sending their nags along, many
animals will strip at this meeting not thoroughly wound
up. Still good sport should be seen, as contingents
from M.L., Vic., and Queensland have arrived to measure
strides with the N.S.W. cracks.
In the Spring Stakes (l|m.) the meeting of St.
Hippo, Allas, Delaware, Newman, and Portsea or Patron
at w.f.a, should prove exciting. The M.L. horse should
he capable of overcoming all opposition, though the best
of the Victorians may give him trouble. Delaware wants
more time, and will he seen to better advantage in Melb.
Cup. The Craven Plate should also fall to Bt. Hippo if
he can beat Georgie.
In the Epsom Handicap most of the candidates
carry their owners’ coin and confidence. Nobleman has
been sharpened up considerably of late, and is now in
fair condition, so this unlucky horse has a good chance of
capturing a rich prize. Euroka and Alcmeiia may turn
out his most dangerous opponents. The flying Georgie
must run well, hut she is virtually handicapped at 51h.
over w.f.a., whilst Nobleman has a drop of 7lh. ; so, on
their All-age Stakes running at R.mdwick Autumn Meet-
ing, Nobleman would appear to have a bit the hestcf it.
In very slow time Sol an urn won the County
Purse at the recent Hawkeshury Club Meeting, and, even
with the 7H). penalty which that victory entails, he is a
prominent favorite for Epsom Handicap ; hut, being far
from ripe, a fast-run mile may not he to his liking. Fit
and well at Caulfield Cup-time, he should be near winning.

a three-year-old, pulled off the two events carrying 7sl.
81h. and Bst. 121 b. If Paris is all right and is worth his
passage-money he should go and do likewise.
Now his luck has turned for the better, Mr S

may he expected to run well. Tills colt should manage
to win a good race shortly.
Little Oxide has undergone a good preparation
for the Metrop., and may here recompense Mr. Walter
Hall for all his previous disappointments. It is bruited
that Mahee will he in commission for this race : if so he
will at least nearly win.
Bob Ray and Mannlicher are the great public
fancies for the Derby', whilst of the Vic, representatives

and Blue Cap, one of which three most likely will win the

The Melb. Bicycle Club’s Austral meeting,
next Nov., will be a big event, with its Austral Wheel
Race first prize of £2OO, and second of £4O. Zimmer-
man will be there, and Iwo or three dark horses who
know as much about the science of training and racing as
Zimmerman does. And that is saying everything.
Privately cabled that Zimmerman has sailed for
Australia in the Oruba ; he will, therefore, arrive in time
to witness the early October meeting in Adelaide. Zim.
wdl Probably race first in Sydney. His mile (paced by a
quad.’ ), in Imin. 57 4-sth sec., shows his recent grand
form ; natheless, many cyclists sav he will meet his man
in Megson, and Megson himself, perhaps the last athlete
to blow, claims to have ridden a mile, at Adelaide, under
2nnn. Time records are imperfect guides, hut in the
present cases they should indicate at least some sort of a
struggle between Zim. and Megson, each having proved
that he can race both against time and men.
* ♦ ♦ *
The bicycling craze in Melbourne has a lotto do
with the depression in the tramway receipts. Recently
the tramway co. decided to investigate the matter, and
stationed a man at Princes Bridge to count the cvolists
coming mto business on the spidery wheel. Between 8
and 9.30 a.m. he reckoned SSO sprinting in from the
Southern suburbs alone. This probably represented
some 400 threepences, or £5 in hard cash, lost to the
tramways, for the Melbournian is not a walking animal
by nature—at least the appearance of his legs doesn’t
suggest it. °

Mr. and Mrs. Maddock passed through Taree at
10 a.m. yesterday en route to Brisbane. They would
reach Port Macquarie about 8.30 p.m., after stopping
three hours at Coopernook to overhaul Mrs. Haddock’s
machine.
Presently, when the gathering cycle-boom eomes along in
earnest, every well-advertised person on wheels will be
accompamed by two special reporters. “Mr. and Mrs.
Maddock stop three hours at Coopernook.” Marvellous -

* * • *
Australian wheelists who have been ’Ome sav
that the racing methods there are quite different to ours
Sprinting is practised as a fine art, and the real racing is
done in the last two laps, the earlier part of the race living
a mere crawl. Zimmerman is great at this game, but he
may hit a snag if he carries it too far, as some of our
stayers make speed from the start.

There was a varied programme at Sydney
Cricket Ground last Saturday. Some Y.M.C.A. persons
clad in spotless white, gave a mediocre gymnastic display
—only remarkable as dispelling the general illusion that
no saint is ever muscular. Among other things they
swung clubs lo music, squatted tailor-fashion upon the
grass, tied themselves in ugly knots, and sat straddle-
legs across parallel bars to be photo'd. The lily-white
cycle amateurs, who are above racing for cash generally
showed that they were also above racing for honor aiid
the one feature of an otherwise funereal display was the
brilliant riding of Macdonald. Paced by a tandem hi the
Five-miles Championship of N.S.W., he gradually saw all
hut three of his competitors give up, and for the last six
laps he raced alone against the previous time record
vvli'le second and third men struggled hopelessly behind'
At the finish he gave the previous record a last grand
spurt and won in 12min. 57 4-ssec. ; the best five-mile
cycle time yet done on Sydney track.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,
round the ankle DU ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread hy placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

ALCOHOLISM.
rpHIS terrible disease of drink, contracted throne I
i excessive sociability, deprives the best of mm
of all respect and reason ; it leads them to acts ol
indecency and crime. Alcohol weakens the mind
in subjection to the awful Ckavk, or morbid desire
for stimulants. The unknown impulse drives the
inebriate to often drink what his palate may even
repel, yet he cannot resist, though he knows him-
self to be drifting to Utter Ruin. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless.
The only hope in this condition is to arrest the
crave, eradicate the accumulated alcoholic poison,
and to re-invigorate the nervous and general sys-
tem—to give the subject his self-control and inhi-
bition. Conscious victims—or their friends—
should not delay investigating the marvellous
results achieved by the ORIENT INSTITUTE
for ALCOHOLISM. During the past 18 months
many prominent and valuable men have been
restored to health, Sobriety, and Position in
Life. Inebriates of the most sceptical nature
have been amazed at their restoration.
Cure for Inebriety has been established beyond
all doubt. The U.S. Government has introduced
it into the army, and already numerous States have
legislated for the medical treatment of inebriates.

Should Fitzroy gain Yio. football premiership
for '95, it will be for the first time. They ran up to
Essendon in '92, and were third in ’9l.
* * • «
Fitzroy’a draw with Essendon on Saturday,
5 goals 5 behinds to 5 goals 7 behinds, marks a big quality
decline in this season’s football. Essendon won on
the play, such as it was. but Fitzroy’s performance, if
executed three seasons ago, would rank it somewhere
towards the bottom of the senior list.
The draw between Fitzroy and Essendon leaves
the Vic. football premiership in an interesting position,
as should Fitzroy be beaten to-day (14th), and Geelong
win they tie for first place and will have to play off.
’ * • • »
The Victorian “ national game ” now stinks,
and its moat ardent supporters will he glad when the
season is over. The in-and-outing of some of the leading
clubs puts even horse-racing to the blush. In one recent
match the goal-keeper couldn’t kick a goal, and the crack
Rover couldn’t hold the leather. But the following
week 1 You should have just seen them.

over during the above matches, they might combine and
elect their own manager. Anyhow, they will have a big

W. G. Grace’s ninth century this present season
in South v. North England the other day will tiring his
average up a little. Four innings for a total of 21 in the
last week of July lowered him to 61-7. Ranjitsinghji was
then second with 59-2.

They say that Turner may remain in England
after the coming tour.

“As a matter of fact, I question whether
Richardson is not more deadly on a medium heavy
wicket than on a fiery one. They don’t spin over the
wickets so frequently,” says an English cricket reporter.
Rather late in the day to make that discovery. Richard-
son on a dull Australian wicket—in the second test-match
—was much more difficult than at any other time during
the tour.

Joe Goddard’s projected fight with Owen Sul-
livan, in South Africa, shortly, shows how Goddard has
deteriorated, although the men fought II rounds six years
ago. Foley says that Goddard’s constitution was broken
by dissipation ; but for that Goddard, with Ins then
acquired cleverness, his fierce courage, and terrific natural
fighting instincts, would have been to-day the world’s
champion.
* • ♦ *
Jimmy Antony, the plucky and clever lad who
downed everything at his weight in Australia, has been
conquering the Pacific Slope. Since landing in ’Frisco
five months back he has fought tour battles, in one of
which he knocked out the previous Pacific champion. He
now goes East in search of worlds to conquer. Antony was
brought out as a boxer at Melb. Democratic Chib, where
he was affectionately known as “ Cleopatra’s bloke.”
» * * *
Walter MTndoe, one-time champion swimmer
of Australia, has turned up in Omeo, en route to Cool-
■'ardie—where he won’t have much chance of giving
natatory exhibitions. He has been track cutting for the
Vio. Mines Department between the Limestone and the
rise of the Murrav. He declares he can still race a hun-
dred yards with the best of them ; and as he is looking
well and muscular, is only 35, and in excellent wind, he
probably could.
Syd. Gregory, one of the most dexterous of
living batsmen, has established in Sydney a cricket and
sports depot. Australia never had a more faithful Repre-
sentative upon the field than “ Syd.,” who, with his Aus-
tralian and English experience, is a reliable authority on
everything connected with cricket. His English goods
are all picked by Albert Ward, who has, by the way, been
hatting better than his late captain, Stoddart, this Eng-
lish season.*

Premier Turner’s lawyer Ministry, enquir-
ing into the question of Melb. police-court
“shamelessness,” will not betaken very seriously.
Some of the most suspected magistrates are re-
garded as particular admirers of the Government’s
legal partners. For instance, their seeming bias
in favor of Turner’s own partner, Sam Lyons, is
a common allegation, and a standing argument
against the admission of shoals of attorneys into
the Legislature. But the modern history of
Smellbourne’s tribunals, from the Supreme Court
downwards, bristles with that argument.

THE UNKNOWN LAND.
There’s a land of the emu and rabbit—
The Australia of Londoners’ tales—
Which no woman, it seems, will inhabit)
For we never hear tell but of males
Who eternally sit
Round a fire and spit,
And their narrative force never fails.
Those who found out that place have decided
And carefully noted it down
That the land into two is divided.
And everything’s “ bush ” but “ the town.''
And they speak with a hush
Of this terrible bush
Where Nature does nothing but “ frown.”
There the scene always matches the story,
For Art unto Nature has neared ;
“Lurid lights to the south ” for the gory,
And “gums grey and gaunt” for the weird.
And the children of toil
Fairly reek of the soil,
And their faces by summer are seared.
Yet under the trees, where it’s shady,
And the thickest of grass ever grows,
Heroes dream of some sweet English lady,
And sniff at the red native rose ;
And not one of the things
Which creep ever stings,
And never the blow-fly blows.
Sydney.
J.P.
Another Case of “Didn’t Know it was
Loaded.”
“Yes, that is so,” said Mr. Robert Grant, of
Sydenham, New Zealand. I never dreamed that
my system was loaded with uric acid until tor-
tured with muscular rhematism. I caught a
severe cold, which culminated in rheumatism in
the feet and legs, and from this time I was subject
to frequent attacks of rheumatism, which were so
bad as to lay me up, and as all the medicines and
applications failed to relieve or cure me, I had
about resigned myself to perpetual lameness and
pain. But, fortunately, I finally tried Warner’s
Safe Cure and Pills. In a few weeks the swelling
and pain lessened. When I had taken seven
bottles the pains had entirely left, and I have en-
joyed good health ever since. I can highly
recommend Warner’s Safe Cure to anyone suffer-
ing from rheumatism.”
It is unwise to wait for such serious develop-
ments, but as soon as you notice the slightest
deviation from good health, have recourse to
Warner’s Safe Cure, which will soon drive this
subtle poison—uric acid—from the system.
At the Florence-Moody-Hambourg Concert
Company’s matinee, in Melbourne, last Wednes-
day week, the exceedingly pretty dresses and
stylish hats worn by these distinguished artistes
were supplied by Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and
Sons. Miss Florence’s gown was of pretty spotted
muslin, elaborated with pink, and trimmed with
quantities of soft, foamy lace —the tout ensemble
being very effective. A picture-hat of black lace
and pink straw, trimmed with large straw bow
and ostrich plumes, completed a most becoming
toilette. Miss Moody also wore a muslin costume
of exquisite taste and perfect fit, relieved with
heliotrope ribbons and lace. The head-dress con-
sisted of soft Leghorn, richly trimmed with
ostrich plumes and handsome bows of black satin
ribbon.*
Nathan Herman’s New Premises.
Nathan Herman, the progressive merchant of 9
Wynyard-street, city, promises to transform that
unobtrusive and quiet thoroughfare into one of the
busiest spots in the heart of the city. The ware-
house has been renovated and enlarged, and
recently Messrs. Rowe and Spain, architects,
formally handed the new premises over to Mr.
Herman. A reporter called upon the enterprising
proprietor the other day, just in time to view a
choice shipment of elegant and up-to-date goods,
of which the latest fashions in dress materials,
beautiful in texture and design, were perhaps the
most striking. The labor of transferring the
stock to the'new departments has teen successfully
accomplished by the large staff which was engaged
when the business attained its present proportions.
The basement has been set apart principally for
bedsteads, and the magnificent lighting arrange-
ments, combined with fine display, render a visit
a pleasure as well as a speculative venture. On
the first floor are all classes of carpets, linoleums
and oilcloths, including genuine Brussels carpets
of undoubted value, which are cut and laid for
buyers at the astonishing price of 2s. a yard. The
famous teas (30s. per chest of about 401 b.), which
are packed on the same floor, are meeting with a
remarkable sale. It Would be tedious to dilate at
length upon the surprises of which a visit is pro-
ductive ; suffice it to say that the new premises
are worthy of the stock, and that the stock is
worthy of a continuance of public patronage. All
household furniture and other requirements, from
the simplest lines to handsome pianos, are offered
at prices which must be experienced to be compre-
hended. The boot department has already created a
stir among the firm’s customers, and this section of
the misfit parlor is one of its bulwarks. The pro-
prietor prides himself upon the excellent watches
which are sold, with a chain attached, at ss. lid.
and 7s. fid. Country residents are receiving these
delightful little timekeepers by post. Mr. Her-
man’s £2 parcel is the choicest fruit of his misfit
parlor. It contains : —Two outer shirts, two
undershirts, six pair socks, two neckties, two
collars, six handkerchiefs, one set of studs, a hat,
one pair braces, and a pair of boots, and, crowning
all, a complete suit in excellent material. Men-
tion of Mr. Herman is never complete without a
reference to his superb iron safes, which have
made a name for themselves throughout Aus-
tralia. Tobacco and cigars are also two of the
choicest bargains which the firm supplies.*
THE WOOL TRADE.
The question of economy in handling and selling
wool is one that affects a very large number of our
people. The system pursued by each woolbroker
of keeping separate stores, carting and re-carting
produce to and from the centre of the city, neces-
sarily entails much expense. All this, in some
form or other, has to be paid for by the producer.
In an article written by the National Wool Com-
pany, of Melbourne, it is proposed to erect cen-
tral stores outside the city proper, and in touch
with the River Yarra and the railways. If some
of the other brokers would join in the enterprise it
would be a great step towards securing economy
and despatch, and should receive encouragement
from the wool-growers.*

Messrs. W, 0. YUILLE & CO.’S CERTIFICATE.
We hereby certify that we sold on account of Mr. John Ckoziek the St. Alban’s Estate to Mr.
W. R. Wilson on March 30, 1890, for the sum ot .£70,000 cash. At this time the property was very
lightly stocked. Wo have examined Mr. Wilson’s books, and certify further that blood stock to the
value (actual cost) of £32,041 has since been added to those originally purchased. Further, that the
sales of St. Alban’s Yearlings conducted by us during the seasons 1890-1-2-3 and 1894 realised a
total of 33,841 guineas, or an average of 210 guineas each.
Bourke-street, Melbourne. (Signed) W. O. Yuille & Co.

Number of Prizes as under—l3o
I.—THE ST. ALBAN S ESTATE, con
sisting of 842 acres, situated 3| miles east of
Geelong, Victoria, Australia. The property is
divided into 30 paddocks, many with live hedge
fences.
As a Breeding and Racing Establishment it is
questionable if any more complete place exists in
Europe; certainly it has no rival in Australia.
Originally selected and laid out by Mr. James
Wilson, one of the keenest judges in the colonies,
money has continued to bo freely expended on
improving and perfecting it by Mr. W. R.
Wilson, its present owner, so that it is now one
of the show places of Victoria,
The land is extremely fertile and sound, nearly
all the paddocks being laid down in English
grasses. The Barwon River (2 chains wide and
20ft. deep) intersects the property, thus ensuring
a never-failing supply of fresh water, alike for use
and irrigation. The system for the latter is per-
fect, the drains being most extensive, and the
steam-pumping plant has a capacity of 120,000
gallons per hour. The rainfall is, however, so
good that it is rarely necessary to irrigate more
than two or three paddocks during a summer.
The plantations and hedges, of which several
miles exist, add to the usefulness and beauty of
the estate in a marked degree.
Shelter Sheds of large capacity are placed in
many of the paddocks, while the Yearling
Quarters consist of 40 loose boxes, cool and well
ventilated, built in quadrangular form, and of
ample size. Stone and brick tanks, windmills,
and feed-bins are placed in convenient places in
the various paddocks, and the yards, &c., are
ample and perfectly arranged.
In the centre of the Estate the Training Track
and Course proper are laid out (1J miles and I
mile 5 furlongs round respectively). Both may
well be compared to Flemington or Randwick,
the going being good in all weathers.
The Scraping Sheds are most suitably placed,
and an Elevated Stand commands a most perfect
view of the tracks, river, and estate generally,
with the far-famed Barrabool Hills in the dis-
tance.
The Stalliob Boxes are lofty and well-designed,
opening into a large covefb'd-in exercise ground.
Feed rooms, men’s rooms, &c., are under the
lame roof. The Training Stables are perfect, and
jontain 15 loose boxes, feed rooms, men’s rooms,
and every necessary adjunct to a first-class estab-
ment. Two cottages for stable boys are in close
proximity, and the out-buildings are extensive
and ample.
THE HOUSE—Brick and of modern design,
jonsists of about 30 rooms, and is a perfectly
ippointed gentleman’s residence, beautifully situa-
ted, having a superb view of the You Yang
Ranges and Corio Bay, the latter only two miles
distant.

The Princess of Wales’ best-looking
daughter bestrides a two-wheeler. -This faot
can’t he mentioned too often just now, seeing that
dozens of damsels who are neither princesses nor
pretty, ride hikes in order to get themselves pho-
tographed as something special.
Purists in Melbourne object to a foundling-
ho-pital on the ground that it might promote im-
morality. Its absence promotes murder. Mrs.
Grundy —she is the murderer. But the Social
Gimlet gang do not tin ill much over a mere
murder. A foundling hospital is a charity that
would cover a multitude of sins which it is the
purpose of the Gimletits to discover.
Chief Justice Madden, Yic., on a default-
ing lawyer: “It is a plain, obvious, criminal
offence. Of course, there can be no other remedy
than to strike this man off the rolls.’’ Struck off
accordingly. Happily, there is always another
and much more emphatic legal remedy for
Buggins, the laborer, when he embezzles a shoul-
der of mutton or lifts a pair of 4s. lid. water-
tights.
Ex-Treasurer Carter (Vic.) complains in
the House that the rising generation doesn’t stick
out its chest properly, and walks with a stoop,
and carries its hands happing weakly in front of
it like the paws of a kangaroo. When he was
young, says this elderly wine and spirit importer,
he was taught to throw out ids chest properly and
Carry his hands with the little finger parallel to the
seam of his pants. All the old men in the House
have big chests, while the young ones —Shiels, for
example—are thin and weedy, and have a feeble
stoop. Carter probably doesn’t make allowance
for the fact that Shiels grew thin and hollow with
his efforts to straighten the finances after the fine,
generous, big-chested old men had tied them up
in a hard knot and left them that way. Ihe
damage that has been done by these big-chested
old men will take a lot of repairing.
At a recent Northam (W.A.) suicide in-
quest one “ Arnold Beard, a youth aged 12 years,
was called, but owing to the incompleteness of bis
knowlege of God and eternity, his evidence was
deemed inadmissible.” So Westralian papers
gravely report. The wisest on earth know of
God and eternity—absolutely nothing ; and, as
Arnold is on a level with the wisest on earth, Ins
evidence was inadmissible. But if Arnold had
learnt the glib cant of the Sunday-school, and
could describe an anthropomorphic deity a mile
high who put little boys into a lake of tire when
they told stories, then Arnold’s evidence wmild
have been accepted without any question. This
paper is somewhat comforted, however, to observe
that the jury’s verdict was “ That on the morning
of July 4th deceased, William Frederick Kadow,
came to his death from the effects of a pistol
bullet wound inflicted by his own hand, whereby
he did feloniously kill himself.” The most sen-
sible suicide verdict The Bulletin remembers.
The Argus, naturally enough, has a pro-
found admiration for London PUNCH, which is
vaguely understood to share with the Bible the
responsibilities of England’s greatness. Says our
contemporary :
That a funny paper can be both humorous and gentle-
manly has been abundantly shown for these 40 years and
more by the London Punch.
There would be no disputing this but for the fact
that Punch is not a funny paper, and is not both
humorous and gentlemanly, as witness its pro-
found contempt for the working-man and the
“lower orders/’ generally. The Argus persists:
PcNCH S humor has never been of a kind to encourage
suspicion, distrust, and cynicism.
That The Bulletin will swear. Its alleged
humor has encouraged nothing but a spirit of
investigation.
Some picturesque stories are told about
the recent great frost in Maoriland, At Kurow,
all the mineral-water bottles in the pub. blew
their corks out with the expansion of the con-
tents. Then a column of the water would fly up-
wards, and, freezing in the air, would remain fixed
like a candle, with the cork on top of it. Most of
the other liquors exploded, and a drink
was left iu Eurow or its vicinity. The meat
froze hard, and so did the whiskers of the resi-
dent*, and the rivers were blocked with ice, and
the thermometer at South Wairarapa fell to 10
degrees below zero. At Benmore, according to
a picturesque local liar, the sheep became so
famished that they assailed anybody who ap-
proached them and ate great pieces of his clothes
off him, thereby compelling him to run for his life
across the snow in a state of partial nakedness.
Everywhere, the very oldest inhabitant lias risen
up and stated, without reservation, that he never
knew anything like it, and he is cordially backed
up by those other two unreliable authorities, the
very old settler and the respected fellow-towns-
man. All three, however, are dying off rapidly
from rheumatism, and, unless the weather mode-
rates, there will hardly be an oldest inhabitant
left.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Hassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.

The Helmsman .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was his spell at the wheel, and he stood with
his hands carelessly holding the spokes. The
old barque steered easily, and, as she was going
free, a touch either way kept her on her course.
It was Sunday night; dark, cold, axxd threaten-
ing wind. The sea lifted with an uneasy motion,
and the dirty craft rolled and groaned with a
motion still more uneasy. She was only 200 tons
burden —a mere speck on the big, black sea. The
helmsman was alone on deck. Bill and Charley
were asleep in the forecastle. Jack, his watch
mate, was dozing in the cook’s galley, and the
mate was down in his cabin soaking rum.
The helmsman felt that drowsiness which comes
to one when rocked and rolled in the darkness of
night. The splash of the water, the moan of the
wind, the clang of the iron sheets, and the groan-
ing of the masts, as they swung loosely between
the stays, seemed to blend altogether in one
dreary tune. *
The helmsman was thinking of Sunday night at
home, and saw his people sitting in a comfortab'e
room singing ana talking. The Jain beat softly
against his buck ana t ickled down his neck. He
haa neither oilskins nor sou’-weste , not even a
change of cloi hes, for he was a hard-up sailo".
The wind began to hum Lh ough tne rigging,
the ba que heeled over just a little more, but the
change was »o grauuai that the helmsman d.d not
notice it. Suddenly a voice shouted in his ear,
“ Whei-e the h —ll are you see ing? F 1 rouse
you up. Go aloft and stow tne topgallant sail-;
I’ll take the helm. Let go the halliaids and clew
up. ‘
The helmsman gaspx d at the order. The top-
gallant sail was as much as two men could . tow ;
but he oocytd o der- instinctively. He let. o the
h Jiiards, clewed up the sail, and went up aloft to
stow it. He forgot to haul in the braces wnich
hao slackened with the lowiring of the yard.
Up he went on to the bulwarks, into the main
rigging, up the loose xattlinsto the main top, then
over the rim into the topgallant rigging ; swinging
to and fro in the darkness, clinging to shrouds and
stays. He reached the topgallant yard. The sail
was blown out like a balloon and flapped madly in
the wind. The folds of canvas struck against his
face, enveloped him, tore away from him. Ho
leaned over the yard, grasped the sail in his hands,
hauled in the loose folds bit by bit. A gust
came and snatched the sail from his gi’asp.
He struggled with it again, drew folds of it
across the yard, pressed against the folds
with his chest gathered in more, crushed all
the wind out of the canvas, hugged it limp
against the m.ist, triumphed. Now he had to
secure it. He made the bunt fast, then laid out
on the yard to pass the gasket round the sail to
leeward. His only foothold was a swinging rope,
his only finger-hold a thin iron jackstay running
along the yard, so close to the yard that only his
finger-tips could grip it. He shuttled along the
rope, loosened the gasket, and the long coils of
this rope fell from his grasp and blew foul of some
rigging. He curled his left fingers against the
jackstay, groped in the darkness for the gasket,
found it, puiled at it. It was fast somewhere.
He tugged at it, gave a fiercer tug. Horror! his
feet slipped off the hard, wet rope, the yard swung
seaward, and he was dangling over the water,
holding only with the Angers of his left hand. Ho
struggled desperately to reach the yard with his
right, groped madly with his legs to find the foot-
rope, failed, and dangled over black space.
He could not cry out—fear and the awful
struggle for life stopped his breath. He caught a
fi asb of the home scene, then the yard went
S ? awai f * withaJerk ’ kia clenched Angers slipped
along the wet iron and he was thrown out, down,
His head crashed against the main yard, and the
shock flung him further out. His body dropped
with a thud into the cold, dark water, a crop of
! jl ° od recldeuc(l lhe foam , crest of a wave > but the
helmsman was dead, sinking slowly through black
depth 3 of brine> do^ n> dow “
The irate captain at the wheel looked upward
at the darkness. “Damn you!’’ he shouted;
“how much longer are you going to be furling
that sail? Aie you asleep up there ?'’
Ashburton, Maoriland.
F. ROLLITT.
"Te Arero Maori.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was on one of my visits as a newspaper corres-
pondent to the interior of the North Island of
Maoriland that the idea of a Maori newspaper
was suggested to me. At the time the Maoris
had plenty of money, derived from the sale of
their lands ; and native friends had no difficulty in
I ersuading me that there was a “good thing in
it.” The Maoris, it was argued, were a distinct
people, with a distinct destiny; and they had
ambitions and aspirations, to voice which a news-
paper was essential. How otherwise could they
become a political force and dictate to the Govt. ?
How spread the principles of temperance and
religion? Clearly, a newspaper was the road to
greatness. And where temperance and religion
were to be the guiding principles, did 1 not possess
exceptional qualities for the conduct of the enter-
prise ? That settled it.
Our first difficulty was the naming of the paper.
A meeting of the proprietors was convened, and
we debated the subject at length. Someone sug-
gested Te Iti ro (the Bird) as a suitable title.
Another thought Taiwara (the Fish) would be
appropriate for a temperance organ. Te Rama
(the Torch) was also put forward. Then Te Pono
(the Truth) was urged as a suitable title for a
newspaper to make the Government tremble. Old
Te Whakawatu, who had subscribed a large por-
tion of the capital, wanted the paper written in a
comic vein. He therefore suggested that we
should call it Te Whakakoekoe (or the Tickler).
This, ho we vex’, did not find favor with one of the
younger men of the tribe, who had t een educated
at a Methodist College, and wanted the title to be
Te Tetere (the Trumpet). Finally, I carried the
day with Te Arero Maori (the Tongue of the
Maori).
Having called up all the available capital, we
cast about for a suitable plant. One of our pro-
prietors proposed to walk in a body to the nearest
European settlement and forcibly carry off the
first newspaper plant we could find. After I had
explained the difficulties attending such an enter-
prise, this idea was reluctantly abandoned. In
the course of a month suitable offices were erected,
plant was secured (and paid for), and Te Arero
was sent upon its mission ; or, as our chairman of
directors, Mr. Nihotunga (Mr. Badtooth) put it,
in his very best metaphor, our “ little canoe was
launched upon the lake of peace.”
After the second number appeared I began to
feel rny responsibility. Three Maori candidates
were standing for one of the Parliamentary divi-
sions, and the board and I sat down one evening
to discuss which should have the support and
influence of “The Tongue.” We unfortunately
failed to agree. The proprietors were divided
into three equal sections, and each section « anted
to boom its own particular candidate, la the end
the matter was left to rny own judgment.
The partisanship which divided the proprietors
soon betrayed itself among the staff. I had the
assistance of an interpreter, a couple of half-caste
compositors and a Maori machinist (who had been
taught the printing art at a missionary station in
the south), besides two or three brown boys who
gave their services in exchange for the privilege
of being connected with the press. My knowledge
of the language being limited, I had to trust
largely to my interpreter, a half-breed and a
bad lot. Everything written for the paper had to
be translated into Maori by this fellow, and he
was not slow to realise the delicate character of
his position, and the possibilities for mischief
which it afforded.
When the election campaign began I wrote an
article singing the praises of Te Pakurangi, one
of the candidates, and urging his claims upon the
Maori electors. A new appointment had been
made to the Native Lands Court bench about this
time, and this was the subject of some flattering
references in the same issue ; the new Judge being
one of my personal friends. The “ staff” not only
objected to my espousal of the claims of Te Paku-
rangi for a seat in Parliament; they also dis-
approved of my complimentary references to the
new Judge. Their disapproval distressed me less
than their method of demonstrating it. For now
I began to appreciate the vicissitudes of aboriginal
journalism.
As I sat alone in my sanctum one day, mentally
complimenting myself on the appearance of the
issue of the Tongue which had just been circu-
lated, my attention was arrested by warlike sounds
at a distance, which seemed to approach nearer and
nearer. Going outside to see what was the
matter, I beheld advancing from a neighboring
hill a band of naked, yelling savages equipped as
if for battle. Puzzled as to what this demonstra-
tion meant, I looked about for a member of my
staff to explain—and it suddenly dawned upon me
that I had not seen any of them since morning.
My interpreter had been missing for two days.
As the warlike party approached I felt re-
assured to see that my friend Pakurangi was at
the head of it. He was waving over his head a
copy of my paper. Clearly he had come to congratu-
late me On my article commending his candidature.
Yet there was something aoout him and his fol-
lowers that did not convey the impression that
their errand was a peaceful one. I was at a loss
how to take them. But they did not long leave
me in doubt.
When they came within fifty yards of the office
of Te Arero, they halted and went through a
performance which to me appeared to be nothing
less serious than a declaration of war. Each man
was armed, and the whole body danced with a
vigor that made the earth tremble.
“Friends,” I said, drawing upon the little
Maori I knew, “ what is it you want of me ? Do
I indeed see the spirit of anger in the eyes of my
brothers ? ”
Te Pakurangi advanced and danced round me
in a paroxysm of indignation. Then he broke into
speech :
“ Brothers? You call us ‘ brothers,’ you white
devil! Brothers, indeed ! Listen to him, Spirit
of my Fathers I ‘Brothers!* Look at that, yon
pale-faced traitor,” and his rigorous frame
trembled with fury as he flung in my face a copy
of the paper.
Clearly there was something in that paper which
Pakurangi did not like, and he had called i ound
in his own simple way to demand an apology. His
feelings were certainly hurt.
“ Friend,” I remonstrated, putting up my hand,
“ Listen, I would speak with you.”
“Speak not, you pakeha dog. Talk to the
winds if you will. Out of the way ! ”
Within five minutes the office of the Maori
newspaper Tb Arkro was levelled with the ground,
the type was scattered to the winds, and the press
rolled into a neighboiing water-hole. The editor
alone was spared.
The “Tongue” which was to speak for the
Maoris had ceased to wag.
That evening I took a copy of the paper to an
interpreter to find out what gave so much offence
to Pakurangi and his people. My suspicions were
confirmed. My staff had sold me cruelly,
Friend Pakurangi was referred to as “a dog ”
and “a bandit,” a man whose hands were “stained
with the blood of the innocenta “brainless
toady,” and the “offspring of a man-eater.” It
was further asserted that he had been in gaol, and
now wanted to get into Parliament to hide his
sins, while a little couplet I had written about his
noble origin was twisted in such a fashion as to
imply a most atrocious libel on the virtue of his
mother. The article I had devoted to congratu-
lating the new Judge was similarly mutilated,
His Honor was referred to as “ a law peddler,”
who sold his judgments, “a cheater of the widow
and orphan,” and a “ hireling crusader against
Truth and Justice.” In another part of the
paper an esteemed missionary was accused of
living in illicit relations with a dark-skinned
beauty; and the Native Minister of the day was
directly warned against “ bartering Government
patronage for the maiden tribute of the Maori
Babylon.”
I did not wait to formally tender my resigna-
tion as editor of Te Akero Maori, but left at
once for another district. No other journalist has
since attempted to supply a “long-felt want” in
that neighborhood.
J. F. EDGAR.
An assault-case recently tried in Melbourne
ia a fair sample of what a harassed judge and jury
have to struggle with when a hysterical female, or
two of them, get into the witness-box. It began
by a “neatly-dressed girl” giving evidence that
her father had assaulted her. Then she asserted
that he hadn’t assaulted her, but that she had
been driven to say that he had, because a police-
man threatened to have her horsewhipped unless
s he gave evidence to that effect. The policeman
climbed into the box and emphatically denied this
charge. The girl’s sister swore in the first
place that she had seen the assault. Then she
stated that she hadn’t seen it, but her aunt had
told her to say she had. Next she declared
that her aunt hadn’t told her to say anything of
the kind, but a young man had scared her into
saying that the aunt had told her to say some-
thing to that effect. The young man got into the
box just here and denied the charge, and sundry
witnesses testified that he wasn’t present on the
occasion when the threats were said to have been
used. The jury here gave the case up as a had
job, and the Judge, whose hair was grow ing white
beneath the strain, postponed the trial to »" n w
the witnesses time to sort themselves out.

Nell’s Letter .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had been standing at the open door
since her brother’s early breakfast. The bieak-
fast-tliings were still on the table, just as Bob had
left them ; but her own tea was now cold, and her
bread and bacon remained untasted. Before her
lay the dreary panorama of ridge and gully,
clothed with poverty-stricken gum-trees, which is
common to the northern slopes of the Blue Moun-
tains. The persistent monotony of the sky-line
was broken only by the barren effort of Mount
Hay towards individuality; and at this distant
land-mark Nell was frowning through the sun-
light, her eyes set in an unseeing stare.
She was thinking about a letter. It was not
one she had received, lor she had never received a
letter in her life ; but one she felt she must write.
Was it not nearly six months since Jim had gone
away, yet no word had come to her of his doings
or intentions ? It was nearly summer again, and
she had told herself all along that with the
summer Jim would certainly return. Not that
she blamed Jim; for it was absurd to suppose
that Jim could know of her trouble, seeing that
when he went away she did not know of it herself.
She passed her hand wearily over her brow, fur
she had been thinking deeply, and she was not
used to thinking. She felt that the letter would
be difficult to write. Of course it was for Jim—
her hero, Jim—who never laughed at her blunders ;
yet even with Jim it would be hard to choose her
words. For Nell, though simple, was not stupid.
She was instinctively modest; and then she knew
she must not hurt Jim’s feelings, or say anything
that might be taken as an appeal to his generosity.
Because Jim was a gentleman, a real gentleman,
whose words or actions musf never be called in
question.
So, having thought it all out in her poor little
way, Nell went inside and cleared away thebreak-
fast-things. Then she took the buttle of violet
ink, and the pink paper with the blue lines, and
the ornamental pen with the impossible point.
She spread them laboriously before her on tlie
table, and sat down with her back to the light to
accomplish an act of common justice to her lover.
Pausing olten for inspiration, she tried hard with
her stiff little fingers to keep the wayward,
spluttering pen within the limits ruled for it.
l)ip succeeded dip in the narrow-necked bottle,
blush followed blush over her freckled face, sheet
after sheet of the pink paper was spoilt and
thrown aside; and before the task was done the
afternoon was well-advanced, and the writer was
nearly exhausted. When her name was duly
signed she rose and stretched her limbs, cramped
by the unaccustomed labor. As she moved about
the room, preparing for what should have been
her mid-day meal, she was able to look back upon
her efforts with something approaching compla-
cency ; having, as she hoped, explained the situa-
tion without affront either to Jim or to herself.
When she had taken some tea and food, she
drew from her pocket a crumpled old envelope.
It contained a collar-stud and other trifles, relics
of Jim, and bore the address ;
Mr. JajiesO’Lyon,
Post Office,
Cook a ware a.
This envelope she had found on the flour after one
of Jim’s visits. She had preserved it as a keep-
sake ; but now it was the only link between her
narrow life and the great outside world into which
her lover hud withdrawn.
So Nell copied the address upon a tinted
envelope, tidied her wavy hair and set out with
her letter to the town, carrying an old shawl over
her arm, though it was not cold and the sun was
still high. It u as with a feeling more nearly akin
to happiness than she had enjoyed for some time,
that Nell followed the winding track from the
selection to the town and back again. 1 hat night
she prepared her brother’s tea and endured bis ill-
humor with comparative serenity, comforted by
the thought that everything would soon be right.
* • •
Six weeks had passed, yet no answer had
come from Jim. in spite of herself, Nell was
losing heart. Since the despatch of her letter,
she had walked daily to the post-office to enquire
for an answer; trudging the three miles and
a-half into the town with faint hopes, and the
three miles and a'-half back again with ever-
deepening despair. At first, on tine days, the
walk Was not unpleasant; but, as weeks went by
and the sun grew hotter, the exertion began to
tell upon her. Her head was always dizzy, and
sleeplessness was wearing her down. There came
a day when she fainted on the road, and lay some
time unconscious. She crawled home with diffi-
culty, and next morning she felt too weak to rise
from bed.
So far, she had succeeded in hiding her trouble
from her brother ; but in her weakness and help-
lessness she made up her mind to face the worst,
and tell Boh all about it. So the following morning,
when Bob was breakfasting in moody silence, she
commenced her faltering tale. It was some time
before her brother grasped the situation; but
when, at length, he did understand, he vented his
passion in a torrent of abuse. He even struck
her; for brutal and vulgar minds, when by
accident they find themselves on the side of
respectability, forego none of the privileges of the
position. When he thought his taunts had begun
to lose their sting he left her. On his return at
night the house was empty. Nell was gone.
» • •
The train sped on through the night. It
was just daylight when Nell, shaken and feverish,
left the carriage at Cookawarra. Bor many hours
she had not tasted food ; her head throbbed ; and
she could scarcely stand. The station-master
noticed her. “You seem ill, miss,” he said;
“ would you like to sit down in the waiting-room
for a few minutes? ” „
Nell thanked him, and turned to follow, then
tottered and fell. When she came to, she was
lying on a seat in the waiting-room, and the
station-master was holding a glass of water to her
lips. “Are you better now?”, he asked kindly.
“ Yes, thank you,” she said feebly. He left her
for a moment, and returned, saying, ‘ bit still
awhile, and rest yourself. I’ve brought you the
paper to look at.” She thanked him wearily and
sat up. He went out. closing the door behind
him, and when he had gone Nell took up the
paper, and almost immediately her eye met this
paragraph :
Caught at Last.—When going to press we were
credibly informed that, owing to the praiseworthy
endeavors of Constable Bolger, the notorious swindb r
James O Lvon has at last been brought within reach of
the law. He is now in the lock-up, and will he brought
up at the police-court this morning. A full account of
the proceedings will appear in our next issue.
With a terrible jump at her heart, Nell realised
what this meant to her. She was helpless, home*
less, almost penniless, and her darling Jim was in
prison, charged with—she knew not what—some
terrible mistake—of course, the charge was false.
But how lung would it take for Jim to clear him-
self ? And what should she do in the meantime?
Instantly she made up her mind to go straight to
the lock-up, and to see Jim, if only for a minute.
Boor old J nn 1
So she enquired her way from the friendly
stationmaster, and slowly walked down the quiet
street, gazing absently about her. At last she
reached the lock-up. It stood by itself on the
other side of the town—a rough, wooden building,
bare and forbidding. A short distance away was
the constable’s house, enclosed within a neat fence
along which honeysuckle grew thickly. Nell
walked boldly in anil knocked at the door. No
one answered; r.nd she knocked again. Still there
was no answer, and Nell bethought herself that it
was hardly past five o’clock. If she disturbed
these people they would be offended, and she must
avoid giving offence if she wished to see Jim. She
walked aimles-ly out through the gate, and round
the corner of the honeysuckle hedge, casting many
wistful glances at the lock-up. She would sit
down and rest —her head ached so. She heard
the distant jangling of cow-bells, the cry of mag-
pies, and all the sounds of early morning, while
from the honeysuckle overhead came a perfume
which she inhaled greedily. Then a drowsiness
came over her, and she slept.
* * *
Constable Bolger had been obliged to post-
pone the official overhauling of the prisoner’s
person, and it was about eight o’clock in the
morning when he came out of the lock-up with a
letter in his hand. It was, of course, the con-
stable’s duty to read over any papers found on a
prisoner, so, bracing himself to carry out the work
he had so well begun, he seated himself upon a
comfortable garden chair beneath the honeysuckle
hedge. Having carefully lighted his pipe and
spread out his feet to the sun, he devoted his
attention to the letter, which was queerly written
on pink paper with blue lines, in wayward violet
characters.
It is certainly a very funny composi-
tion, for Constable Bolger rolls about on bis seat
as though a tit were imminent. Now he has read
it through, and looks round for someone with
whom to share his enjoyment. Surely that is
Banks—there, by the lamp post! “ Holloa there,
Banks ! ” roars Bolger. “ Coe-e-e ! Come here;
I want you.”
Banks seems deaf, and the constable roars
again. On the otlu r side of the hedge a tired girl
opens her eyes, and tries to rise. But her head is
whirling—she seems to have no power over her
limbs —she will lie down again—in a little while
she will feel better.
“ Now, then, just listen to this ! ’ chucKles the
constable when bis friend is seated comfortably
beside him, “Listen to this! ‘My own, dear,
beautiful Jim.’ Eh? All right; it’s only the
cows. Never knew Jim O’Lyon did you, Banka .
‘My own, dear, beautiful Jim.’ That’s him in the
lock-up now. He’s a dear, beautiful daisy is Jim ;
but wait. * I hope this finds you in as good health
as it leaves me at present.’ Hope it does, so
help me three buck niggers ! ‘ Since yon went
away it has been cold mostly. O, Jim, what
beautiful days them was before you went away.’
My colonial troubles! Them was eight-hour
days, them was! ‘ I’m not so happy as I was,
because Bob does go on so with drinking and swear-
ing and such like, and there ain’t no one to say
what you used to say.’ No; strike me ugly if
they can any of ’em put it so genteel as the
irresistible Jimmie I ‘I lay awake at nights
thinking of you know who.’ ‘O, my oath, Jim
knows who right enough I ‘0 Jim, if you was
here I could tell you, hut writing ain’t the same,
somehow.* Ha, ha, ha ! Why, what’s the
matter, man? What? Somebody cry out?
\Vby, it’s only them cows laughin’. It ..would
make a snake laugh 1 Sit down. It’s nothing !
Listen ! ‘ But I’ve told no one that you love me.’
James has her on a bit of whipcord this time, and
no mistake. Just listen here! ‘O, Jim,, some-
times I think what mother would have said, but
if she had known you she would say it was all
right, ’cause you are a gentleman and knows best.
But, 0 Jim, do come and see me again in summer
same as you did before.’ Not quite. Probably
James will have other things to do this summer I
‘ With heaps of kisses.’ Rows of ’em ; just look I
What! Going? Well, so long 1 ” And Con-
stable Bolger went inside.
• * •
On the other side of the fence a worn,
pitiful figure lies still, very still. The cows are
beginning to feel the growing power of the sun,
and graze closer in the shadow of the honeysuckle.
One great brindled beast comes and sniffs at the
sleeping figure of the girl, then calmly crops a
tuft of grass which her head is almost touching.
She does not heed it; the hoarse, hollow sound of
the bell upon its neck does not seem to disturb
her slumber.
She lies so very still.
W. B. YOUNG.
