OF OMAR KHAYYAM.
The announcement that George Meredith
ottered his maiden speech at the last meeting of
the Omar Khayyam club, awoke (it is to be feared)
in few Australian bosoms a responsive thrill.
Conan Doyle we know, and Anthony Hope ;
of “Ships that Pass” and “Trilby” we can
prattle with assurance; but George Meredith,
“ the greatest living master of English fiction
how rare his disciples in the booksellers' flock !
As for Omar Khayyam—ask for him at the
counter, and observe the more than Egyptian
darkness which shrouds the bland assistant’s
phiz ! Yet among all the guides, philosophers
and friends that Man has vouchsafed unto men—
not forgetting Solomon, and his brother Ecclesi-
astes, and his cousin Eeclesiasticus (a most admir-
able-foolish cuss); not forgetting Horace, and
Sterne, and Montaigne, and the grave Antoninus
—there is none like unto Omar—none !
Meredith is “ tough ” —to the flippant literary
slap-dasher. Maybe. So is Henry George (not his
sentences, but his ideas). Yet j t ou will meet more
than one enthusiastic Single - taxer who has
assimilated “Progress and Poverty” as thoroughly
as a Plymouth Brother his Bible. What saith
(with emphatic inversion) the Scriptures : “ Unto
whomsoever much is given, of him shall be much
required.” And Meredith gives more than he
requires by 90 times 9 —and ever so many ciphers.
The book to begin on. of course, is “ The Ordeal
of Richard Feverel,” which most appeases the
hunger for plots—and chapters 15 and 20 may
be dipped into by way of foretaste. Anyone then
who gets no farther, deserves not to —which is a
cruel thing to say, but quite fair in the circum-
stances.
Omar is another matter (Khayyam —“the tent-
maker ” —is a tagged poetical pen-name, like—a
suitable modern parallel that won’t come to mind).
Money cannot really be measured against Fitz-
gerald’s Omar—practically the only one worth
knowing ; but, commercially speaking, there is no
“ cheap ” edition (the least price is 11s. posted;
and Eli Vender's famous illustrations run his
edition up to anything over £r> 55.), so that the
book is better not bought if anywhere, from any-
body,, you can borrow a copy and learn it by heart
—there are only 101 stanzas in the latest version.
Only 101 stanzas —begotten of Omar, conceived
and born of Fitzgerald, with the wit and wisdom
painfully gathered by a million philosophers of
East and West inherited and distilled in their
fragrance ! And the essence of all is
Cakpe Diem !
“ Go thy way, eat thy bread with joy, and drink
thy wine with a merry heart. Let thy garments
be always white; and let thy head lack no oint-
ment. Live joyfully with the wife whom thou
lovest all the days of this vanity—thy life.”,
A. Book of __ Vernes underneath the Bough,
A“Jiig of Wine, a Loaf of Bread —and Thou
Beside me singing ip th§ Wilderness—
Oh, Wilderness were Paradise "enow !
Every Omarist lip his favorite verse, in J]ita-
gerald’s exquisite metre. Herels'one (spoken by
a “ shape of clay ” in the “ Potter’s house ”) :
“ Well,” murmured one, “lei? whoso make or buy,
My Clay with long Oblivion is gone dry :
But fill me with the old familiar Juice,
Methinks I might recover by and by ! ”
Another i
And much as Wine has play’d the Infidel,
And robb’d me of my Robe of Honour —Well,
I wonder often what the Vjntners buy
One-half so precious as the stuff they sell.
Others: —
I must abjure the Balm of Life, I must,
Scared by some After-reckoning ta’en on trust,
Or lured with hope of some Diviner Drink,
To fill the Cup—when crumbled into Dust !
Oh threats of Hell and Hopes of Paradise !
One thing at least is certain — This Life flies ;
One thing is certain and is Lies;
The Flower that once has blown for ever dies.
Strange, is it not ? that of the myriads who
Before us pass’d the door of Darkness through,
Not one returns to tell us of the Road,
Which to discover we must travel too.
I sent my Soul through the Invisible,
Some letter of that After-life to spell:
And by and by my Soul return’d to me,
And answer’d “ I Myself am Heav’n and Hell: ”
Heav’n but the Vision of fulfill’d Desire,
And Hell the Shadow from a Soul on fire,
Cast on the Darkness into which Ourselves
So late emerged from, shall so soon expire.
Best of all :
Some for the Glories of This World ; and some
Sigh for the Prophet’s Paradise to come ;
Ab,’take the Cash, and let the Credit go,
Nor heed the rumble of a distant Drum !
PERIODICALS.
With the excellent Aug. number, Pall Mall
Magazine raises its price Gd. (now 2s. sd. posted).
It has Jong been obvious that this, the most sump-
tuous of English magazines, must be printed
at a loss, in spite of its fine array of advts. Pro-
bably the circulation in England, where its patrons
belong to a class which does not value incidental
sixpences, will be unaffected by the change ; but
the Australian sale is likely to decrease. So many
good books are obtainable at 2s. or 2s. Gd. that the
Pall Mall, still splendid value, goes down con-
siderably in the scale of comparative value ; and
the Australian buyer, though he never grudges
his money, always wants to make the best bargain
possible.
The Aug. English Illustrated (lid.) is a
specially good number of a specially good maga-
zine. A tale —“ With Madness in his Method ”
a charming example of the lighter American style
—and the continuation of Weyman’s “Memoirs
of a Minister of France,” plead for space-forbidden
comment; and one illustration, “Dawn,” is the
prettiest anywhere to be seen this month.
The July Englishwoman (lid.) is well-varied
and readable. The best story is Richard Pryce’s
“ Book of the Curl.” Frankfort Moore gives
another glimpse of that remarkable country,
English-Australia.
The Album for July 15 (Bd.) cannot be too
highly praised. The illustrations are exquisite,
particularly those which depict the beautiful
Thames from Oxford to Hampton Court. This
weekly is well worth a continuous subscription
(15s. Gd. per half-year).
The July Yellow Book (Gs. 6d.) doesn’t reach
previous standards ; it has less originality, less
literary value. There are one or two good pic-
tures ; the verse (with the exception of a trans-
lation from the Portuguese) is dim and eoho-y;
only two stories can be called good—“in an
American Newspaper Office,” and “ A New
Pos'ter.” Kenneth Grahame’s “ Long Odds ”is a
clever and suggestive paper.
The July Forum (375. per annum) has a par-
ticularly good article by Frederic Harrison on
“Charles Kingsley’s Place in Literature” —the
eighth of a splendid series which will be well
worth buying when republished. Harrison is a
better critic than Saintsbury ; and no less pleasant
a writer.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
“ Australian Zoology,” by G. Metcalfe, M.A., a
N.S.W. State school-teacher, is published by E.
W. Cole at Is. 3d. (posted), and is heartily recom-
mended as a most interesting book, worthy a
much better dress. Mainly, of course, it is com-
pilation ; but the work is exceedingly well done,
and the little volume should be bought by every-
one interested in Australian birds, beasts and
fishes.
Gunsberg’s just-published “Chess Openings”
(Is. 3d.) is a very good little manual, with the
great advantage of being up-to-date. (Other good
chess books are Green’s general handbook (Is, 3d.);
Staunton’s Hand-book, Praxis, and Chess-player’s
Companion (ss. 6d. each); and Morphy’s Games
(ss. Gd.). A more scientific book is Horwitz and
Kling’s “ Chess Studies and End Games ” (Bs. 3d.).
Steinitz’s “Instructor” (11s.), of which only
Part I. is yet published, is the completest so far
as it goes.)
“ Almayer’s Folly ” (paper, 35.; cloth, 45.), by
Joseph Conrad, is a powerful tale of life in Borneo
-which has been father over-praised by the English
press—always grateful to the writer who exploits
a new milieu. The central idea is the struggle
between civilised and barbaric instincts in a half-
white, half-yellow girl ; and her Dutch father’s
broken heart when barbarism triumphs and she
rushes to the arms of a Malay lover, is the last
drop in a cup of tragic life-failure.
Percy Fitzgerald supplies a gossipy, readable
“Life of Henry Irving” (paper, Is. 3d. ; cloth,
Is. lOd.)
“ The Cult of Beauty, a manual of personal
hygiene ” (35.), by C. S. Thompson, is an elegantly -
gotten-up little book, for which any lover would
deserve the thanks of his lady.

NOTES.
Alex. Lindsay is issuing an “ Australander
series ”,(ls. 3d.), to include new books by favorite
Australian authors. The first number, “His
Native Wife,” by Louis Becke, will bo out in three
or four weeks.
Booksellers find Morris’s “Life of Higin-
botham ” very dead stock. The life of a democrat
should certainly be published at a more demo-
cratic price than 9s. Bd. (posted).
The late Sir James Stephen put a familiar life-
philosophy in some lines quoted by his biographer-
brother Leslie:
The know-nothing books may alarm you ; but
many a better man
Knows he knows nothing and says so, and lives
the best life he can.
If there is a future state, face its hopes and terrors
gravely;
The best path to it must be to bear life’s burthens
bravely.
And even if there be none, why should you not
live like a man,
Enjoying whatever you have as much and as long
as you can ? ”
Melb. and Sydney retailers complain bitterly of
the ladies —generally related to most influential
personages —who get parcels of new books sent
home “on appro.,” and return them after any
time from two months to two years ; when, of
course, they are quite unsaleable. “My relation,
Sir Blank Blank,” covers a multitude of book-
lenders’ curses.
New volumes in the pretty pocket edition of
Chas. Kingsley (Is. lOd. each) are the “ Poems ”
and “Alton Locke.”
Miss Wilkins, a writer of charming American
village tales, has won the Chapman’s Magazine
first prize of =£loo for a short detective tale, “ The
Long Arm. ” Brander Matthews, another Ameri-
can, came second among 3000 competitors.
